The Composition of Body and the Verbal Dispute
Sanusi defines the body as composite and addresses a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Kalam doctrine that the body is composed of hyle and form, and the conditions under which the dispute about the body's definition becomes merely verbal.

Core Idea
The body's essence is the extended substance; matter is the hidden component requiring proof.

Sanusi begins by affirming the composite nature of the body from hyle and form. He clarifies that what we first perceive is the extended substance (the 'connection'), which is the body in initial perception. The matter (hyle) is what needs proof. If one accepts the existence of both matter and form, then the dispute over what the body is becomes purely verbal. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing on details. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the core claims
Find the three main claims: (1) the body is composed of hyle and form, (2) the extended substance is the first thing perceived, (3) matter needs proof. Also see the condition for verbal dispute.
The passage uses technical terms like hayula, sura, ittisal.
3
Understand the verbal dispute
Notice the condition: if one accepts the existence of matter and that one is receptive and the other received, then the dispute about what the body is becomes verbal. Why? Because both sides would agree on the components.
The author suggests this resolution is valid only if the opponent admits two substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة هذا الاتصال) المقبول ) ولا شك أن الصورة الاتصالية أي الجوهر الممتد في مع الجهات الذي تبين بنفي الجزء اتصاله في نفسه ( أول ما يدرك من جوهرية الجسم ) أي حقيقته بل هو الجسم في بادئ الرأي المعلوم وجوده بالضرورة ) والذي يحتاج إلى الإثبات ( بالدليل ( هو المادة ( المتصفة بذلك الجوهر المتصل، فإذا سلم ثبوتها وأن أحدهما قابل والآخر مقبول (فيصير النزاع) في أن الجسم ماذا نزاعاً لفظياً) لا فائدة فيه. وأنت تعلم أن هذا إنما يصح إذا سلم ذلك القائل أن هناك جوهرين في أن الجسم ذلك القابل فقط أو هو مع الاتصال بهذا المعنى فإنه لا يقول أحد أن الوحدة جزء من الجسم فالوجه ترك هذه المقدمة والاكتفاء بما بعده. قوله ولا شك أن الصورة إلخ) يعني المراد بالاتصال هو الجوهر الممتد ولا شك ثبوته بعد نفي الجزء، وكونه من حقيقة الجسم فإنه الجسم في بادئ الرأي والذي يحتاج إلى الإثبات هو المادة حتى يثبت كونه جزءاً من الجسم فإذا ثبت بقوله : التفريق أمر آخر يكون القابل بالحقيقة حتى لا يكون التفريق إعداماً بالكلية ثبت كون الاتصال بمعنى الجوهر الممتد جزءاً وعدم كونه تمام الحقيقة. قوله : فيصير النزاع إلخ الأولى تركه لأن النزاع في وجود ذلك القابل أو عدمه في الجسم بعد الاتفاق على أن الجسم هو الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة لا أن الجسم ماذا هو . قوله : ( إنما يصح إلخ ) هذا يرد على تقرير الشارح حيث قال : فإذا سلم ثبوتها وأن هناك جوهرين إلخ . وأما على ما قررناه فلا حدوث قلنا : فإذا أثبتنا بقوله : التعريف أمر آخر في الجسم حتى لا يكون التفريق إلخ . كما لا يخفى .
The First Observatory - Seventh Section: The body is composed of hyle and form. This connection (the receptive) and there is no doubt that the form of connection (al-sura al-ittisaliya), i.e. the extended substance (al-jawhar al-mumtadd) in the directions which is proven by negating the part, its connection in itself (is the first thing perceived of the substantiality of the body) i.e. its essence, rather it is the body in the first view, known necessarily to exist; and what needs to be proven by proof is the matter (al-madda) qualified by that extended substance. So if its existence is accepted and that one is receptive and the other is received, then the dispute becomes verbal, with no benefit. And you know that this is only valid if that speaker accepts that there are two substances, regarding whether the body is just the receptive or it is together with connection in this sense, because no one says that unity is a part of the body, so it is better to abandon this premise and suffice with what follows. His saying: 'and there is no doubt that the form etc.' means: the intended meaning of connection is the extended substance, and there is no doubt of its existence after negating the part, and that it is from the reality of the body, for it is the body in the first view; what needs proof is the matter until it is proven that it is a part of the body. So if it is proven by his saying: 'division is another matter, so the receptive is in reality so that division is not complete annihilation,' it is proven that connection in the sense of the extended substance is a part and not the complete reality. His saying: 'then the dispute becomes etc.' it is better to leave it, because the dispute is about the existence or non-existence of that receptive in the body after agreement that the body is the substance receptive to three dimensions, not what the body is. His saying: 'it is only valid etc.' this refutes the commentator's statement where he said: 'if its existence is accepted and that there are two substances etc.' As for what we have established, there is no occurrence; we say: if we prove by his saying: 'definition is another matter in the body so that division is not etc.' as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a definition and initial argument. It begins by stating the doctrine that the body is composed of hyle and form. It then clarifies that the 'connection' (al-ittisal) refers to the extended substance (al-jawhar al-mumtadd), which is the first thing perceived of the body's substantiality; it is known necessarily and is considered the body in initial perception. What needs proof is the matter (al-madda) that qualifies this extended substance. If one accepts the existence of both the receptive (the matter) and the received (the extended substance), then the dispute over what the body is becomes merely verbal—whether it is just the receptive or the receptive together with connection. However, this verbal resolution only holds if the opponent admits the existence of two substances (the extended substance and the matter). The author notes that no one claims that unity is a part of the body, so it is better to drop that premise and rely on what follows. The subsequent commentary elaborates: 'there is no doubt' confirms the extended substance as the intended meaning and its necessity. The proof needed is for the matter as a part. Once that is established, connection as a part is also established. The commentator then critiques the earlier formulation, suggesting that the real dispute is about the existence of the receptive in the body after agreeing that the body is the substance receptive to three dimensions, not about what the body is. The passage ends by showing how the original claim refutes a particular presentation.

الجوهر الممتد (jawhar mumtadd)
المادة (madda)
النزاع اللفظي (verbal dispute)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dispute about the body's definition is merely verbal from the start.
Correction: The author clarifies it only becomes verbal if one accepts the existence of matter (the receptive substance).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first thing perceived of the body's substantiality?
A) The matter (al-madda)
B) The extended substance (al-jawhar al-mumtadd)
C) The composite of hyle and form
D) The dimensions

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the body's definition is only verbal if one accepts the existence of both matter and form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi says the extended substance is the first thing you perceive of your body's essence. But have you ever truly considered what you perceive when you look at your own hand or face? Is it merely the extension and shape, or do you sense something deeper—the underlying matter that requires proof? How does this perspective shift your awareness of the physical world? Commit to noticing one aspect of your body today that you normally take for granted and reflect on its composition.
?
The passage suggests that once both matter and form are accepted, the dispute becomes verbal and futile. Is there a similar dispute in your own life—a theological or philosophical question you've debated that, upon accepting certain premises, becomes a matter of semantics? What would it mean for you to stop arguing and instead focus on the reality that is known necessarily? Identify one such debate you can let go of this week.
?
Sanusi emphasizes that matter needs proof. Yet most people live their lives assuming the physical world is straightforward. How often do you pause to question the reality of what you see, touch, and feel? Are you content with surface-level perception, or are you willing to seek proof for what underlies your existence? Pray for guidance to see beyond the apparent and to seek truth with humility.

The Objection Against Continuity as a Part of Body
Al-Jurjani presents the objection that if continuity is a part of body, the body cannot receive discontinuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that if continuity is a part of the body, the body cannot receive discontinuity, and the contradiction that arises.

Core Idea
A part's removal destroys the whole, so the body cannot receive discontinuity.

This lesson examines a specific objection within the kalam debates on the composition of bodies. The philosopher Al-Jurjani presents a question that targets the claim that continuity is an essential part of the body. He shows that this claim leads to a contradiction: the body must both cease to exist and remain when discontinuity occurs. By following the reasoning closely, you'll see how kalam masters used logical arguments to critique opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two premises: continuity as a part, and body's receptivity to discontinuity.
Notice that the objection is presented as a difficult question.
2
Understand the core contradiction
The objection shows that if continuity is a part, its removal destroys the body's identity, so the body cannot be receptive to it. But if it is receptive, it must remain. This forces a contradiction.
Ask: How does the objector derive the contradiction?
3
Appreciate the argumentative method
Notice that the objector assumes the opponent's premises and shows they lead to a logical impossibility (ijtima' al-mutanafiyayn). This is a standard kalam refutation technique.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this objection means for the theory that body is a continuous substance. Does it refute it? The author may have an answer later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة
جوهراً وراء هذا الجوهر المتصل، لكن المشهور أنه يقول : إن هذا الجوهر المتصل قائم بنفسه وهو حقيقة الجسم ومحل للاتصال الذي هو الوحدة والانفصال الذي هو الكثرة على معنى أنهما عرضان يحلان فيه على التعاقب كما ذهب إليه أفلاطون من أن آخر ما تنحل إليه الأجسام هو هذا الجوهر المتصل الممتد في الجهات كلها فطريق الرد عليه أنه يلزم من ذلك أن يكون التفريق إعداماً للجسم بالكلية وإيجاداً لقسمين آخرين من كتم العدم وهو باطل كما سيأتي تحقيقه ) وهاهنا سؤال يستصعبه بعض و ) ذلك السؤال ( هو أن الاتصال إذا كان جزءا للجسم كما زعمتم ( فبزواله ) الذي الانفصال ( تعدم هوية الجسم لانتفاء الكل بانتفاء جزئه ( فلا يكون الجسم قابلاً له ) أي لزواله أعني الانفصال ( وإذا كان الجسم ( قابلاً لزواله كما ادعيتموه أيضاً، فلا بد أن ( يبقى مع زواله) وإذا بقي معه فليس هو أي الاتصال ( جزءاً للجسم) والحاصل أن كون الجسم قابلاً للانفصال الذي هو زوال الاتصال ينافي كون الاتصال جزءاً له، فقد لزمكم فيما ذهبتم إليه القول باجتماع المتنافيين .
First Observatory - Seventh Purpose: The body is composed of matter and form. A substance behind this continuous substance, but the well-known (view) is that he says: This continuous substance is self-subsistent and is the reality of the body and the locus for continuity which is unity and discontinuity which is multiplicity, meaning that they are two accidents that inhere in it successively, as Plato held that the ultimate resolution of bodies is this continuous substance extended in all directions. The way to refute him is that it follows from that that division would be complete annihilation of the body and bringing into existence two other parts from the concealment of non-existence, and it is false as its verification will come. And here there is a question that some find difficult: That question is: If continuity is a part of the body as you claim, then by its removal—which is discontinuity—the identity of the body is destroyed because the whole ceases with the cessation of its part; so the body is not receptive to it, i.e., to its removal, meaning discontinuity. And if the body is receptive to its removal as you also claim, then it must remain with its removal; and if it remains with it, then continuity is not a part of the body. The upshot is: the body's being receptive to discontinuity, which is the removal of continuity, contradicts continuity's being a part of it. So you have been forced, in what you have gone to, to affirm the conjunction of two opposites.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of a specific objection within a broader discussion on whether the body is composed of matter and form. It first states the view that the continuous substance (al-jawhar al-muttasil) is self-subsistent, is the reality of the body, and is the locus for the accidents of continuity (unity) and discontinuity (multiplicity). It then presents a question that some find difficult: if continuity is a part of the body, then when continuity is removed (i.e., when the body is divided), the body's identity is destroyed because the whole ceases when its part ceases. This would imply that the body cannot be receptive to discontinuity. But if the body is receptive to discontinuity, it must remain when discontinuity occurs. From these two premises—continuity is a part, and the body is receptive to its removal—a contradiction follows: the body cannot both cease and remain upon the removal of continuity. Therefore, the claim that continuity is a part leads to the conjunction of two opposites (ijtima' al-mutanafiyayn).

Key Terms
الجوهر المتصل
الاتصال
الانفصال
هوية الجسم
اجتماع المتنافيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that the body has parts or is composite.
Correction: The objection does not deny composition; it only targets the specific claim that continuity is a part, showing it leads to contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what follows if continuity is a part of the body?
A) The body can be divided without losing its identity.
B) The body cannot be receptive to discontinuity because its identity would be destroyed.
C) The body is composed of atoms.
D) Continuity is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses a reductio argument to show that the claim 'continuity is a part of body' is incompatible with the body's receptivity to division, forcing a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held two beliefs that logically contradict each other but ignored the inconsistency? How does this objection challenge you to examine your own assumptions and seek consistency in your faith and reasoning?
?
The objector uses the opponent's own premises to expose a contradiction. In your interactions with others, do you strive to understand their position fully before critiquing? How can you apply this method of fair engagement in defending and discussing your beliefs?
?
This passage shows that affirming a contradiction is impossible. In your own life, are there areas where you are living out contradictory values—like claiming trust in Allah while worrying excessively? How can this lesson inspire you to realign your actions with your convictions?

Resolving the Objection: Two Meanings of 'Continuity'
Al-Jurjani resolves the objection by distinguishing two meanings of 'ittisal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the objection by distinguishing between two meanings of 'continuity' (al-ittisal): one essential and one accidental.

Core Idea
Continuity has two meanings: one never leaves the body, the other can change.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses an objection that seemed powerful by showing it rests on a simple equivocation. The term 'continuity' (al-ittisal) is used in two distinct senses: the substantial form that gives a body its capacity for extension, and the accidental quantity of the extensions themselves. By clarifying these meanings, the objection dissolves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that al-Jurjani begins by identifying the objection as a sophism due to equivocation.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find where al-Jurjani distinguishes two meanings of 'continuity'. List them in your own words.
The first meaning is substantial and inseparable; the second is accidental and changeable.
3
Understand the example
Study the example of the body changing shapes while its corporeal form remains. How does this illustrate the distinction?
The example shows that accidental changes in extension do not affect the essential continuity.
4
Consider why this matters
Reflect on why this distinction refutes the objection. Why would confounding the two meanings lead to a false conclusion?
The objection assumed that any discontinuity would destroy the body, but that only applies to the essential meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظن المستصعب أن ذلك السؤال مغالطة وقعت من الاشتراك اللفظي، فإن الاتصال يقال للصورة الجوهرية التي بها للجسم قبول الامتدادات الثالث وهو أمر لا يزول عن الجسم بحال من الأحوال إذ لا يتصور بقاء جسم مع زوال هذه الصورة عنه. ويقال أيضاً لنفس الامتدادات وهو كم وليس جزءاً للجسم لأنه عرض فلا يكون مقوماً للجوهر بل عارضاً له فلا يلزم من زواله زوال الجسم كما إذا شكل الجسم بأشكال مختلفة المقادير مع بقاء صورته الجسمية بعينها
And the one who found it difficult thought that that question is a sophism arising from equivocation (al-ishtirāk al-lafẓī). For 'continuity' (al-ittiṣāl) is said of the substantial form (al-ṣūra al-jawhariyya) by which the body has the reception of the three extensions, and it is something that does not leave the body in any state, since the remaining of a body without this form is inconceivable. And it is also said of the extensions themselves, which is quantity (kamm), and it is not a part of the body because it is an accident; so it does not constitute the substance, but rather is an accident of it, so its removal does not necessitate the removal of the body, just as when the body is shaped into different shapes of various magnitudes while its corporeal form (ṣūratuhu al-jismiyya) remains the same.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the previous objection. It identifies the objection as a sophism (mughālata) arising from equivocation (ishtirāk lafẓī). The term 'continuity' (al-ittiṣāl) has two distinct meanings: (1) the substantial form (al-ṣūra al-jawhariyya) that gives the body the capacity to receive the three dimensions (extension). This meaning is inseparable from the body—a body cannot exist without this form. (2) The extensions themselves (al-imitidādāt), which are accidental quantity (kamm). This quantity is not a part (juz') of the body; it is an accident that inheres in the body and can change without affecting the body's substance. Therefore, the objection fails because it conflates these two meanings. When continuity as the substantial form is meant, the body cannot lose it; when continuity as quantity is meant, its removal (discontinuity) does not destroy the body—just as a body (like wax) can change its shape and size while retaining its substantial form.

Key Terms
الاتصال
الاشتراك اللفظي
الصورة الجوهرية
الامتدادات
الكم
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies any real continuity in the body; it claims continuity is only accidental.
Correction: It actually affirms an unchanging essential continuity (substantial form) while allowing accidental continuity (quantity) to vary.

Checkpoint
What are the two meanings of 'continuity' (al-ittiṣāl) distinguished in the answer?
A) The form and the matter.
B) The substantial form that gives capacity for extension, and the accidental quantity (extensions themselves).
C) The body and the soul.
D) Unity and multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani resolves the objection by showing that 'continuity' has two distinct meanings: one essential and inseparable, the other accidental and changeable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a wrong belief simply because you assumed a word had only one meaning? What specific term in your understanding of Allah's attributes might you be equivocating on? Write down the two distinct meanings you need to separate.
?
When you encounter a difficulty in aqeedah, do you quickly label it as a sophism, or do you humbly investigate the meanings? Which approach does this passage teach you to adopt starting today?
?
This passage shows that clarifying ambiguity can resolve a seemingly devastating objection. Is there any doubt about your faith that might dissolve if you distinguished two meanings of a key term? What is that doubt, and what is the first step you will take to resolve it?

The Argument from Rarefaction and Condensation for Hyle and Its Refutation
Argument from takhallul/takathuf for hyle and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument from rarefaction and condensation for hyle and the refutation based on the bodily form's capacity to receive different magnitudes.

Core Idea
The bodily form itself can receive different magnitudes — no separate hyle is needed.

Philosophers argued that expansion and contraction of bodies requires an underlying substrate not inherently sized, which they called hyle. The Mutakallimun countered that the bodily form (jismiyya) entails dimension in general but not a specific dimension, so it alone can account for changes in size. This passage presents that argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for hyle
Read the first part of the passage which presents the proponents' reasoning: real rarefaction and condensation require an entity not intrinsically determined to a magnitude.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument's core premise: if there were no such entity, then size changes would require something joining or separating, which is not observed.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation points out that the bodily form entails dimension in existence and conception, but not a particular dimension, so it can be the receiver of different magnitudes. Thus hyle is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استعانوا في إثبات الهيولى بالتخلخل والتكاثف الحقيقيين، فإنه إذا لم يكن في الجسم أمر غير متقدر بذاته حتى يتصور قبوله للمقادير المختلفة امتنع ازدياد حجمه وانتقاصه من غير انضمار شيء إليه وانفصاله عنه وجوابه أن الصورة الجسمية، وإن كانت مستلزمة في الوجود والتعقل للمقدار إلا أنها لا تستلزم مقداراً مخصوصاً فجاز أن تكون هي قابلة لتلك المقادير المختلفة فلا يثبت وجود أمر آخر
They sought help in proving hyle by means of rarefaction and condensation, both real. For if there were not in the body an entity not intrinsically determined to a particular magnitude, such that it may receive different magnitudes, then an increase in its size and a decrease would be impossible without something joining to it or separating from it. The answer is that the bodily form, although it entails dimension in existence and conception, does not entail a particular dimension, so it is possible that it itself is the receiver of those different magnitudes, and thus the existence of another entity is not established.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents an argument used by proponents of hyle (primordial matter) to prove its existence, and then provides the refutation from the Mutakallimun. The argument from rarefaction and condensation claims that a body undergoing expansion or contraction must contain an underlying substrate that is not intrinsically sized, so that it can receive different sizes. The refutation points out that the bodily form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya) necessarily entails dimension in existence and conception, but it does not entail a specific dimension. Therefore, the bodily form itself can be the receiver of different magnitudes, eliminating the need for a separate entity called hyle.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة الجسمية
التخلخل والتكاثف
المقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in magnitude requires a distinct substrate independent of form.
Correction: The bodily form itself can receive different magnitudes because it entails dimension without specifying a particular magnitude.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the refutation draw from the bodily form's relation to magnitude?
A) The bodily form requires hyle to change magnitude.
B) The bodily form does not entail a specific magnitude and thus can receive different magnitudes.
C) The bodily form is identical to hyle.
D) The bodily form is impossible without hyle.

Key Takeaway
The bodily form's indifference to specific magnitude makes it sufficient to account for size changes, undercutting the need for hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your body changes—whether through growth, illness, or age—do you instinctively look for an unchanging 'thing' inside you to hold onto? This lesson says the bodily form itself is enough. Does your trust in Allah's creation extend to accepting that change does not threaten your identity? What would it take to let go of the idea that you need a fixed core?
?
If the bodily form can receive different magnitudes without needing hyle, then Allah has made creation self-sufficient in that respect. Is there an area of your life where you are adding unnecessary 'hyle'—extra beliefs or practices—because you think the original is insufficient? How can you simplify your reliance on what Allah has given?
?
The refutation hinges on the fact that form does not entail a specific magnitude. Are you attached to a specific 'magnitude' in your life—a role, status, or possession—as if it defines you? This lesson shows that the essence (form) can remain while the size changes. Can you find security in your essence as a servant of Allah, even if your circumstances expand or contract?

Argument from Generation and Corruption for Hyle and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the argument from generation and corruption for hyle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from generation and corruption for hyle and its refutation, including why the argument is considered circular.

Core Idea
Generation and corruption do not prove hyle; the bodily form suffices and the argument is circular.

The argument from generation and corruption (al-kawn wa al-fasād) is one of the classic philosophical proofs for the existence of hyle, the primordial substratum. However, the refutation demonstrates that the argument is flawed on two grounds: the bodily form itself can account for the change, and the argument is circular. This lesson examines both the argument and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's line of reasoning.
Notice the structure: first the argument is presented, then the refutation is given in two parts.
2
Identify the two prongs of the refutation
The refutation has two independent arguments: (1) the bodily form can serve as the substratum for changing specific forms, and (2) the argument is circular because it presupposes what it aims to prove.
Reflect on how each prong undermines the original argument.
3
Understand the circularity
The argument uses the very phenomenon (generation and corruption) that itself depends on hyle, creating a vicious circle. Ask yourself: how does this make the argument invalid?
Circular arguments prove nothing; they assume the conclusion in the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكون والفساد . أي وربما استعانوا بهما أيضاً إذ لا بد فيهما من أمر يخلع صورة ويلبس أخرى وهو الهيولى وفساده ظاهر لأن المتبدل في الكون والفساد هو الصور النوعية فجاز أن يكون القابل لها خلعاً ولبساً هو الصورة الجسمية على أنا نقول : وجود هذه الأمور التي استعين بها مبني على وجود الهيولى فيلزم الدور.
And generation and corruption. That is, they perhaps also sought help from both, for in them there must be an entity that removes one form and puts on another, which is hyle. But its corruption is apparent, because that which changes in generation and corruption is the specific forms, so it is possible that the receiver for them in removal and putting on is the bodily form. Except that we say: the existence of these things which are used as aids is built upon the existence of hyle, so circularity follows.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents another argument for hyle based on generation and corruption (substantial change). The argument states that when one thing becomes another, there must be a persistent substrate (hyle) that loses one form and acquires another. The refutation points out that what changes are the specific forms (al-ṣuwar al-nawʿiyya), and the bodily form could be the substratum that receives the forms. Moreover, the argument is circular because the very phenomena used to prove hyle (generation and corruption) themselves presuppose hyle, leading to a vicious circle. Thus, the argument is invalid.

Key Terms
الكون والفساد
الصور النوعية
الهيولى
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substantial change necessarily requires a substratum distinct from the bodily form (i.e., hyle).
Correction: The bodily form can serve as the substratum for the change of specific forms, so hyle is not proven by generation and corruption.

Checkpoint
What is the circularity mentioned in the refutation?
A) The argument uses hyle to prove hyle.
B) The existence of generation and corruption depends on hyle, which is what is being proved.
C) The bodily form depends on hyle.
D) The specific forms depend on hyle.

Key Takeaway
The argument from generation and corruption for hyle is refuted because the bodily form can serve as the substratum and the argument is circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever argue for something based on an assumption that itself relies on what you are trying to prove? Can you identify a belief you hold that might be circular?
?
The refutation suggests that the bodily form is sufficient to explain change. How content are you with simple explanations for complex phenomena, or do you feel the need for an underlying 'something'?
?
When you read this passage, did you instinctively side with the proponents of hyle because their argument felt intuitive? What does this reveal about your reliance on intuitive assumptions in matters of faith and reason?

The Mutakallimun's Main Argument Against Hyle: The Three Branches on Spatiality
Mutakallimun's argument against hyle via three spatial branches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mutakallimun's decisive argument that negates the existence of hyle by analyzing its possible relations to space and showing that each leads to absurdity or contradicts its definition.

Core Idea
Hyle cannot be a substratum because any relation to space makes it either a body, an attribute, or a non-entity.

The Mutakallimun (rational theologians) attacked the Aristotelian concept of hyle (prime matter) by focusing on its relation to space. They argued that if hyle exists, it must be either in space or not. Both options lead to contradictions that prove hyle cannot serve as the substratum of forms. This lesson walks through the three branches of that argument and examines a possible response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dichotomous setup
The argument begins by dividing the supposition of hyle's existence into two possibilities: having spatiality or not. This sets the stage for the three branches.
2
Examine the first branch: independent spatiality
If hyle occupies space independently, it would be a body, causing problems: the bodily form would be like it, leading to infinite regress or dependence issues.
This branch is further supported by multiple sub-arguments.
3
Examine the second branch: dependent spatiality
If hyle occupies space dependently via the bodily form, it becomes an attribute of the form, not a substratum.
4
Examine the third branch: no spatiality
If hyle has no spatiality, it is a pure intelligible incapable of being a locus for a spatial form.
5
Consider the response
A possible answer reinterprets dependent spatiality as the bodily form inhering in hyle, but this does not ultimately save hyle.
The response is part of the dialectic, but the Mutakallimun maintain their negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد عند المتكلمين في نفي الهيولى أنها على تقدير وجودها إما أن يكون لها حصول الحيز أو لا يكون. فإن كان لها حصول فيه فإما أن يكون ذلك الحصول على سبيل الاستقلال فجسم أي فالهيولى جسم لأن المتحيز بالذات لا بد أن يكون جوهراً ممتداً في الجهات ولا معنى للجسم إلا ذلك. وأيضاً فالصورة الجسمية حينئذ مثل لها، فكيف تحل فيها؟ وأيضاً إن احتاجت الهيولى إلى محل لزم التسلسل، وإلا كانت الجسمية مستغنية عن المحل لأنها مثلها. أو لا يكون ذلك الحصول على سبيل الاستقلال بل على سبيل التبعية للصورة الجسمية فالهيولى حينئذ صفة حالة في الجسمية تابعة لها في التحيز لا جوهر، هو محل لها كما هو مطلوبكم. وإلا أي وإن لم يكن لها حصول في الحيز لا استقلالاً ولا تبعاً فلا تختص الجسمية بها اختصاصاً ناعتاً لها لأنه أي لأن ما لا تحيز له أصلاً أمر معقول محض لا تعلق ولا اختصاص له بحيز قطعاً فكيف يتصور حلول الجسمية المتحيزة بالذات فيه؟ وقد يجاب بأنا لا نسلم أنها لو كانت متحيزة بالتبعية لكانت صفة للجسمية فإن تحيز الشيء بالتبعية قد يكون باعتبار حلوله في الغير، كما في الأعراض الحالة في الأجسام، وقد يكون باعتبار حلول الغير فيه، فليس يلزم من تحيز الهيولى لا بالاستقلال أن يكون تحيزها على سبيل حلولها في الجسمية بل يجوز أن يكون تحيزها بشرط حلول الجسمية فيها فتكون موصوفة بها لا صفة لها.
And the relied-upon [argument] of the Mutakallimun in negating hyle is that, on the supposition of its existence, either it has possession of space or not. If it has possession in it, then either that possession is independent, so it is a body — that is, hyle is a body, because that which is intrinsically spatial must be a substance extended in directions, and the meaning of body is nothing but that. Also, the bodily form would then be its like; how then could it inhere in it? Also, if hyle needs a locus, then there would be an infinite regress; otherwise, the bodily form would be independent of a locus because it is like it. Or that possession is not independent but instead dependent on the bodily form; then hyle is at that time an attribute inhering in the bodily form, following it in spatiality, not a substance that is a locus for it as you claim. Otherwise, that is, if it has no possession in space, neither independently nor dependently, then the bodily form does not have a characterizing connection with it, because that which has no spatiality at all is a pure intelligible, having absolutely no relation or connection to space; so how can the inherence of the bodily form, which is intrinsically spatial, be conceived in it? And it may be answered: we do not accept that if it were spatially dependent then it would be an attribute of the bodily form, for the spatiality of a thing dependently may be by virtue of its inherence in another, as in accidents inhering in bodies, or by virtue of the inherence of another in it; so it does not follow from hyle's not having independent spatiality that its spatiality is by way of its inherence in the bodily form; rather, it is possible that its spatiality is conditioned by the inherence of the bodily form in it, so that it is described by it, not an attribute of it.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the decisive argument of the Mutakallimun against hyle, structured as a dichotomy regarding hyle's relation to space (al-ḥayyiz). The argument proceeds in three branches: (1) If hyle has independent spatial occupation, then it is a body — which contradicts its role as a substratum, since the bodily form would then be similar to it and could not inhere in it, and an infinite regress would ensue. (2) If hyle has dependent spatial occupation (i.e., by virtue of the bodily form), then it is merely an attribute of the bodily form, not a separate substance. (3) If hyle has no spatial occupation at all, then it is a pure intelligible abstraction incapable of being a locus for the bodily form, which is intrinsically extended. The passage then presents a possible response: dependent spatiality need not mean the hyle inheres in the bodily form; it could mean the bodily form inheres in hyle, making hyle the substratum described by spatiality, not an attribute. However, this response is not the final word; the argument overall favors the negation of hyle.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الحيز
الاستقلال
التبعية
الصورة الجسمية
المتحيز بالذات
أمر معقول محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the response proves hyle can exist if it has dependent spatiality interpreted as the bodily form inhering in it.
Correction: The response is a potential objection, but the Mutakallimun would reject it because the bodily form would still need a substratum, and hyle as a pure intelligible cannot provide extension.

Checkpoint
According to the Mutakallimun, what follows if hyle has independent spatial occupation?
A) Hyle would be an accident.
B) Hyle would be a body, leading to problems like similarity with the bodily form and infinite regress.
C) Hyle would be a pure intelligible.
D) Hyle would be dependent on the bodily form.

Key Takeaway
The Mutakallimun's argument systematically shows that hyle cannot fulfill its role as a substratum regardless of its relation to space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After following this precise logical dismantling of hyle, do you see your own intellect as a gift that demands rigorous use in matters of faith? Can you point to a time when you used reason to defend a belief, and what did that cost you?
?
Consider that hyle, in the third branch, becomes a 'pure intelligible' with no connection to the physical. Is there any belief you hold about the unseen that, upon honest examination, becomes just an abstract concept with no real grounding? Are you willing to test it as the Mutakallimun tested hyle?
?
This argument forces a choice: either hyle is a body, an attribute, or nothing at all. In your spiritual life, what 'hyle'—some assumption or comfort—are you clinging to that, when examined, leads only to contradictions? Will you let it go for the sake of a sounder foundation?

The Argument from Conceptual Understanding and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the argument that body is not composed of hyle based on conceptual understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that since we conceive body without conceiving hyle, body is not composed of hyle, and the response that this only works if we have exhaustive knowledge of body's essence.

Core Idea
Conceiving something without its parts does not prove the parts are unreal.

This passage presents an objection against the existence of hyle based on how we conceptualize body. The objectors argue that if body were composed of hyle and form, then thinking of body would require thinking of both. But we think of body without thinking of hyle. The defenders respond by distinguishing between ordinary conception and full essential knowledge. Let's examine this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: if-then premise, false consequent, conclusion that premise is false.
The argument uses conditional reasoning.
2
Focus on the key terms
Pay attention to 'taʿaqqul' (conceptualization), 'ḥaqīqa' (reality), and 'bi-l-kunh' (in essence). These are crucial for the response.
The response denies that we grasp the body's reality fully.
3
Understand the response's nuance
The response does not deny that we conceptualize body; it denies that our conceptualization captures the entire essence. Thus hyle could be part of the reality without being part of our ordinary concept.
This is a common move in kalām: distinguishing between ordinary knowledge and essential knowledge.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument shows that kalām debates often hinge on the extent of human knowledge. The response protects the possibility of hyle by limiting the scope of our conceptual grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال في نفي الهيولى وإبطال تركب الجسم منها لو كان الجسم مركباً من جزأين كما ذكرتم لزم من تعقله تعقلهما ولم يحتج في ثبوت شيء منهما له إلى برهان واللازم باطل فإنا نعقل الجسم ولا نعقل الهيولى ونحتاج في إثباتها إلى البرهان والجواب منع تعقل حقيقته يعني أن ما ذكرتم إنما يلزم إذا كان حقيقة الجسم معقولة بالكنه وهو ممنوع.
And it may be said in negating hyle and invalidating the composition of body from it: if the body were composed of two parts, as you have mentioned, then from conceptualizing it would follow conceptualization of them, and no proof would be needed for establishing something of them for it. But the consequent is false, for we conceptualize the body and do not conceptualize hyle, and we need a proof for establishing it. The answer: the denial of conceptualizing its reality, meaning that what you have mentioned would follow only if the reality of the body were conceptualized in its essence, which is denied.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents a further argument against hyle based on conceptualization (taʿaqqul). The argument states that if the body were composed of two parts (hyle and form), then understanding the body would require understanding those parts. But we understand the body without understanding hyle, and we need proof to establish hyle. Therefore, the premise is false. The response (al-jawāb) is that this would follow only if the reality (ḥaqīqa) of the body were fully grasped in its essence (bi-l-kunh). Since we do not have exhaustive knowledge of the body's essence, it is possible that hyle is part of its reality without being included in our ordinary conception. Thus, the argument does not disprove hyle.

Key Terms
الهيولى
تعقل
حقيقة
برهان
كنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we can think of body without thinking of hyle, then hyle cannot be a real part of body.
Correction: The response shows that this reasoning assumes our concept fully captures the essence, which it does not. Hyle may be part of the reality without being included in our ordinary thought.

Checkpoint
How do the defenders of hyle respond to the argument from conceptualization?
A) They deny that we conceptualize the body.
B) They argue that we do conceptualize hyle implicitly.
C) They say the argument only works if we fully grasp the body's reality in its essence, which we do not.
D) They admit the argument is valid.

Key Takeaway
The argument from conceptualization fails because our ordinary conception of body does not exhaust its essential reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself rejecting a belief because you cannot conceive of it, pause. Is your inability to conceive proof of falsehood? Name one Islamic belief you struggle to conceptualize. Will you commit to seeking proof rather than dismissing it based on your mental limits?
?
The argument assumes our understanding is exhaustive. Where in your life have you mistaken your limited perception for reality? How can you cultivate humility about your own conceptual framework?
?
If you cannot grasp the essence (kunh) of even a physical body, how much less can you grasp the nature of Allah? Does this challenge your faith or deepen it? What step will you take to trust in proof over personal conception?

The Response: Denying the Unity of Body-Form
The author responds to Ibn Sina by denying the unity of bodily nature and clarifying the role of divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part response to Ibn Sina's argument: the denial that the bodily nature is a single specific nature, and the clarification that divisibility is only a means of knowing the need for matter, not the cause.

Core Idea
The bodily nature is ambiguous like quantity, not a fixed single essence.

In this passage, the author counters Ibn Sina's argument that all bodies need matter because of their divisible nature. He launches a two-pronged response: first, he denies that the bodily nature is a single specific nature; second, he clarifies that divisibility is merely a means of knowing the need for matter, not the cause. This subtle distinction preserves the possibility that matter is required by the essence of body itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main moves
Read the response and identify the two distinct moves: the denial of unity and the conditional analysis of divisibility. Notice how they interconnect.
The passage shifts from a decisive denial to a hypothetical concession.
2
Understand the analogy with quantity
Focus on the claim that the bodily nature is 'ambiguous like quantity' and cannot exist without constitutive differentiate. Reflect on why this undercuts Ibn Sina's premise.
This is a critical insight: bodily nature is not a ready-made essence.
3
Grasp the logical point about divisibility
The author argues that even if bodily connection were a single nature, divisibility is only a middle term in our affirmation of the need for matter, not a real cause. Otherwise, matter would be posterior to division, which is impossible.
This subtle point preserves the essential need for matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب منع اتحاد الاتصال الجسمي ) ، أي لا نسلم أن الطبيعة الجسمية طبيعة واحدة نوعية ) وذلك مما لا سبيل إلى إثباته ) فإن ما ذكرتموه من اختلافها بالأمور الخارجية عنها مسلم لكن انحصار اختلافها فيه ممنوع فإن الطبيعة الجسمية مطلقاً أمر مبهم كالمقدار، فلا يتصور وجودها إلا بأن يتنوع بفصول مقومة لها، أو بعد تنوعها ينضم إليها أمور خارجة عنها فلم قلتم : إنها ليست كذلك ؟ ( وإن سلم ( أن الاتصال الجسمي حقيقة واحدة نوعية ( فقد ) يجوز و يكون الاحتياج إلى المادة مقتضى ذاته . قلت : قبول الانفصال واسطة في التصديق بالاحتياج وليس بواسطة في الثبوت، وإلا لكان ثبوت الهيولى للأجسام متأخراً عن قبول الانفصال فتدبر فإنه دقيق .
The answer: denial of the unity of bodily connection, i.e., we do not accept that the bodily nature is a single specific nature. And that is something that cannot be proven. For what you mentioned about its differences being due to external things is granted, but the restriction of its differences to that is denied. For the bodily nature absolutely is an ambiguous thing like quantity; its existence cannot be conceived except by being diversified by constitutive differentiate, or after its diversification external things are joined to it. So why do you say it is not like that? And if it is granted that bodily connection is a single specific nature, then it may be that the need for matter is a requirement of its essence. I say: acceptance of division is a middle term in the affirmation of the need, not a middle term in the reality; otherwise the existence of matter for bodies would be posterior to the acceptance of division, so reflect, for it is subtle.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents the response to Ibn Sina's argument. The author denies that the bodily nature is a single specific nature, arguing that it is ambiguous like quantity and requires intrinsic differentiate to exist. He also argues that even if it were a specific nature, its need for matter could be essential rather than derived from its nature. Finally, he clarifies that the original argument's basis, division, is only a means of knowing the need for matter, not the cause of that need itself. Otherwise, matter would be posterior to division, which is impossible.

Key Terms
اتصال جسمي (bodily connection)
طبيعة نوعية (specific nature)
مبهم (ambiguous)
فصول مقومة (constitutive differentiate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The response completely denies that bodies need matter (hylomorphism).
Correction: The response does not deny the need for matter; it only denies Ibn Sina's specific proof from the unity of bodily nature. The author leaves open that matter may be required essentially.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what does the author compare the bodily nature to?
A) A single specific nature
B) Quantity
C) An external accident
D) Matter itself

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the proof for matter by denying the unity of bodily nature and showing that divisibility is only epistemic, not the real cause of the need for matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that the need for matter could be an essential requirement of the bodily nature itself, not derived from divisibility. Think about your own spiritual needs: Are they merely a response to external circumstances, or are they essential to your very nature as a created being? How does this shift your understanding of your constant dependence on Allah?
?
The author points out that divisibility is only a means to affirm the need, not the real cause. In your life, when you feel distant from Allah, do you often look for a 'divisibility'—a specific reason or event—rather than recognizing that the need for Him is inherent in your nature? What would change if you internalized that your need for Allah is not contingent on any particular circumstance?

Body-ness as a Specific Nature
Defense of body-ness as a uniform specific nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that body-ness qua body-ness is a single specific nature that uniformly requires matter.

Core Idea
Body-ness is a uniform nature that demands matter in all bodies.

The commentator now defends Ibn Sina's argument against an objection that denies body-ness is a specific nature. This passage establishes that body-ness, considered purely as substantial extension, is a single simple nature with no internal differences. From this, it follows that if any body needs matter, all bodies must need it — because the nature is the same. Let's see how this unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'قوله: أي لا نسلم' to 'فقد بان أن الأجسام مؤلفة من مادة وصورة'. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the definition of body-ness.
The commentator is refuting the denial that body-ness is a specific nature.
2
Identify the nature claim
Notice that body-ness is defined as 'substantial extension' and is called a 'single simple nature'. The commentator argues that differences (like hot/cold) are external to body-ness.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Since body-ness is uniform, if it requires matter in one body, it must require it in all. This shows that all bodies are composed of matter and form.
This refutes the objection that some bodies might not need matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أي لا نسلم أن الطبيعة إلخ ) هذا المنع مدفوع لأن المقصود أن الجسمية من حيث جسمية أي امتداد جوهري طبيعة نوعية لكونها موجودة في الخارج من غير اعتبار أمر آخر متحد معها . بل إنما يعتبر من حيث جسميته إلى المادة في العنصريات كانت كذلك في الكل في الشفاء، أما الصورة الجسمية من حيث هي جسمية فهي طبيعة واحدة بسيطة محصلة لا اختلاف فيها ولا يخالف مجرد صورة جسمية لمجرد صورة جسمية بفصل داخل في الجسمية. وما يلحقها إنما يلحقها على أنها شيء خارج عن طبيعتها، فلا يجوز إذا أن تكون جسمية محتاجة إلى مادة وجسمية غير محتاجة إلى مادة واللواحق الخارجية لا بعينها محتاجة إلى المادة بوجه من الوجوه لأن الحاجة إلى المادة إنما تكون للجمسية ولكل ذي مادة وصورة لأجل ذاته، وللجمسية من حيث هي جسمية لاحقة فقد بان أن الأجسام مؤلفة من مادة وصورة انتهى . ولا يخفى أنه كما يندفع بهذا البيان منع كونها طبيعة نوعية لا احتياج فيه إلى إثبات عدم الواسطة بين الاحيتاج في الغنى الذاتيين فإنه استدلال بأن مقتضى الطبيعة النوعية لا يتخلف عنه، فلا يختلف الاحتياج عنها في جسم من الأجسام سواء كان بينهما واسطة أو لا، فتدبر حق التدبر يظهر لك الحق الصريح . قوله : ( فإن الطبيعة الجسمية مضناً إلخ) هذا مكابرة فإنه بعد نفي وجود الجزء وما في حكمه ثبت وجود جوهر لا مفصل فيه والمبهم لا وجود له في الخارج، نعم لمفهوم المأخوذ منه
His saying: 'We do not accept that the nature, etc.' – this denial is refuted, because what is intended is that body-ness qua body-ness, i.e., substantial extension, is a specific nature because it exists externally without considering any other thing united with it. Rather, it is considered qua its body-ness in relation to matter in elemental bodies, and it was like that in all (bodies) in the Shifa. As for the bodily form qua bodily form, it is a single simple acquired nature with no difference in it, and a mere bodily form does not differ from a mere bodily form by a differentiate internal to body-ness. What attaches to it attaches to it as something external to its nature. Therefore, it is not permissible that a body-form should need matter and another body-form not need matter, and external accidents are not themselves in need of matter in any way, because the need for matter is for body-ness and for every possessor of matter and form for its own sake, and for body-ness qua body-ness it is a consequent. Thus it has become clear that bodies are composed of matter and form. End. And it is not hidden that as this explanation refutes the denial of its being a specific nature, there is no need to prove the absence of a middle term between need and self-sufficiency, for it is a proof that the requirement of a specific nature does not fail to apply, so need does not differ concerning it in any body, whether there is a middle term or not. So reflect deeply, and the plain truth will appear to you. His saying: 'For the bodily nature is ambiguous, etc.' – this is a stubborn denial, because after denying the existence of a part and what is in its judgment, the existence of a substance with no division in it is established, and the ambiguous has no existence externally. Yes, for the concept taken from it
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's refutation of the response's denial. He argues that body-ness as such is a specific nature because it exists in the external world without needing intrinsic differentiate to constitute it. The differences that attach to it (like hot/cold) are external. Therefore, if body-ness needs matter in some bodies, it must need it in all, because its nature is uniform. The commentator also criticizes the opponent's claim that the bodily nature is ambiguous, stating that after rejecting atomism (the part), the existence of a substance with no internal division is established, and the ambiguous has no external existence. The passage concludes with an exhortation to reflect deeply.

Key Terms
جسمية من حيث جسمية
امتداد جوهري
طبيعة واحدة بسيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is body-ness a specific nature?
A) Because it requires intrinsic differentiate to exist.
B) Because it exists externally without any other thing united with it.
C) Because it is ambiguous like quantity.
D) Because it is purely mental.

Key Takeaway
Body-ness as a specific nature is uniform, so its need for matter is universal in all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If body-ness demands matter uniformly, what uniform spiritual discipline does your soul demand regardless of your mood or circumstances? Have you been inconsistent because you treated it as optional?
?
The commentator urges deep reflection to see the plain truth. What truth about your relationship with Allah have you been avoiding because it would require a change in your daily habits? Will you set aside 10 minutes tonight to reflect on that truth?
?
External accidents do not change the essence of body-ness. What external distractions have you allowed to change the essence of your devotion (ikhlas)? How can you strip them away and return to the core?

Refuting the Ambiguity of Bodily Nature
Refutation of the claim that bodily nature is ambiguous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the claim that the bodily nature is ambiguous, by establishing the existence of an indivisible substance.

Core Idea
The bodily nature is not ambiguous; an indivisible substance exists externally.

The opponent had claimed that the bodily nature is ambiguous (mubham), meaning it does not refer to a determinate reality. The commentator responds sharply, calling this a stubborn denial. He argues that after rejecting atomism (the part), the existence of a substance with no internal division is established. Such a substance is not ambiguous — it is a definite reality. The ambiguous concept, he adds, is merely a mental abstraction. Let's see how he dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage from 'قوله : ( فإن الطبيعة الجسمية مضناً إلخ)' to the end. Then read the translation. Identify the opponent's claim and the commentator's response.
The word 'مضناً' is likely an OCR error for 'مطلقاً' (absolutely).
2
Understand the key move
The commentator says: after denying the existence of a part (i.e., atom), we prove the existence of a substance with no division. This substance is not ambiguous; the ambiguous is only a concept in the mind.
3
Grasp the implication
If the bodily nature were ambiguous, it could not exist externally. But we have established an external substance, so the nature is determinate. This reinforces the earlier conclusion that body-ness is a specific nature.
This connects back to the previous lesson's argument.

Proof of Hyle from Determinate Place
Iji presents the first proof for hyle from the necessity of a determinate place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why hyle is necessary as a substratum for a body to occupy a determinate place.

Core Idea
Hyle is needed because without it, a body would have no reason to be in one place rather than another.

Before we can accept the existence of prime matter (hyle), we need a rational proof. Iji begins with a simple observation: every body occupies a place. But what makes it occupy one specific place over another? He argues that the bodily form alone cannot provide that specification, so hyle must exist as a neutral substratum that receives specification from something else.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how Iji sets up the necessity of a place for every body.
The passage is the first part of the source chunk, ending with 'لا معيناً.'
2
Understand the necessity of a determinate place
Iji states that a body cannot be in two places at once, and without a specifier it would have no reason to be in one specific place. Grasp this foundational idea.
3
Analyze why bodily form alone is insufficient
The bodily form only demands an absolute place, not a specific one. So it cannot serve as the specifier. This forces the need for hyle as a neutral substrate.
Think: if the form determined a specific place, it would have to be a particular form, but bodily form is generic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : إثبات الهيولى في الجسم
جسم، وكل جسم لا بد له من حيز ولا يمكن أن يكون جسم واحد في زمان واحد في مكانين أو أكثر، والأخير باطل لعدم المخصص بالنسبة إلى ذلك البعض، لأن الهيولى على ذلك التقدير نسبتها إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية وكذا نسبة الصورة الجسمية، فإنها تقتضي حيزاً مطلقاً لا معيناً. فإن قيل : لعل صورة نوعية تحل في الهيولى مع حلول الصورة الجسمية فيها فهي تخصصها بحيز معين. وأيضا ينتقض ما ذكرتم بالجزء المعين من الأرض ومن سائر العناصر الكلية واختصاصه بحيزه المعين بلا مخصص يقتضيه، فإن نسبة أجزاء العنصر الكلي إلى أجزاء حيزه على السواء مع أن كل واحد من أجزائه حاصل في حيز معين. قلنا : الصورة النوعية وإن عينت موضعاً كلياً لكن نسبتها إلى جميع أجزاء حيز الكل واحدة، فالكلام في تخصيصه بحيزه المعين من أجزاء حيز الكل. فإن الهيولى المجسمة مع تلك الصورة النوعية إما أن تحصل في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء، أو في بعضها أو لا تحصل في شيء منها، والكل باطل.
First Observatory - Eighth Objective: Proving Hyle in the Body.
Body, and every body must have a place and it is impossible for one body to be in two places or more at one time, and the latter is false due to the absence of a specifier for that particular part, because hyle on that estimation has equal relation to all places, and likewise the relation of the bodily form, for it requires an absolute place not a specific one. If it is said: Perhaps a specific form indwells in the hyle along with the indwelling of the bodily form in it, so it specifies it to a specific place. Also what you mentioned is refuted by the specific part of earth and of all the universal elements, and its specification to its specific place without a specifier that necessitates it, for the relation of the parts of the universal element to the parts of its place is equal while each one of its parts is obtained in a specific place. We say: The specific form, although it determines a universal location, but its relation to all parts of the place of the whole is one, so the discussion is about its specification to its specific place from the parts of the place of the whole. For the hyle with that specific form either occurs in each one of those parts, or in some of them, or occurs in none of them, and all are false.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of hyle (prime matter) in physical bodies. It begins by stating that every body must occupy a place, and cannot be in two places at once; if hyle were without form, it would have equal relation to all places, so a specifier is needed. The objection raises that a specific form could provide that specification. The reply argues that the specific form only determines a universal location, not a particular part, so the problem of specification remains. The hyle would have to occupy all, some, or none of the parts—all impossible—thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة جسمية
حيز
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the bodily form alone can determine a specific place.
Correction: Iji shows the bodily form only requires an absolute place, not a specific one; it cannot be the specifier.

Checkpoint
What does Iji claim about the bodily form's relation to place?
A) It determines a specific place.
B) It requires an absolute place, not a specific one.
C) It has no relation to place.
D) It requires multiple places.

Key Takeaway
The bodily form alone cannot specify a determinate place, so a neutral substratum (hyle) is required to receive specification from a specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Iji proves that without a specifier, hyle relates equally to all places, having no reason to be in one rather than another. Reflect on your own life: What 'specifiers' have determined the path you are on—your career, your city, your relationships? Have you ever considered that without God's specification, you would have no meaningful direction? How does this realization compel you to seek His guidance in your next decision?
?
The bodily form only demands an absolute place, not a specific one. This means that your physical existence alone cannot give you a unique purpose or location. What are the specific influences (family, society, your own choices) that have given your life a particular shape? Are you conscious that these are ultimately secondary to the divine Specifier? How can you realign your life to be more receptive to God's specification?
?
In the argument, the need for a specifier arises because the body cannot be in two places at once. This points to a fundamental limitation of material existence. How does this limitation reflect the human condition—you cannot be in two spiritual states simultaneously? What does this teach you about the need for a single, focused commitment to God, rather than a divided heart?

Reply to the Specific Form Objection
Iji presents and refutes the specific form objection to his proof for hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji refutes the objection that a specific form could serve as the specifier of place.

Core Idea
A specific form only determines a universal location, not a particular part, so it cannot explain why this part of matter is here.

The objection claims that a specific form (ṣūrat al-nawʿiyya) entering the hyle together with the bodily form could specify it to a particular place. Iji responds by showing that even with a specific form, the hyle would still be indifferent to the sub-parts of that universal place. The problem of particularization remains.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic passage for the objection and reply, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, counterexample, response.
This passage is the second half of the source chunk.
2
Understand the objection
The objector suggests a specific form (e.g., the form of earth) dwelling in hyle could specify its place. They also give an example of a part of earth occupying a specific place naturally.
3
Analyze Iji's response
Iji concedes the specific form determines a universal location (e.g., the whole region of earth), but it does not determine which particular part of that region. He then presents three possibilities for the hyle's relation to the sub-parts and shows each is false.
The three possibilities: hyle in all sub-parts, some, or none.
4
Appreciate the argument's force
Since all three options are impossible, the objection fails. The need for a specifier beyond the specific form remains, reinforcing the proof for hyle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Is there an area in your life where you have allowed a 'universal explanation' (like fate, personality, or society) to cover over the need for a personal Specifier? What would change if you truly believed that every particular part of your life has a Specifier?

Second Objection: Parts of Universal Elements and the Reply
Iji presents and responds to an objection about specification of parts of elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun defend the need for a specifier against objections regarding the parts of universal elements.

Core Idea
Hypothetical parts do not need real specifiers, but actual specification requires a cause.

The second objection challenges the need for a specifier, suggesting that forms or states can determine place, and that isolated hyle might itself be universal. The reply carefully distinguishes between hypothetical parts and real ones, and explains how a chain of previous forms accounts for the specification of actual parts of earth. This passage sharpens the argument for a specifier by addressing subtle metaphysical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that a form or state could specify parts of place for hyle, and that isolated hyle might already be universal, so no extra specifier is needed.
Notice that the objection tries to remove the need for a specifier altogether.
2
Understand the initial reply
The reply first argues that the parts of a universal element are merely hypothetical (مفروضة), not externally existing, so they don't require places.
This is a key distinction between conceptual and real existence.
3
Follow the analysis of earth's part
The reply then explains that a specific part of earth gets its place because its matter had a previous form that determined it to that or another place, from which it moved rectilinearly.
The chain of previous forms goes back infinitely, showing that specification is not arbitrary.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The specifier is the previous position obtained from a prior form, and this chain extends infinitely, implying each specification has a cause.
This supports the overall argument for a first specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : جاز أن يقارن الهيولى صورة أخرى أو حالة من الأحوال تعين لها بعض أجزاء المكان الكلي، وأيضاً قد تكون الهيولى المجردة هيولى عنصر كلي، فلا حاجة في التخصيص إلى غير الصورة النوعية. فإن قلت : ننقل الكلام إلى اختصاص أجزاء ذلك العنصر بأمكنتها الجزئية، قلنا : تلك الأجزاء مفروضة فيه لا موجودة في الخارج فلا تقتضي مكاناً. وأيضاً جاز أن يفرض هناك حالة مخصصة للأجزاء بوضع معين. والجزء من الأرض إنما اختص بحيزه المعين الذي هو فيه لكون مادته قبل تلك الصورة الأرضية كانت لها صورة أخرى مخصصة لذلك الجزء بذلك الحيز، أو مخصصة له بحيز آخر انتقل ذلك الجزء منه بالاستقامة إلى ذلك الحيز. والحاصل أن مخصص ذلك الجزء من الأرض بحيزه المعين هو الوضع السابق الحاصل لمادته بسبب صورة سابقة إما في ذلك الحيز أو في حيز آخر انتقل ذلك الجزء بعد حصول صورته الأرضية منه إلى حيزه على أقرب الطرق، وتلك الصورة السابقة مسبوقة بصورة ثالثة، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية.
And it may be said: It is possible that another form or a state from among the states accompanies the hyle and determines for it some parts of the universal place, and also the isolated hyle may be the hyle of a universal element, so there is no need in specification for other than the specific form. For if you say: we transfer the discussion to the specification of the parts of that element to their particular places, we say: those parts are hypothetical in it, not existing externally, so they do not require a place. And also it is possible that there is a specifying state for the parts with a specific position. And the part of earth is specified to its specific place in which it is only because its matter before that earthly form had another form specifying that part to that place, or specifying it to another place from which that part moved rectilinearly to that place. And the conclusion is that the specifier of that part of earth to its specific place is the previous position obtained for its matter due to a previous form either in that place or in another place from which that part, after the occurrence of its earthly form, moved to its place along the shortest path, and that previous form is preceded by a third form, and so on ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: that a specific form or state could determine the place of hyle, and that isolated hyle might be the hyle of a universal element, so no additional specifier is needed. The reply argues that the parts of the element are hypothetical (مفروضة) and do not exist externally, so they do not require a place. It then explains the determination of a specific part of earth through a chain of previous forms and positions, tracing specification back through an infinite regress of forms.

Correction: The reply clarifies that the parts of a universal element are merely hypothetical (مفروضة) and not externally existing, so they do not require a separate specifier.

The Form's Need for Hyle Through Finitude
Iji's first proof that corporeal form needs hyle, based on finitude of extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof demonstrates that the corporeal form, being a substantial extension, must be finite and therefore requires hyle.

Core Idea
Every corporeal form is extended and finite, so it cannot exist without matter.

In this passage, Iji presents the first of three reasons why the corporeal form itself cannot exist independently of hyle. The argument hinges on the nature of extension: anything extended occupies space and is therefore finite. This finitude implies dependence on a substrate, i.e., hyle. The commentary clarifies the reasoning and links it to earlier discussions about the form's need for indwelling.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, including both the main argument and the commentator's remarks. Let the structure sink in.
Notice that the commentator clarifies each phrase of the matn.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms 'مشار إليها' (pointed to) and 'متناه' (finite). Understand their relation to extension.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: form without hyle → must be pointed to → finite → requires hyle. Note the additional point that even if not pointed to, finitude still holds.
This is a proof by disjunction.
4
Understand the importance
Reflect on why this proof is foundational for issues like the eternity of the world. The commentary mentions this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثها أي ثالث التفاريع ( أن الصورة الجسمية أيضاً ( لا تخلو عن الهيولى لوجوه ) ثلاثة : ( الأول : لو فرضنا صورة بلا هيولى ) كانت إما مشاراً إليها أو غير مشار إليها فإن كانت مشاراً إليها كان ذلك المشار إليه (متناهياً). قوله : (لكانت إلخ) هذا لا يجوزه العقل بعد ملاحظة أنها امتداد جوهري، فإن الامتداد الجوهري لا يمكن وجوده بدون فراغ يشغله فلا بد أن يكون مشاراً إليه. قوله : (فإن كانت مشاراً إليها كان متناهياً هذه قضية اتفاقية لو لم يكن مشاراً إليها، كانت أيضاً متناهية لأن الثابت بالبراهين تناهي الأبعاد سواء فرض مشار إليها أو لا . بل لا تزيد إلخ) يعني أن المراد الوحدة في المقدار وهي المساواة، لأن الهيولى لا تخلو عن الصورة هذا المطلب وإن علم مما تقدم حيث ثبت أن الصورة بذاتها تقتضي حلول وهو الوجه الثاني بعينه إلا أنه لما كان أصلاً لقدم العالم وغيره من المسائل جعلوه مطلباً برأسه حينئذ إذ في إثباته بالوجه الأول بيان احتياج الصورة إلى المادة والشكل والتساوي ووجوب تناهيها، وأن الهيولى لا تحتاج إلى الصورة المعينة.
Third of them—i.e., the third branch—is that the corporeal form also does not lack hyle, for three reasons. First: If we suppose a form without hyle, it would either be pointed to or not pointed to; if it is pointed to, then that which is pointed to is finite. His saying: ('it would be' etc.)—the intellect does not allow this after noting that it is a substantial extension, for substantial extension cannot exist without a void it occupies, so it must be pointed to. His saying: ('if it is pointed to, it is finite'—this is an agreed-upon proposition; even if it were not pointed to, it would still be finite because it is established by proofs that dimensions are finite whether assumed pointed to or not. Rather, ('does not increase' etc.) means that what is intended is unity in quantity, which is equality, because hyle does not lack form. This point is known from what preceded, where it was established that the form by itself requires indwelling, and that is the very second aspect, except that since it is a foundational matter for the eternity of the world and other issues, they made it an independent topic. For in proving it by the first aspect, there is a demonstration of the form's need for matter, shape, equality, and the necessity of its finitude, and that hyle does not need a specific form.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage introduces the third branch of arguments proving that the corporeal form itself requires hyle. The first proof states that if a form existed without hyle, it could either be pointed to (i.e., occupy space) or not. If it is pointed to, it is finite. The commentator adds that this is axiomatic; even if not pointed to, dimensions are finitely known. He further explains that the term 'does not increase' refers to unity in quantity, which is equality, and that the discussion is a continuation of earlier proofs, particularly the second aspect about the form's need for indwelling. This issue is treated separately due to its role in debates about the eternity of the world.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a form could exist without matter as an independent entity, but the argument shows it must be extended and finite, thus needing hyle.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that any corporeal form, being extended, is finite and therefore cannot exist without a material substrate (hyle).

Checkpoint

A) It is infinite.
B) It is finite.
C) It is immaterial.
D) It is not extended.

Key Takeaway
The corporeal form, being substantial extension, must be finite and therefore depends on hyle as its material substrate.

The Wax Objection and the Meaning of Connection and Disconnection
Explains the initial objection and its refutation based on the meaning of connection/disconnection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the wax example does not refute the argument that shape change requires connection and disconnection in a continuous body.

Core Idea
Shape change in a continuous body always involves internal separation and connection, even if not visible.

The author defends the first argument against an objection based on wax. The objection claims that wax changes shape without being cut, so connection and disconnection are not necessary. The response clarifies that the intended meaning of connection/disconnection applies to the body's parts, not external cuts. This lesson examines that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The objection uses wax as a counterexample. Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully.
Notice the phrase 'as you see in wax'.
2
Understand the two meanings of shape change
The response distinguishes between shape as outer form and shape as formation of bodyness. Connection/disconnection is necessary in the latter sense.
3
Apply the distinction to the wax example
Wax is a continuous body; its shape change involves internal rearrangement of parts, which is a form of separation and connection.
This refutes the objection.

His statement: (So it is not necessitated from the attribution of shape etc.) It cannot be said: Everything that is receptive to affection and change of shapes is receptive to connection and disconnection, as in wax for example. For we say: We do not concede that change of shapes only occurs through connection and disconnection, for that is the point of dispute. And it cannot also be said: Everything that is receptive to the mentioned disconnection can be receptive to connection and disconnection, even if not actually receptive to both, because this possibility is also denied.
