For we say: If that suffices) to repel the prevention (to be independent in indicating the requirement, by saying: If the form separates from matter, it would be receptive to imaginary division leading to disjunctive division, so the independence of bodyness in accepting separation and connection would follow, and we have refuted it. On this, these premises) mentioned in your proof (are all wasted) no need for them). And an answer) to this which we said (is possible) that it does not contradict the reality (of the discourse and the correctness of the proof with its premises, but it is of the type of specifying the shorter path).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the first argument against objections. It begins with a potential objection: shape change does not require connection and disconnection, as seen in wax that takes different shapes without being cut. The author responds by distinguishing the intended meaning: if 'shape' refers to the outer form of a continuous body, then change requires separation or connection; if it refers to the formation of bodyness itself, then shape change implies separation of parts. Then another objection argues that the bodily form could by itself necessitate a specific shape for the whole universe, with an additional concomitant causing wholeness and partiality. The response is that such a concomitant would be necessary for the existence of the abstract form, not its quiddity, so it does not imply separability. Further, the claim that receptivity to shape change implies receptivity to connection/disconnection is rejected as the point of dispute. The passage then discusses imaginary division: accepting shape change in an abstract form entails imaginary division, which leads to disjunctive division and absurdity. The author concludes that this does not undermine the proof but simply offers a shorter route.

الشكل
الشمعة

Correction: Wax is a continuous body; its shape change involves internal rearrangement of parts, which is a form of separation and connection.

Checkpoint

B) He distinguishes between two meanings of shape change.
C) He denies that wax is a body.
D) He abandons the argument.

Key Takeaway
Shape change in a continuous body always involves internal connection and disconnection, even if not externally visible.

The Bodily Form and the Necessitation of Shape
Addresses the objection about bodily form necessitating shape and the response about a concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that bodily form could necessitate a specific shape by itself and the response about the concomitant.

Core Idea
The bodily form cannot by itself determine the shape of the universe without an additional concomitant.

A second objection argues that the bodily form itself might necessitate a specific shape for all bodies, but the author responds that such a shape would require an additional concomitant that is necessary for the form's existence, not its quiddity. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection suggests the bodily form could determine shape by itself. Note the claim about the whole universe.
2
Understand the response
The author posits an additional concomitant that causes wholeness and partiality. This concomitant is necessary for the form's existence after abstraction.
The form's quiddity does not require it.
3
Consider the implication
The concomitant does not imply the form can separate from matter; it only explains particularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن لمانع أن يمنع أن الشكل ) وتبدله ( إنما يكون بالاتصال والانفصال كما ترى (في الشمعة ) فإنها ( تتشكل بأشكال مختلفة من غير فصل) ووصل فليس يلزم من استناد الشكل العارض للصورة المجردة إلى سبب مغاير لنفس الجسمية وكونها قابلة لشكل
جزء
عن
:
عليك ظهر لك إن كان النقض بالأجزاء المفروضة للفلك، وأرادوا أن الرد الذي ذكره الشارح وهو مذكور في المحاكمات غير وارد، لأن الاستدلال أيضاً كان يفرض جزء المفروضة للجمسية بأن فرضها يستلزم رفعها فتدبر وأما قوله وأيضاً الجزئية إلخ، فالجواب عنه أن اعتبار التأخر ليس لأجل أن له مدخلا في منع مساواة الجزء للكل بل لأنه في الواقع كذلك لأن الأجزاء المفروضة للبسيط لا تكون إلا متأخرة بخلاف المركب وقد صرح به المحقق في شرحه .
قوله : (لمانع أن يمنع إلخ) هذا إنما يرد لو أريد بقوله : هو أي الشكل آخر إلا بالفصل والوصل في نفس الجسم أما لو أريد به وما هو أي تشكل الجسمية إلا بفصل بعضها عن البعض فلا ورود له كما لا يخفى فإن تعدد الأشكال في الامتداد إلا باعتبار فصل بعضه ولولاه لكان امتداد واحد .
قوله : ( فلا تكون هناك إلا الطبيعة الجسمية الحصر ممنوع لم لا يجوز أن تكون الصورة الجمسية لذاتها مقتضية لمجموع عالم الأجسام شكلاً معيناً ومقداراً معيناً، ذلك يكون ومع هناك أمر آخر مقارن للصورة الجسمية حال كونها مجردة عن الهيولى، ويكون ذلك الأمر سبباً للكلية والجزئية فإن قيل : فحينئذ تكون الصورة قابلة لشكل آخر وذلك بالفصل والوصل بدون الهيولى وهو باطل. قلنا : ممنوع، فإن ذلك الأمر لازم لوجود الصورة المجردة بعد تجردها وإن لم یكن لازماً لماهيته كما مر .
قوله: (فليس يلزم من استناد الشكل إلخ لا يقال : كل ما كان قابلاً للانفعال وتبدل الأشكال فهو قابل للاتصال والانفصال كما في الشمعة مثلاً، لأنا نقول: لا نسلم أن تبدل الأشكال لا يكون إلا بالاتصال والانفصال، فإن ذلك محل النزاع بعد ولا يقال أيضاً : كل ما كان قابلاً للانفصال المذكور فهو يمكن أن يكون قابلاً للاتصال والانفصال، وإن لم يكن قابلاً لهما بالفعل لأن هذا الإمكان ممنوع أيضا .
آخر استقلالها بقبول الفصل والوصل كما زعمتم ) ولا يجاب ) عن هذا المنع ( بأن ذلك ) أي قبول تبدل الأشكال ) يقتضي ) لا محالة ( القسمة الوهمية ) إذ لا يتصور تبدل شكل فيما لا يمكن أن يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء ) وتفضي ) القسمة الوهمية كما مر ) إلى ( القسمة ( الانفكاكية ويلزم المحال المذكور لأنا نقول : لو كفى ذلك ) في دفع المنع ( الاستقل بالدلالة على المطلوب بأن يقال : لو فارقت الصورة المادة لكانت قابلة للقسمة الوهمية المفضية إلى الانفكاكية فيلزم استقلال الجسمية بقبول الفصل والوصل، وقد أبطلناه وعلى هذا فكان هذه المقدمات) المذكورة في دليلكم ( كلها ضائعة ) لا حاجة إليها ( ويمكن الجواب ) عن هذا الذي قلناه ( بأنه لا ينافي حقيقة (الكلام وصحة الدليل بمقدماته، بل هو من قبيل تعيين الطريق الذي هو أقصر.
But an objector may object that shape (and its change) only occurs through connection and disconnection, as you see (in wax) for it (takes on various shapes without separation and connection. His statement: (An objector may object etc.) This only applies if what is meant by his statement 'it' i.e. shape is only through separation and connection in the body itself; but if what is meant is that the formation of bodyness is only through separation of some parts from others, then it does not apply, as is not hidden, for multiplicity of shapes in extension is only by consideration of separation of some of it, otherwise it would be one extension.
His statement: (So there is nothing except the bodily nature) The restriction is denied. Why is it not allowed that the bodily form by itself necessitates for the totality of the world of bodies a specific shape and a specific magnitude, and that there is another thing concomitant with the bodily form while it is abstracted from hyle, and that thing is the cause of wholeness and partiality. If it is said: Then the form would be receptive to another shape through separation and connection without hyle, which is false. We say: It is denied, for that thing is necessary for the existence of the abstracted form after its abstraction, even if it is not necessary for its quiddity, as has passed.
His statement: (So it is not necessitated from the attribution of shape etc.) It cannot be said: Everything that is receptive to affection and change of shapes is receptive to connection and disconnection, as in wax for example. For we say: We do not concede that change of shapes only occurs through connection and disconnection, for that is the point of dispute. And it cannot also be said: Everything that is receptive to the mentioned disconnection can be receptive to connection and disconnection, even if not actually receptive to both, because this possibility is also denied.
...the end of its independence in accepting separation and connection as you claimed) and it is not answered) about this prevention (that) i.e. acceptance of change of shapes) necessarily (entails) imaginary division) since it is inconceivable that shape change in something in which it is impossible to suppose something other than something) and (imaginary division leads as before to (disjunctive division) and the mentioned absurdity follows. For we say: If that suffices) to repel the prevention (to be independent in indicating the requirement, by saying: If the form separates from matter, it would be receptive to imaginary division leading to disjunctive division, so the independence of bodyness in accepting separation and connection would follow, and we have refuted it. On this, these premises) mentioned in your proof (are all wasted) no need for them). And an answer) to this which we said (is possible) that it does not contradict the reality (of the discourse and the correctness of the proof with its premises, but it is of the type of specifying the shorter path).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the first argument against objections. It begins with a potential objection: shape change does not require connection and disconnection, as seen in wax that takes different shapes without being cut. The author responds by distinguishing the intended meaning: if 'shape' refers to the outer form of a continuous body, then change requires separation or connection; if it refers to the formation of bodyness itself, then shape change implies separation of parts. Then another objection argues that the bodily form could by itself necessitate a specific shape for the whole universe, with an additional concomitant causing wholeness and partiality. The response is that such a concomitant would be necessary for the existence of the abstract form, not its quiddity, so it does not imply separability. Further, the claim that receptivity to shape change implies receptivity to connection/disconnection is rejected as the point of dispute. The passage then discusses imaginary division: accepting shape change in an abstract form entails imaginary division, which leads to disjunctive division and absurdity. The author concludes that this does not undermine the proof but simply offers a shorter route.

Key Terms
الصورة الجسمية
الهيولى
الكلية
الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that if the bodily form had a concomitant, then it would be receptive to separation from matter.
Correction: The concomitant is necessary for the existence of the abstract form, not for its quiddity, so it does not imply separability.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the concomitant not imply the form's ability to separate from matter?
A) Because the concomitant is part of the form's quiddity.
B) Because the concomitant is necessary only for the form's existence after abstraction.
C) Because the form is material.
D) Because the concomitant is accidental.

Key Takeaway
The bodily form can have a concomitant that causes particularity, but that concomitant is necessary for the form's existence, not its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the bodily form abstracted from matter—a purely intellectual concept. Yet the author insists it still requires a concomitant. What does this reveal about your own life? Identify one area where you believe you can exist independently, without a deeper reality sustaining you. How does this lesson challenge that belief? What will you change today?
?
The passage argues that a thing can have a concomitant necessary for its existence but not its essence. Reflect on a time you tried to separate yourself from something essential to your existence (like a relationship, habit, or belief), thinking it was a separable accident but found it was necessary for your being. How did that lead you to a false conclusion? How does this apply to your dependence on Allah's attributes? What step will you take to realign your understanding?

Imaginary Division and the Independence of Bodyness
Demonstrates that imaginary division leads to disjunctive division and defends the original proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how imaginary division leads to disjunctive division and why the premises of the proof are not wasted despite a simpler argument.

Core Idea
Imaginary division in an abstract form would lead to actual separation, which is absurd, so the original proof stands.

The final objection involves imaginary division: if the abstract form is receptive to shape change, it would be subject to imaginary division, which leads to disjunctive division and absurdity. The author concludes that the original proof's premises are not wasted but a shorter path exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about imaginary division
The passage argues that shape change in an abstract form entails imaginary division.
Imaginary division means supposing parts in a simple entity.
2
Understand the consequence
Imaginary division leads to disjunctive division (actual separation), which is absurd for a simple form.
3
See the final point
The author notes that the original proof's premises are not wasted; a shorter argument is available but the original remains valid.

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you encounter a scholar's argument that requires careful reasoning about what is conceivable and what is absurd, do you tend to give up and say 'this is too hard' rather than wrestle with it until you see its strength? What will you do to cultivate intellectual patience with demanding truths?
?
The author shows that even if a shorter path exists, the original proof is still correct. Are you quick to abandon older, established proofs in favor of newer ones, without verifying that the old still hold? How will you train yourself to value truth regardless of the path length?

Objection to Form's Independence via Imaginary Division
Objection and answer regarding form's independence via imaginary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that form's independence would entail imaginary division leading to separative division, and the defense that this does not undermine the proof.

Core Idea
Imaginary division is a key test for independence from matter.

In this passage, the objector argues that if form were independent of matter, it would have to accept change of shape, which requires imaginary division. Since imaginary division leads to an impossible conclusion, the claim of independence is refuted. The answer clarifies that this objection does not invalidate the original proof but merely suggests a shorter path.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Start by reading the objector's argument: form's independence would mean it can accept division and connection, leading to shape changes that entail imaginary division.
The objector uses the term 'القسمة الوهمية' (imaginary division).
2
Trace the Consequence
Note that imaginary division leads to separative division, which is considered impossible. This is the core of the objection.
The objector refers to a previously established rule that imaginary division leads to separative division.
3
Examine the Answer
The answer states that this objection does not refute the proof; it only points out a shorter argument. The original proof's premises are still sound.
The answer emphasizes that the objection is about economy of argument, not logical validity.
4
Understand the Gloss on Shape
The gloss clarifies that shape (shakl) requires extension, so imaginary division only applies to things with a specific nature having multiple individuals.
This limits the scope of the objection.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آخر استقلالها بقبول الفصل والوصل كما زعمتم ) ولا يجاب ) عن هذا المنع ( بأن ذلك ) أي قبول تبدل الأشكال ) يقتضي ) لا محالة ( القسمة الوهمية ) إذ لا يتصور تبدل شكل فيما لا يمكن أن يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء ) وتفضي ) القسمة الوهمية كما مر ) إلى ( القسمة ( الانفكاكية ويلزم المحال المذكور لأنا نقول : لو كفى ذلك ) في دفع المنع ( الاستقل بالدلالة على المطلوب بأن يقال : لو فارقت الصورة المادة لكانت قابلة للقسمة الوهمية المفضية إلى الانفكاكية فيلزم استقلال الجسمية بقبول الفصل والوصل، وقد أبطلناه وعلى هذا فكان هذه المقدمات) المذكورة في دليلكم ( كلها ضائعة ) لا حاجة إليها ( ويمكن الجواب ) عن هذا الذي قلناه ( بأنه لا ينافي حقيقة (الكلام وصحة الدليل بمقدماته، بل هو من قبيل تعيين الطريق الذي هو أقصر. قوله : (تبدل إلخ) بل أصل الشكل إذا إحاطة الجد والحدود لا يتصور فيما لا امتداد ويقتضي القسمة الوهمية أي فيما له طبيعة نوعية متعددة الأفراد كما فيما نحن فيه . قوله : (كما مر من أن حكم الأمثال واحدة .
The last [part] of its being independent in accepting division and connection as you claimed; and it is not answered regarding this prevention that that, i.e. acceptance of change of shapes, necessarily entails imaginary division, for change of shape is inconceivable in what cannot be supposed to have something other than something; and the imaginary division leads, as previously mentioned, to separative division, and the aforementioned impossible consequence follows. For we say: if that sufficed to repel the objection regarding independence in indicating the desired conclusion, it would be said: if the form separated from matter, it would be capable of imaginary division leading to separative division, so the independence of corporeality in accepting division and connection would follow, and we have disproven it. Thus all these premises mentioned in your proof are wasted, unnecessary. And one can answer regarding what we have said that it does not contradict the reality of the discourse and the soundness of the proof with its premises; rather it is a matter of specifying the path that is shorter. His statement: (change etc.) Rather the origin of shape, if [it means] surrounding by boundary and limits, is inconceivable in what has no extension, and it entails imaginary division, i.e., in what has a specific nature with multiple individuals, as in our case. His statement: (as previously mentioned that the rule of similar things is one).
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer within the argument for the existence of hyle. The objector claims that if form were independent of matter, it would be capable of accepting change of shape, which requires imaginary division (al-qisma al-wahmiyya). The objector argues that imaginary division leads to separative division (al-infikakiyya), which is impossible. The author of the objection asserts that their premises are sufficient and not wasted. The answer provided is that this does not undermine the soundness of the discourse; rather it is a matter of choosing a shorter path. The gloss explains that shape (shakl) requires extension, and imaginary division only applies to things with a specific nature having multiple individuals.

Key Terms

قبول الفصل والوصل (accepting division and connection)
القسمة الوهمية (imaginary division)
القسمة الانفكاكية (separative division)
شكل (shape)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That imaginary division cannot apply to forms because they are immaterial.
Correction: The passage shows that if form were independent, it would have to accept change of shape, which entails imaginary division. But the answer clarifies that shape requires extension, so imaginary division only applies to extended things. Thus the objection is not relevant to truly immaterial forms.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim necessarily follows from form's acceptance of change of shape?
A) Imaginary division
B) Separative division
C) Form's independence
D) The body's division

Key Takeaway
The objection that form's independence would lead to impossible imaginary and separative divisions is answered by noting that it does not undermine the original proof but only points to a shorter argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a deep truth because a clever-sounding objection made you doubt the original argument, only later to realize the objection was merely a shortcut to the same conclusion? Will you now be more cautious before rejecting a well-grounded proof?
?
The objector thought he found a fatal flaw, but the response shows it does not undermine the original proof. Is there an area of your faith where you have accepted an objection without re-examining the strength of the original evidence? Are you willing to revisit that issue today?
?
The gloss notes that shape requires extension, and so imaginary division only applies to material things. Do you sometimes confuse the properties of created matter with the nature of spiritual realities? How can you train your mind to avoid such category mistakes?

The Second Argument: Corporeal Form's Need for Hyle
Second argument: form's inherence proves its need for matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam argument that corporeal form cannot exist independently and must inhere in hyle.

Core Idea
If form were independent, it would not need a substrate—but it does inhere, so it cannot be independent.

This lesson presents the second of three arguments demonstrating the necessity of hyle. The argument uses a conditional: if corporeal form were self-subsisting, it would have no need for a substrate; yet we observe it inheres in bodies, contradicting independence. This reasoning establishes the essential dependence of form on matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the conditional logic of the argument. Notice the structure: 'if... then... but... therefore...'.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms 'corporeal form', 'hyle', and 'substrate' in the passage. Understand how each functions in the argument.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why the assumption of independence leads to a contradiction. This shows that form must depend on a substrate.
This is a classic kalam proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ( من الوجوه الثلاثة ( الصورة الجسمية (لو) خلت عن الهيولى و (قامت بذاتها لاستغنت في نفسها ( عن المحل فلا تحل فيه) أصلاً لكنها حالة فيه فلا يجوز حلولها عنه وقد عرفت جوابه . قوله : ( وقد عرفت جوابه من أنه يجوز أن لا تكون محتاجة ولا مستعيرة . قوله : ( وقد عرفت جوابه وقد عرفت أيضاً ما في هذا الجواب من أنه لا واسطة بين الاحتياج الذاتي إلى المحل والغنى الذاتي عنه، وقد عرفت أيضاً ما ذكرنا في توجيه كلام المصنف .
The second (of the three aspects): The corporeal form, if it were devoid of hyle and subsisted by itself, it would be independent in itself of a substrate, so it would not inhere in it at all; but it is inherent in it, so its inherence in it cannot be revoked; and you have known its answer. His statement: (and you have known its answer) that it is permissible that it is neither needy nor borrowing. His statement: (and you have known its answer and you have also known what there is in this answer: that there is no intermediate between essential need for a substrate and essential independence from it; and you have also known what we mentioned in directing the author's words).
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the necessity of hyle. The argument states that if the corporeal form were independent of hyle and subsisted by itself, it would not need a substrate and therefore would not inhere in anything. But in fact it does inhere in bodies, so it cannot be independent. The answer, referenced as already known, is that it is possible for the form to be neither in need of a substrate nor borrowing one. The gloss elaborates that there is no intermediate between essential need and essential independence, and this is part of the author's explanation.

Answering the Argument and Its Critique
Answer and critique regarding the necessity of a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the second argument and the critique that there is no middle state between essential need and independence.

Core Idea
The answer claims form might be neither needy nor borrowing, but the critique says there is no middle ground.

After presenting the second argument, the text references an answer that has been previously given: the form could be neither in need of a substrate nor borrowing one. However, the author then points out a flaw in that answer, namely that there is no intermediate between essential need and essential independence. This lesson explores the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the phrase 'يجوز أن لا تكون محتاجة ولا مستعيرة' (it is permissible that it is neither needy nor borrowing).
This is the proposed answer.
2
Understand the critique
The critique states 'لا واسطة بين الاحتياج الذاتي إلى المحل والغنى الذاتي عنه' (there is no intermediate between essential need and essential independence).
This is a key logical principle.
3
Identify key terms
Note the terms 'احتياج ذاتي' (essential need) and 'غنى ذاتي' (essential independence). They are opposites with no gradation.
4
Reflect on the dialectic
Consider why the answer fails: if there is no middle state, then the form must be either in need or independent. The answer's third option is invalid.
This shows the rigor of kalam reasoning.

Third Argument: No Distinction Without Matter
Third argument against form without matter based on loss of distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that form cannot be differentiated into whole and part without matter.

Core Idea
Without matter, whole and part become indistinguishable.

This lesson presents the third argument against form existing independently of matter. It examines the logical implications of separating form from matter, specifically regarding the distinction between the whole and its parts. The argument shows that such distinction is only possible through matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Then read the translation. Note the structure: assumption, hypothetical scenario, two logical possibilities, and the gloss.
The passage includes both the main argument and a clarifying commentary.
2
Understand the scenario
The argument supposes that form could exist without matter. It then imagines a composite whole (hyle+form) and considers what happens if its form separates before and after division. Focus on the meaning of 'whole' and 'form of the whole'.
The 'whole' here is the composite, not the form alone.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
If there is no distinction between the form of the whole and the form of the part, an absurdity follows: the thing with another is like it without. If there is a distinction, then earlier principles on individuation tell us that no distinction or multiplicity exists without matter. Both options lead to problems for the idea of form without matter.
4
Examine the commentator's clarification
The gloss explains that wholeness and partness are due to matter. If the form is isolated, there is no whole or part, so the absurdity does not actually arise because the scenario is incoherent. The argument ultimately shows that the initial assumption (form without matter) is impossible because it leads to a paradox.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( من تلك الوجوه أن يقول : على تقدير أن يجوز خلو الصورة عن المادة ( نفرض الكل تفارقه صورته قبل التجزئة وبعدها فإن كان لا تميز ثمة بين صورة الكل وصورة الجزء ( فالشيء مع غيره كهو لا وإن كان ) بينهما تميز ( وقد عرفت ) في مباحث التعين ( أنه لا تميز) ولا تعدد قوله : (فإن كان لا تميز (إلخ فيه أن الكلية والجزئية باعتبار المادة فإذا فرضت الصورة منفردة عن المادة فلا كل ولا جزء ولا تعدد فيها ولا يلزم أن يكون الشيء مع غيره كهو لا معه فتدبر . قوله : ( نفرض الكل تفارقه صورته المراد من الكل هاهنا المجموع المركب من الهيولى والصورة وقوله : صورته هذه الإضافة من قبيل إضافة الجزء إلى الكل ومعنى مفارقة الصورة عن الكل هو أن تبقى الصورة بدون الكل فتكون حينئذ مجردة عن الهيولى وقوله : قبل التجزئة وبعدها متعلق بقوله: تفارقه، ثم إن إمكان وقوع المجموع الذي فرضتم ممنوع في هوية الصورة التي فرض كونها مجردة عن الهيولى لا بد لإثباته من دليل .
The third (of those aspects) is to say: on the assumption that it is permissible for the form to be devoid of matter, we suppose the whole whose form separates from it before and after division. Then if there is no distinction there between the form of the whole and the form of the part, then the thing with another is like it without; and if there is a distinction between them, and you have known in the investigations of individuation that there is no distinction and no multiplicity. His statement: (if there is no distinction etc.) in it is that wholeness and partness are by virtue of matter; so if the form is supposed isolated from matter, then there is no whole, no part, and no multiplicity in it; and it does not follow that the thing with another is like it without it, so reflect. His statement: (we suppose the whole whose form separates from it) what is meant by 'the whole' here is the composite of hyle and form; and his statement 'its form' is an addition of a part to the whole; and the meaning of the separation of the form from the whole is that the form remains without the whole, thus it is then abstracted from hyle; and his statement 'before and after division' is attached to his statement 'separates from it'; then the possibility of occurrence of the whole that you supposed is untenable regarding the identity of the form that is supposed to be abstracted from hyle; a proof is needed for its establishment.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument. It posits that if form could exist without matter, we could imagine a whole whose form separates from it before and after division. If there is no distinction between the form of the whole and the form of the part, then the thing with another is identical to itself without the other, which is absurd. If there is a distinction, then earlier investigations on individuation show that there is no distinction or multiplicity without matter. The gloss explains that wholeness and partness depend on matter; so if form is isolated, there is no whole or part, and the absurdity does not follow. The gloss also clarifies that the 'whole' refers to the composite of hyle and form, and the separation means the form remains without the whole, requiring proof.

Key Terms
كل (whole)
جزء (part)
تميز (distinction)
تعدد (multiplicity)
تعين (individuation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument proves that form cannot be separated at all, but it actually shows that without matter, the distinction between whole and part collapses.
Correction: The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming form without matter leads to an incoherent scenario where whole and part are indistinguishable.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument claim would happen if form were abstracted from matter?
A) Form would have multiple parts
B) There would be no distinction between whole and part
C) Form would become eternal
D) The body would cease to exist

Key Takeaway
The third argument demonstrates that without matter, the concepts of whole and part lose their meaning, thereby refuting the possibility of form existing independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Causal Independence of Hyle and Form
Sanusi argues that hyle is not an efficient cause of form, nor form a cause of hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that hyle and form are not causes of each other in the composition of body.

Core Idea
Hyle and form are not causes of each other; they are mutually dependent without circular causality.

In this passage, Sanusi examines whether hyle (matter) or form serves as the cause of the other in the composition of body. Drawing on principles from the investigation of quiddity, he argues that true composition requires a mutual need without circularity. He then presents three reasons why hyle cannot be an efficient cause of form, and one reason why form cannot be a cause of hyle, clarifying the nature of their mutual dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of arguments.
Notice the structure: first a general principle about composite quiddities, then specific arguments.
2
Identify the three arguments against hyle as cause
The passage gives three reasons: (1) hyle would precede form, (2) hyle would be both agent and recipient, (3) hyle would cause a specific form arbitrarily. Find each.
The glosses clarify that hyle is not truly simple.
3
Understand the argument against form as cause
Form inheres in hyle so it needs hyle; thus form cannot be a cause of hyle. Consider the implication and the gloss about need for agent vs. recipient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعها أي رابع تفريعات الهيولى وتركب الجسم منها ومن الصورة ( قد علمت ) في مباحث الماهية ( أنه لا بد ) في الماهية الحقيقية المركبة ( من احتياج أحد الجزأين إلى الآخر) فقط أو احتياج كل منهما إلى صاحبه على وجه لا يلزم منه دور، وحينئذ فلا بد بين جزأي الجسم من حاجة وأما كيفية تلك الحاجة ( فاعلم أن الهيولى ليست علة للصورة، وإلا لتم لها أي للهيولى ( وجود قبل وجود الصورة ) لأن العلة متقدمة بالوجود على معلولها لكنا قد بينا أن المادة لا تكون بالفعل إلا بسبب الصورة لأن الشيء الواحد لا يكون متصفاً بالقوة والفعل معاً، وقد عرفت فساده فلا نعيده (و) أيضا لو كانت الهيولى علة للصورة ( لاجتمع فيها ) أي في الهيولى (القبول والفعل بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد فإنها حينئذ فاعلة للصورة وقابلة لها وهو باطل، وجوابه أنه مبني على أن البسيط لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً علمت ما فيه (و) أيضاً لا يجوز أن تكون الهيولى علة للصورة ( لأنها ) في حد ذاتها تقبل صوراً لا نهاية لها فلا تكون علة (للمعينة أي لا تكون علة لمعينة من الصور حتى يكون حصولها في الهيولى أولى من حصول غيرها دفعاً للتحكم، بل ليس للمادة إلا مجرد القبول، وأما سبب حصول الصورة المعينة فيها فأمر آخر ( ولا الصورة) أي وليس الصورة أيضاً علة ( للهيولى لأنها حالة فيها فتحتاج) الصورة ( في وجودها إليها ويتجه على هذه العبارة أنه يلزم حينئذ كون الهيولى علة للصورة قوله : ( في الماهية الحقيقية أي المتصفة بالوحدة الحقيقية أي الوحدة في الخارج. قوله : (ليست علة ) أي علة فاعلية . قوله : ( مبني على أن البسيط إلخ ) مع أن الهيولي ليست بسيطاً حقيقياً. قوله : ( ويتجه على هذه العبارة إلخ فيه أن المثبت هاهنا الاحتياج إلى الفاعل والمنفي فيما سبق الاحتياج إلى القابل. قوله: (فلا تكون علة للمعينة لم لا يجوز أن يكون علة للمعينة لا لذاتها. بل بشرط خارج عن ذاتها منضم إليها فلا يلزم التحكم وأما عدم كونها قابلاً وفاعلاً فقد عرفت ما فيه قوله : ( ويتجه على هذه العبارة أنه يلزم حينئذ كون الهيولى علة للصورة) إذ المحتاج إليه لا بد أن يكون علة للمجتاج، فإن معنى العلة هاهنا هو ما يحتاج إلى الشيء في وجوده في نفسه لكن الصورة لم تكن محتاجة في وجودها في نفسها إلى المحل الذي هو الهيولى وإلا يلزم أن
The fourth of them, i.e. the fourth of the subdivisions of hyle and the composition of the body from it and from form: (you have known) in the investigations of quiddity that in a true composite quiddity there must be a need of one of the two parts for the other only, or a need of each for its companion in a way that does not entail a circle. Then there must be a need between the two parts of the body. As for the manner of that need, (know that hyle is not a cause of form, otherwise it would have existence before the existence of form, because the cause is prior in existence to its effect; but we have shown that matter is actual only by virtue of form, because one thing cannot be qualified by potency and act simultaneously, and you have known its falsity so we do not repeat it. And also if hyle were a cause of form, then acceptance and act would be combined in it, i.e. in hyle, with respect to one thing, for then it would be agent of form and recipient of it, which is false. Its answer is that it is based on the idea that the simple cannot be both recipient and agent together; you have known what is in it. And also it is not permissible for hyle to be a cause of form because it in itself receives infinite forms, so it cannot be a cause of a specific [form], i.e. it cannot be a cause of a specific form such that its occurrence in hyle is more fitting than another, to avoid arbitrariness; rather matter has only mere receptivity; as for the cause of the occurrence of the specific form in it, it is another matter. And neither is form a cause of hyle, because it inheres in it, so form needs it in its existence. And this expression implies that then hyle would be a cause of form. His statement: (in the true quiddity) i.e. qualified by true unity, i.e. unity in the external. His statement: (is not a cause) i.e. efficient cause. His statement: (built on the idea that the simple etc.) even though hyle is not truly simple. His statement: (and this expression implies etc.) in it is that what is being affirmed here is need for the agent, while what was negated earlier was need for the recipient. His statement: (so it cannot be a cause of a specific form) why may it not be a cause of a specific form not by itself, but with a condition external to it attached to it, so arbitrariness does not follow? As for it not being both recipient and agent, you have known what is in it. His statement: (and this expression implies that then hyle would be a cause of form) because that which is needed must be a cause of the needy, for the meaning of cause here is that which the thing needs in its existence in itself; but form is not in need in its own existence of the substrate which is hyle, otherwise it would be necessary that...
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth subdivision regarding the relationship between hyle and form. It first establishes that in a true composite, each part needs the other in a non-circular way. Then it argues that hyle is not an efficient cause of form because: (1) if hyle were a cause, it would have existence prior to form, but matter is actual only through form, and a thing cannot be both potential and actual. (2) If hyle were a cause, it would be both agent (giving form) and recipient (receiving form), which is impossible for a simple being. (3) Hyle cannot be a cause of a specific form because it receives infinite forms; specifying one would be arbitrary unless an external factor determines it. Similarly, form is not a cause of hyle because form inheres in hyle and needs it. The glosses clarify that hyle is not truly simple, and the need here is for an agent, not a recipient; also that form is not in need of a substrate in its own essence.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
هيولى (hyle)
صورة (form)
قبول (receptivity/acception)
فعل (act/agency)
بسيط (simple)
تحكم (arbitrariness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because hyle receives form, it causes form to exist.
Correction: Sanusi argues that reception is not agency; matter is merely receptive, and the cause of form is external.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason given that hyle cannot be a cause of form?
A) Hyle would then exist before form
B) Hyle would then be both agent and recipient
C) Hyle receives infinite forms
D) Form is eternal and uncreated

Key Takeaway
Neither hyle nor form causes the other; their relation is one of mutual need without circular causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that hyle cannot cause form because it is merely receptive. In your own life, are there areas where you pretend to be the cause of your own characteristics, when in reality you are only receiving them from an external source—your Creator? How would your daily habits change if you truly felt that your specific form is determined by Allah, not by yourself?
?
The lesson shows that form does not cause its substrate; it inheres in it. Are there attachments or behaviors that you think define you (your 'forms') that you actually 'cause' and control? What if the real cause is your need for the substrate—your human weakness and dependence? Will you let go of the illusion of self-causation and turn to the One who truly gives form to your life?

6 min
Proof that Form Is Not the Cause of Matter
Al-Jurjani presents three arguments that form is not a cause of matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational arguments proving that form (sura) is not the efficient cause of matter (hayula) and the objection that the argument only disproves a specific form.

Core Idea
Form depends on matter, not the other way around.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents three arguments to show that form cannot be the efficient cause of matter. The first argument is revised for precision, the second relies on the priority of matter, and the third uses the persistence of matter through form change. The passage then raises an objection that the third argument only targets specific forms, not form in general.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to get the overall flow.
Note the three 'وأيضا' that introduce each argument.
2
Identify the three arguments
Find each argument: (1) form is inherent, needing matter, (2) form is posterior to matter because it requires finitude and shape, (3) matter persists when form changes.
The first argument is refined to avoid implying matter causes form.
3
Analyze the third argument and objection
See how the third argument uses the persistence of matter to disprove form as cause, and then note the objection that it only disproves a specific form.
The passage cuts off before the full answer to the objection is given.
4
Reflect on the incomplete resolution
Consider what the full answer might be: perhaps form in general cannot be a cause because matter is independent of any particular form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا الصورة أي وليس الصورة أيضاً علة للهيولى لأنها حالة فيها فتحتاج الصورة في وجودها إليها ويتجه على هذه العبارة أنه يلزم حينئذ كون الهيولى علة للصورة. فالأولى أن يقال: فلا تكون علة لوجود محلها. وأيضا ليست الصورة علة للهيولى لأنها لا توجد إلا مع التناهي والتشكل لما مر، والهيولي متقدمة عليهما لأنهما من توابع المادة المتأخرة عنها، وما مع المتأخر متأخر كما أن ما مع المتقدم متقدم، فتكون الصورة متأخرة عن الهيولى فلا تكون علة لها. ولا يخفى عليك أن الحكم بتأخر ما مع المتأخر إنما تظهر صحته في المعية والتأخر الزمانيين دون غيرهما. وأيضا ليست الصورة علة للمادة للزوم انتفائها عند عدم الصورة المعينة، يعني لو كانت الصورة علة لها لانتفت عند انتفاء الصورة المعينة لوجوب انتفاء المعلول عند انتفاء علته لكن الصورة الجسمية تتبدل وتزول عند ورود الانفصال والهيولى باقية على حالها. فإن قيل: ما ذكرتم إنما يدل على أن الصورة المعينة ليست علة لها ولا يلزم من عدم علية الصورة المعينة عدم علية الصورة مطلقا.
And not the form—i.e., form is also not a cause for matter, because it is inherent in it, so form needs matter for its existence, and this statement implies that then matter would be a cause for form. So it is better to say: it is not a cause for the existence of its locus. Also, form is not a cause for matter because it does not exist except with finitude and shape, as passed, and matter is prior to them because they are accidents of matter posterior to it, and that which is with the posterior is posterior, just as that which is with the prior is prior, so form is posterior to matter and thus is not a cause for it. And it is not hidden from you that the judgment that what is with the posterior is posterior only appears correct in temporal concomitance and posteriority, not in others. Also, form is not a cause for matter because of the necessary non-existence of matter when a specific form is absent, meaning that if form were a cause for it, matter would cease to exist when that specific form ceased, due to the necessity of the non-existence of the effect when its cause is absent, but the corporeal form changes and disappears upon the occurrence of separation while matter remains as it was. For if it is said: What you have mentioned only indicates that the specific form is not a cause for it, and the lack of causality of the specific form does not imply the lack of causality of form absolutely.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments that form (sura) is not the efficient cause of matter (hayula). The first argument (revised): form is inherent in matter, so it depends on matter for its existence, making matter a cause of form rather than the reverse. The second argument: form only exists with finitude and shape, which are accidents posterior to matter, so form is posterior to matter and cannot be its cause. The third argument: if form were the cause of matter, matter would cease when any specific form disappears, but matter persists through changes of form. The passage then raises an objection: the argument only disproves that a specific form is the cause, not that form in general could be a cause. The text cuts off before providing the answer.

تناهي

انتفاء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think form is a cause of matter because form actualizes matter.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that form depends on matter for its existence, that form is posterior to matter, and that matter persists even when form changes.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument use the persistence of matter during form change?
A) To show form is eternal.
B) To show matter cannot exist without form.
C) To show form cannot be the cause of matter because matter survives form change.
D) To show matter is created.

Key Takeaway
Form is not the efficient cause of matter because it depends on matter, is posterior to it, and matter persists through changes of form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you find yourself prioritizing the 'form' of your actions—how things appear—over the 'matter' of your intentions and inner state? How does this lesson challenge you to reverse that priority?
?
Consider a deeply held belief or practice: have you ever mistaken a secondary aspect (form) for its core essence (matter)? What would it mean for you to acknowledge that the core is what persists and the secondary aspect is dependent on it?
?
When you face a change or loss of some 'form' in your life (a role, status, appearance), do you feel your identity is shaken? How does the truth that matter remains while forms come and go urge you to anchor your identity in what is more enduring?

Proving the Substantial Form and al-Razi's Objection
Proof for substantial form with objection and rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the existence of substantial form in bodies and the key objection raised by Imam al-Razi, along with the rebuttal based on the distinction between constitutive form and accidental powers.

Core Idea
Substantial form is the specific, constitutive principle that makes each body what it is, not mere accidents.

In this passage, the author presents a foundational argument in Islamic metaphysics: bodies display diverse effects and properties, which cannot arise from their shared matter (hyle) or from a separate intellect. Therefore, each body must possess a specific, inherent principle—its substantial form. Imam al-Razi objects that the observed differences are due to accidental powers, not constitutive forms. The subsequent commentary provides a robust rebuttal, clarifying the logical necessity of a constitutive principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the full translation. Note how the passage moves from premises to conclusion to objection to rebuttal.
The text includes both the main argument and glosses (قوله). Focus on the main flow first.
2
Identify the premises for substantial form
The argument begins by ruling out hyle and separate intellects as causes of specific differences. Locate these two premises and understand why they are insufficient.
Hyle is receptive and common; separate intellect relates uniformly.
3
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi agrees that there are specific powers in bodies, but he denies they are constitutive forms. Instead, he holds they are accidental. Read his objection carefully.
He also claims the infinite regress argument applies to forms as well.
4
Examine the rebuttal in the commentary
The commentary responds by explaining that substantial form functions like a differentia that specifies the genus, requiring a specifier before multiplicity, unlike accidents.
The commentary also questions the assumption about separate intellects.
5
Reflect on the overall debate
Consider why this distinction between constitutive form and accidental powers matters for the metaphysics of bodies. How does it relate to the broader argument for hylomorphism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنها قابلة فلا تكون فاعلة وأيضاً هيولى العناصر مشتركة فلا تكون مبدأ لأمور مختلفة، ولا للمفارق لأن نسبته إلى الأجسام كلها على السوية ( بل) لا بد أن يكون ذلك لأمر مختص) أي ثابت لبعض من الأجسام دون بعض ويجب أن يكون ذلك ) الأمر المختص لازماً ليمكن استناد ما هو لازم إليه ) فإن كان ذلك الأمر المختص اللازم ( مقوماً للجسم فهو المطلوب ) إذ لا بد حينئذ من أن يكون جوهراً، فقد ثبت في الأجسام جواهر مختصة هي مباد لآثارها ولوازمها المختلفة ولا معنى للصورة النوعية إلا ذلك ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن مقوماً للجسم بل كان خارجاً لازماً (عاد الكلام فيه ) لاحتياجه حينئذ إلى أمر آخر مختص يستند هو إليه، ( ويتسلسل قال الإمام الرازي : ( الذي حصل لنا بالدليل هو أن هذه اللوازم من الكيفيات والأيون وغيرهما مستندة إلى قوى موجودة في الأجسام، وأما أن تلك القوى أسباب لوجود الجسمية حتى تكون صوراً مقومة فلا بل الأقرب ( الظاهر) عندنا (أنها من قبيل ( الأعراض ) وما ذكروه من لزوم التسلسل وارد عليهم في الصور فإن اختصاص الأجسام
قوله: (مشتركة) بدليل الكون والفساد.
قوله : ( ولا للمفارق فيه بحث مشهور بقي هاهنا احتمال آخر وهو أن تكون الصورة الجسمية بشرط حلولها في هيولى كل فلك علة، فلا تثبت الكلية.
قوله : (إذ لا بد إلخ امتناع تقوم الجوهر بالعرض القائم به ضرورة لأنه يلزم تقدم العرض وتأخره وكذا كونه جزءاً محمولاً عليه، وأما تقومه بالعرض القائم بجزئه فجوزه البعض متمسكين بأن السرير مركب من الخشب والهيئة السريرية والحق امتناعه لأن المركب من المقولتين ليس داخلا في شيء من المقولتين، لأنه باعتبار جزء موجود لا في الموضوع وباعتبار جزء آخر موجود في الموضوع ولا ترجيح لاعتبار حكم أحد الجزأين دون الآخر له في نفسه. وما قيل من أن صدق تعريف الجوهر على السرير بمعنى المجموع فوهم لأن صدق السرير بمعنى معروض الهيئة السريرية كما أن الجسم بمعنى حمل الأعراض القائمة جوهر لا المجموع المركب بينهما، وبما ذكرنا ظهر جوهرية الصورة النوعية وإن أشكل على الفحول .
قوله : ( فإن اختصاص إلخ لا وجه لهذا الكلام لأن نسبة الصورة النوعية إلى الجسم كنسبة الفصول إلى الجنس فالصورة النوعية إذا حلت في الجسم تخصص الجسم وصار كل حصة مختصة بصور معينة وقبل حلولها يتعدد فيحتاج إلى المخصص بخلاف الأعراض فإنها عارضة للأجسام بعد تكثرها في الخارج، فلا بد من المخصص.
قوله: (ولا) للمفارق لأن نسبته إلى الأجسام كلها على (السوية فيه منع، لم لا يجوز أن يكون هناك أمور مخالفة مختلفة الماهيات ويكون لكل منها نسبة مخصوصة إلى جسم مخصوص ؟ فعلى هذا لا يتصور الاختلاف في تلك اللوازم .
Because it (hyle) is receptive, it cannot be an agent; also the hyle of the elements is common, so it cannot be a principle for different things; nor the separate [intellect], because its relation to all bodies is uniform. Rather, it must be for a specific matter (amr mukhtaṣṣ) — i.e., established for some bodies and not others. And that specific matter must be a necessary concomitant (lāzim) so that what is necessary can be attributed to it. If that specific necessary matter is constitutive (muqawwim) of the body, then it is what is sought, for then it must be a substance (jawhar). So it has been established that in bodies there are specific substances that are principles for their different effects and concomitants, and the meaning of substantial form (al-ṣūra al-nawʿiyya) is nothing other than that. Otherwise, if it is not constitutive but an external concomitant, the discussion returns to it, for then it needs another specific matter to which it is attributed, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Imam al-Rāzī said: 'What has been proven to us by evidence is that these concomitants—qualities, accidents, and others—are attributed to powers (quwā) existing in bodies. As for these powers being causes for the existence of corporeality such that they are constitutive forms, no. Rather, the closer (apparent) opinion to us is that they are of the category of accidents (aʿrāḍ). What they mentioned of the necessity of infinite regress applies to them regarding forms, for the specification of bodies...' His words: '(common) by evidence of generation and corruption.' His words: '(nor for the separate) there is a well-known investigation; another possibility remains here: that the corporeal form, conditioned by its inherence in the hyle of each sphere, is a cause, so universality is not established.' His words: '(since it is necessary etc.) the impossibility of a substance subsisting by an accident inherent in it is necessary, because it would require the accident to be prior and posterior, and likewise its being a part predicated upon it. As for its subsistence by an accident inherent in a part of it, some permitted it, holding that the bed is composed of wood and the bed-form. But the truth is its impossibility, because the composite of the two categories is not included in either of the two categories, since it is considered with respect to a part existing not in a subject and with respect to another part existing in a subject, and there is no preference for the judgment of one of the two parts over the other for it in itself. And what was said that the truth of the definition of substance (jawhar) on the bed in the sense of the sum is an error, because the truth of the bed in the sense of the substrate of the bed-form is like the body in the sense of the bearer of accidents that subsist in a substance, not the composite of the two. By what we have mentioned, the substantiality (jawhariyya) of the substantial form is apparent, even if it is difficult for the masters.' His words: '(for the specification etc.) there is no basis for this statement, because the relation of the substantial form to the body is like the relation of differentiae to genus. So when the substantial form inheres in the body, it specifies the body, and each portion becomes specified with particular forms, and before its inherence it is multiple, thus needing a specifier, unlike accidents, which are accidental to bodies after their multiplicity externally, so a specifier is necessary.' His words: '(and nor) for the separate because its relation to all bodies is uniform' contains a rejection: Why is it not permissible that there are different things with different essences, each having a specific relation to a specific body? On this, the difference in those concomitants is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a definitional proof and objection. It begins by presenting the argument that hyle (prime matter) and separate intellects cannot be the cause of the specific differences in bodies; therefore, there must be a specific inherent principle—the substantial form (al-ṣūra al-nawʿiyya)—that constitutes the body as a substance. The argument proceeds: either this principle is constitutive (muqawwim), which directly proves the substantial form, or it is a mere concomitant, leading to an infinite regress. Next, Imam al-Rāzī objects: what we actually observe are powers (quwā) that are accidental, not constitutive forms; the argument's infinite regress applies equally to forms if assumed. Then the commentary (starting with 'قوله:') rebuts al-Rāzī: The substantial form functions like a differentia (faṣl) specifying a genus, requiring a specifier before inherence, whereas accidents occur after multiplicity. The final comment questions the assumption that separate intellects relate uniformly to all bodies, leaving room for specific relations.

Key Terms
هيولى (hyle)
صورة نوعية (substantial form)
قوى (powers)
أعراض (accidents)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
جوهر (substance)
مقوم (constitutive)
خاصة (specific)
فاعل (agent)
مفارق (separate intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is for atoms or indivisible parts, not substantial form.
Correction: The argument specifically establishes a constitutive substance inherent in bodies, which is the substantial form, not discrete atoms.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why can hyle (prime matter) and separate intellects not be the cause of specific differences in bodies?
A) Because hyle is receptive and common, and the separate intellect relates uniformly to all bodies.
B) Because hyle is active and distinct, and the separate intellect is material.
C) Because hyle is eternal and separate intellects are multiple.
D) Because hyle is an accident and separate intellects are substances.

Key Takeaway
The existence of substantial form is logically necessary to explain specific differences in bodies, as neither prime matter nor separate intellects can serve as the principle of individuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see the vast diversity in creation—plants, animals, humans—each with a unique nature. Do you truly believe that these natures are not random or accidental, but are constituted by a deliberate, specific form placed by the Creator? How does this conviction change the way you view the world and respect its order? Will you pause today to reflect on the wisdom behind the distinctive form of a single thing, and thank Allah for His precise design?
?
The proof argues that without a constitutive principle, we face an infinite regress. Is there any area of your own life where you attribute your actions or character to mere external circumstances or passing moods—accidental 'powers'—rather than to a stable, constitutive identity? How would anchoring your sense of self in something substantive and God-given change your choices? Are you willing to examine your assumptions today?
?
Al-Razi's objection attributes effects to accidental powers, but the rebuttal insists that forms are necessary to avoid regress. Think of your own convictions about right and wrong: do you treat them as accidental preferences or as constitutive principles that define who you are? If they are merely accidental, what happens when circumstances change? Will you commit to grounding your moral framework in something essential and unshakeable, like the fitra Allah placed in you?

Refuting Arguments from Infinite Regress and Intuition for Substantial Form
The author refutes two arguments for substantial form: infinite regress and intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments for substantial form based on the specificity of celestial spheres and the claim of intuitive knowledge, and how both are refuted by showing the permissibility of infinite regress of accidents and the limitation of intuitive knowledge to accidental differences.

Core Idea
Intuition only reveals accidental differences, not essential ones.

In the ongoing debate about substantial forms, proponents attempt to prove them through the unique hyle of celestial spheres and the chain of forms in elements, arguing that infinite regress of forms is acceptable but not of accidents. They also claim we intuitively know that fire and water have different realities. The author dismantles both arguments, showing that infinite regress applies equally to accidents, and that what we intuitively know are the qualities, not the essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument: celestial spheres and elements
Examine how proponents claim that the hyle of celestial spheres are distinct and that elements have a chain of forms leading to infinite regress, which they accept.
Notice they argue that infinite regress of forms is not impossible, but they want to stop it for accidents.
2
Understand the counter-argument
The author points out that if infinite regress of forms is permissible, then it is also permissible for accidents, so there is no need to posit substantial forms.
This is a symmetric argument.
3
Read the intuitive knowledge claim
Proponents claim we know intuitively that the reality of fire differs from water, so the difference must be substantial.
4
Analyze the rebuttal of intuition
The author argues that intuition only tells us about qualities, not essences. The claim of essential difference is the very point in dispute.
The commentary reinforces that mutakallimun deny such intuitive knowledge.

Source Text

( قلنا : ( إِن سلم أن في الجسم أمرا هو مبدأ للكيفية فلا يجديكم ( ومن أين يلزم كونه من مقومات الجسم حتى يكون صورة نوعية ؟ على أنا لا نسلم ذلك، (و) نقول : (لم قلتم : إنه ) أي عود الماء إلى البرودة ( ليس بفعل الفاعل المختار) على طريقة جري العادة (وهذا الفرع الخامس أعني ثبوت الصورة النوعية (مع ضعفه لعدم صحة أدلته ( أصل ) كبير له فروع كثيرة ) من المباحث الفلكية ) والعنصرية ( فتحققه ولا تنس كيلا تحتاج إلى التنبيه على ضعف ما يتفرع عليه من تلك المباحث . قال الإمام الرازي: لما فرغنا من بيان ذاتيات الجسم ومقوماته فلنذكر أحكامه ثم شرع في إثبات الحيز الطبيعي إلا أن المصنف جعله من تفاريع الهيولى،

There must be other specific forms. And they answered that by [saying] that the hyle of the celestial spheres are different in essence (māhiyya), and each one of them accepts only a particular form. As for the elements' specification with their forms, it is because matter before this form was characterized by another form, for which it became prepared to accept the subsequent form, and so on to infinity. Then we say to them: Since the succession of forms without end is not impossible, why is the succession of accidents without end impossible? Rather, this too is permissible, so there is no need to prove the substantial form in the elements for that reason, nor in the celestial spheres, because their matters accept only what is accidental to them. And some of them answered that by [saying] we know by intuition (bi-dīhā) that the reality of fire is different from the reality of water, so they must differ by a specific substantial thing. And perhaps one may deduce [proof] for the substantial form from [the fact] that when water is heated and then left, it naturally returns to cold; so there is something that is a principle for the quality (kayfiyya) that remains and returns the water to the vanishing quality after the removal of the compeller. We said: If it is granted that in the body there is something that is a principle for the quality, it does not benefit you. And whence does it necessarily follow that it is among the constitutives of the body such that it is a substantial form? Moreover, we do not grant that. And we say: Why do you say that the return of water to coldness is not by the act of the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) according to the way of customary occurrence? And this fifth branch—that is, the establishment of the substantial form—despite its weakness due to the unsoundness of its proofs, is a great root (aṣl) with many branches in celestial and elemental investigations. So verify it, and do not forget, so that you do not need to be alerted to the weakness of what branches off from it from those investigations. Imam al-Rāzī said: When we have finished explaining the essentials (dhātiyyāt) of the body and its constitutives, let us mention its properties; then he proceeded to prove the natural place (al-ḥayyiz al-ṭabīʿī), except that the author made it a branch of hyle. His words: 'We know by intuition'—the claim of intuition in the subject of dispute is not admissible. How? The theologians (mutakallimūn) have held that bodies are similar due to the similarity of the indivisible atoms (jawāhir farda), not differing by accidents. His words: 'by a substantial thing'—based on what has passed of the impossibility of the substance subsisting by accident, and you have known what is in it. His words: 'it does not benefit you'—an indication that the conjoined object is omitted, implied by the conjunction. His words: 'He made it a branch of hyle'—either by way of predominance (taghlīb) or considering that the establishment of the place... His words: 'And some of them answered that'—i.e., he answered by changing the proof. And his words: 'We know by intuition that the reality of fire is different from the reality of water' here is a rejection, for what we know by intuition is that the qualities of fire are different from the qualities of water. As for the difference between their realities such that each of them has in itself a determinant for essential distinction from the other, that is still the subject of dispute.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a series of responses and counter-arguments in the debate over substantial form. It begins by addressing the objection from infinite regress: proponents argue that celestial spheres have specific hyle, and the elements have a chain of forms, which the author refutes by pointing out that an infinite regress of accidents is equally permissible, thus the argument does not necessitate substantial forms. Then, an objection from intuitive knowledge (fire vs. water) is raised, but the author dismisses it as a claim not admitted in dispute. Next, a proof from the behavior of water (heating and returning to cold) is presented, which the author counters: even if there is a source of quality, it does not prove it is constitutive; also, it could be merely the act of the free agent by habit. The passage concludes that the proof for substantial form is weak, though it is a major root with many branches. The final comments (قوله:) critique the intuitive knowledge claim, arguing that only qualities are intuitively distinct, not essences.

Key Terms
هيولى (hyle)
صورة نوعية (substantial form)
أعراض (accidents)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
بديهة (intuition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the claim of intuitive knowledge of different realities is a strong proof for substantial form.
Correction: The author shows that intuition only tells about qualities, not about essences; the real dispute is about whether bodies are essentially similar or different.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the argument that infinite regress of forms is acceptable but not of accidents?
A) By denying infinite regress altogether.
B) By saying that if forms can regress infinitely, then accidents can too.
C) By proving that accidents cannot regress infinitely.
D) By introducing a new type of form.

Key Takeaway
The two arguments for substantial form—from infinite regress and intuition—are refuted by symmetry and by limiting intuition to accidents rather than essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perceive differences between things—like fire and water—do you automatically assume their essences differ? How might this assumption lead you to attribute independent powers to created things, forgetting that all differences are merely accidental and that Allah alone is the true agent?
?
In your own life, have you ever relied on 'intuition' to judge a person's character or a situation's reality, only to find later that you were only seeing surface accidents? What would it take for you to suspend intuition and seek deeper knowledge from revelation and sound reasoning?
?
The author refutes the idea that infinite regress of forms necessitates a substantial form. In your faith, do you ever feel pressured to accept a theological position simply because it seems intuitive or because 'everyone knows it'? How can you ensure your beliefs are grounded in evidence rather than convenience or cultural intuition?

Refuting the Proof from Water's Return to Cold
The author presents and refutes the proof for substantial form from water's return to cold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof for substantial form based on water's natural return to cold after heating, and the author's refutation that the source of quality could be accidental or due to divine action.

Core Idea
Water's return to cold does not prove a substantial form; it could be an accident or divine habit.

A common proof for substantial form is the observation that heated water naturally returns to coldness when left, implying a persisting principle that brings back the quality. The author counters this by arguing that even if such a principle exists, it need not be constitutive of the body; it could be an accident. Moreover, the return could simply be the act of the free agent (God) acting by custom. The lesson concludes by noting that despite its weakness, the issue of substantial form is a major root in natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the water argument
The proof: when water is heated and left, it naturally returns to cold. This suggests there is something (a principle) that remains and restores the cold quality after the external compeller is removed.
2
Analyze the first response
The author says: even if we grant that there is a principle for the quality, it does not benefit you, because it does not prove that this principle is a constituent of the body (i.e., a substantial form). It could be an accident.
The key is that the principle might be accidental, not essential.
3
Analyze the second response
Alternatively, the return to cold could be directly caused by God the free agent acting according to His custom, not by an inherent form.
This invokes occasionalist causality.
4
Understand the concluding remarks
The author states that despite the weakness of the proof, the doctrine of substantial form is a major root with many branches in celestial and elemental sciences. He advises the reader to verify it and not forget.

This passage is a series of responses and counter-arguments in the debate over substantial form.
