The Natural Place of Bodies: Claim and Discussion
Al-Jurjani discusses the meaning of natural place for body and different views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'natural place' for bodies and how different theories of place (surface, dimension, arrangement) affect the claim that every body has a natural place.

Core Idea
Natural place is defined by what a body's nature requires, not just any location.

Al-Jurjani examines the claim that every body has a natural place, a key notion in proving prime matter. He contrasts this with the atomist view and explores how different definitions of 'place' impact the claim. This passage clarifies the precise meaning of natural place and its dependence on an inherent nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and its context
Read the opening statement: 'Every body has a natural place that its nature requires its occurrence in it.' Understand that this is part of proving prime matter (hayula).
The claim is attributed to those who affirm natures in bodies.
2
Understand the atomist rejection
Notice that atomists (partisans of the atom) reject the claim because they hold bodies are similar and differences come from the free agent, not nature.
This rejection shows the dependence of the claim on the existence of inherent natures.
3
Examine different theories of place
See how the claim is discussed under different views: surface theorists, dimension theorists, and the correct view from Al-Shifa (place as location or arrangement).
Al-Jurjani critiques each view and cites Ibn Sina's Al-Shifa.
4
Grasp the meaning of 'its nature requires'
Read the explanation: natural place is defined by what nature necessitates, under conditions, so a body cannot have two natural places.
This addresses objections about surface-place being accidental.
5
Absorb the conclusive quote from Al-Isharat
Consider Ibn Sina's point: if a body is left to its nature with no external influence, it must have a specific position and shape, showing nature's principle of necessitation.
This reinforces the idea of natural place as an inherent tendency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : إثبات الهيولى في الجسم
فقال : ( سادسها كل جسم له حيز طبيعي تقتضي طبيعته حصوله فيه ( ضرورة أنه لو الطبيعي يتوقف على ثبوت الطبيعة الحالة في الهيولى، فإن أصحاب الجزء يقولون بتماثل الأجسام فلا طبيعة ولا اقتضاء واختصاص الأجسام بالآثار بإرادة الفاعل المختار .
قوله : (كل جسم له حيز (طبيعي هذه المسألة لا تصح عند القائلين بالجزء سواء كان موجوداً أو موهوماً، إذ لا اختلاف فيه حتى يقول أن بعضه طبيعي وبعضه غير طبيعي، قال الشارح في بحث المكان: إنه قد استدل بعضهم على امتناع كون لكان بعداً مجرداً باستلزامه أن لا يسكن جسم في حيز ولا يتحرك عنه وأجيب بأن اختصاص الأجسام بأحيازها لما بينها من الملاءمة والمنافرة، وبما ذكرنا ظهر عدم صحة ما في التجريد بعد ما اختار أن المكان مكاناً طبيعياً. وأما عند القائلين بالسطح، فلا يصدق كلية إذ قيل بترادف من أن لكل جسم الحيز عن هو البعد المكان فقيل بعموم كما مر في بحث المكان من أن الحيز ما به تمايز الأجسام في الإشارة الحسية، وهو أعم من المكان متناول للوضع الذي به يمتاز المحدد عن غيره في الإشارة الحسية فهو متحيز وليس في المكان ولا بعد في أن يكون الحالة التي تميزه في الإشارة الحسية عن غيره طبيعية له، وإن لم يكن شيء من أوضاعه يشبه بالقياس إلى ما تحته أمراً طبيعياً. وفيه بحث لأن الحيز ينسب إلى الجسم بكلمة فيه، ويصح الانتقال منه . ويدل على ما ذكروا من أن الجسم لا يجوز أن يكون له حیزان ،طبیعیان فلا يمكن إدخال الوضع بهذا المعنى في الحيز. والصواب ما في الشفاء أن من الحيز إما مكان أو وضع ترتيب الأجسام بعضها مع بعض والعين والمشترك للجزئين وضع الترتيب بأن يشار إلى الجسم بأنه هناك وهنا سواء كان سطحاً أو وضعاً حاصلاً بالترتيب، وإليه يرشد الدليل المذكور عليه .
نفسه
مع
قوله: (تقتضي طبيعته حصوله (فيه يعني أن المراد بالحيز الطبيعي ما تقتضي الطبيعة حصوله فيه ولذا لا يجوز أن يكون لجسم واحد حيزان طبيعيان وفيه إشارة إلى رد ما في شرح أنا لا نعني بالحيز الطبيعي إلا ما يكون حاصلاً للجسم في المقاصد وحكمة العين من قطع النظر عما سواه وإلى دفع ما أورده بعض من أن المكان بمعنى السطح كيف يكون طبيعياً وهو حاصل له بسبب الحاوي، وليس حاصلاً للجسم إذا خلي وطبعه لأن اللازم في المكان الطبيعي أن يكون الجسم بطبيعته مقتضياً للحصول فيه وإن كان الحصول متوقفاً على شرط وارتفاع مانع وفي الإشارات أن الجسم إذا خلي وطباعه ولم يعرض له من خارج تأثير غريب لم يكن له بد من وضع معين وشكل معين فإذن في طباعه مبدأ استيجاب ذلك وفي شرحه، وإنما قال : مبدأ استيجاب ذلك ولم يقل : مبدأ ذلك أو مبدأ وجوب ذلك، لأن الحصول في الموضع المعين والشكل المعين، وربما يلزمهما القسر كما ذكرنا، لكن الجسم يكون بحيث يعود إلى ما اقتضته طباعه عند زوال القسر إلخ .
The First Observatory - The Eighth Objective: Proving Prime Matter in Body. He said: '(The sixth:) Every body has a natural place that its nature requires its occurrence in it' necessarily because the natural [place] depends on the establishment of the nature subsisting in prime matter. For the partisans of the atom say that bodies are similar, so there is no nature, no requirement, and the specification of bodies with effects is by the will of the free agent. His words: 'Every body has a natural place' – this issue is not valid according to those who affirm the atom, whether it is existent or imaginary, since there is no difference in it such that one could say some is natural and some is not natural. The commentator said in the discussion of place: Some have argued for the impossibility of place being a pure dimension because it would entail that no body rests in a place nor moves from it. And it was answered that bodies are specified to their places due to the compatibility and incompatibility between them. By what we have mentioned, the incorrectness of what is in Al-Tajrid after he chose that place is natural place has become apparent. As for those who affirm the surface, the universal does not hold true, for it was said that there is synonymy [between them], from that every body has a place which is the dimension, so it was said that it is general, as has passed in the discussion of place, that place is that by which bodies are distinguished in sensory indication, and it is more general than place, encompassing the position by which the limited is distinguished from others in sensory indication. So it is located and is not in place, and there is no remoteness in that the state that distinguishes it in sensory indication from others is natural to it, even if none of its positions resemble by analogy to what is under it a natural thing. There is investigation in this because place is attributed to the body with the word 'in', and it is valid to be transferred from it. And it indicates what they mentioned that a body cannot have two natural places, so it is impossible to include position in this sense in [the definition of] place. And the correct [view] is what is in Al-Shifa: that place is either location or the arrangement of bodies relative to each other, and the common thing for the two sides is the arrangement, by pointing to the body that it is there or here, whether it is a surface or a position resulting from arrangement. And the aforementioned proof guides to it. His words: 'Its nature requires its occurrence in it' means that what is meant by natural place is what nature requires its occurrence in, and therefore it is not permissible for one body to have two natural places. And in it there is an indication of refuting what is in the commentary 'We do not mean by natural place except what accrues to the body in the purposes and the wisdom of the eye, disregarding what is other than it', and to repelling what some have objected: that place in the sense of surface, how can it be natural while it is obtained for it by means of the container, and not obtained for the body when left to itself and its nature? Because the requirement in natural place is that the body by its nature be necessitating occurrence in it, even if occurrence depends on a condition and removal of an obstacle. And in Al-Isharat: The body, if left to itself and its nature and no extraneous influence from outside affects it, must have a specific position and specific shape. So in its nature there is a principle of necessitating that. And in its commentary: He only said 'a principle of necessitating that' and did not say 'a principle of that' or 'a principle of obligation of that', because occurrence in the specific place and specific shape, perhaps coercion necessitates them as we mentioned, but the body is such that it returns to what its nature required when coercion ceases, etc.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses the concept of 'natural place' (al-hayz al-tabi'i) for a body, which is central to proving prime matter (hayula). The author begins by stating the claim: every body has a natural place that its nature requires. He notes that this claim is rejected by those who affirm atoms (juz'), because they hold that bodies are similar and have no inherent nature; all differences are due to the will of the free agent. The commentary then examines the claim under different theories of place. For those who hold that place is a surface, the universal claim is not true because they consider place and location to be synonymous, so the limited (al-muhaddid) is located but not in place. The author corrects this by citing Al-Shifa: place is either location (makan) or arrangement (wad'). The common element is that a body can be pointed to as 'there' or 'here'. He then explains the phrase 'its nature requires its occurrence in it': natural place is defined by what nature requires, so a body cannot have two natural places. He also addresses an objection that surface-place cannot be natural because it depends on a container. The reply is that natural place requires that the body by nature necessitates being there, even if external conditions are needed. The passage ends with a quote from Ibn Sina's Al-Isharat: if a body is left to its nature with no external influence, it must have a specific position and shape, showing that its nature has a principle of necessitating that.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural place is any place a body happens to be.
Correction: Natural place is specifically the place that the body's nature requires, not any accidental location.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view of place according to Al-Shifa?
A) Place is only a surface.
B) Place is either location or arrangement.
C) Place is the same as dimension.
D) Place is the will of the free agent.

Key Takeaway
Every body has a natural place that its nature requires, but this does not mean any place; it is defined by the body's inherent tendencies, and different theories of place interpret 'place' differently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When external pressures (society, desires, fears) push you away from what your fitrah (innate nature) requires, do you have a 'principle of necessitating' that draws you back? What is that principle in your own life, and is it active or dormant?
?
Your soul has a natural place—a state of closeness to Allah and submission to His will. Are you currently in that place, or are you in a place of coercion and alienation? What is the 'extraneous influence' that keeps you from returning, and when will you let it cease so you can come back?
?
The body returns to its natural place when coercion ends. When the last excuses and distractions are stripped away, and you stand before Allah alone, where will your nature lead you? What are you doing today to ensure that your nature—not external forces—is the one directing your course?

The Proof from Natural Place
Al-Jurjani presents the proof from natural place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a body left to its nature must have a specific place, and why this leads to positing an internal nature and ultimately prime matter.

Core Idea
A body left alone cannot be without a specific place; that place must come from its own nature.

Al-Jurjani presents a proof from natural place, claiming that if a body is isolated from all external influences, it must still occupy a specific location. This is used to argue for an internal nature and ultimately for prime matter. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage that presents the proof. Notice how it sets up the scenario of a body left to itself and its nature.
The text uses 'khalī' (left alone) and emphasizes 'nature' as an internal cause.
2
Identify the premises
The proof has three key premises: (1) a body must be in some place, not all places; (2) that place cannot come from external factors, common corporeality, or prime matter; (3) therefore it must come from an internal specific thing called nature.
The argument moves from necessary place to necessary nature.
3
Understand the role of nature
Nature is posited as the internal principle that determines the specific place. Without it, the body would be indeterminate, which is impossible according to the argument.
This nature is later linked to prime matter (hayula) in the broader context.
4
Connect to prime matter
Although not explicit in this passage, the proof is part of an argument for prime matter. Ask yourself: why would this proof be used to support prime matter?
The commentary hints that nature requires a substrate (prime matter) to subsist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : إثبات الهيولى في الجسم
خلي ) الجسم ( وطبعه ) أي فرض بعد وجوده خالياً عن جميع ما يمكن خلوه عنه من التأثيرات الغريبة ) لكان له مكان ضرورة ) إذ لا يمكن جسم لا في مكان ولا يتصور حصوله في جميع الأمكنة معاً، بل لا بد أن يحصل في حيز معين، ولا يكون حصوله في ذلك الحيز مستنداً إلى أمر خارج، إذ المفروض خلوه عنه ولا إلى الجسمية المشتركة لأن نسبتها إلى الأحياز كلها على السوية ولا إلى الهيولى لأنها تابعة للجسمية في اقتضاء حيز ما على الإطلاق، بل إلى أمر آخر داخل فيه مختص به، وهو المراد بالطبيعة (قلنا :) ما ذكرتم (ممنوع، بل لو خُلّي) الجسم وطبعه (لكان كالمحدد لا مكان له كما هو مذهب أرسطو ومن تابعه ( أو ) نقول : إذا خلي وطبعه ( تكون نسبته إلى الأحياز ( كلها ( سواء حتى يخصصه الفاعل ) المختار) بحيز معين ولا نسلم إمكان خلوه في نفس الأمر عن تأثير المختار وتخصيصه، (و) نقول: (لو
 قوله : ( أي فرض بعد وجوده ) إشارة إلى أن الحيز من لوازم الوجود لا الماهية فالفاعل معتبر من حيث أنه موجد له . قوله : ( عن جميع ما يمكن خلوه منه وهو ما سوى لازم ماهيته والفاعل من حيث هو موجد له فلا يرد ما قيل : إن أريد التخلية من الفاعل أيضاً، فالجسم حينئذ لا يكون موجوداً فضلاً عن اقتضاء الحيز، وإن أريد التخلية من الفاعل أيضاً، فالجسم حينئذ لا يكون موجوداً فضلاً عن اقتضاء الحيز، وإن أريد التخلية مما سوى الفاعل فليجز أن يكون المخصص هو الفاعل لأن المفروض تخليته عنه من حيث هذا الاعتبار أيضاً.
قوله : (الضرورة) الضرورة الأولى بالنسبة إلى نفس الحكم أعني الملازمة والثانية بالنسبة إلى أن الحكم بالضرورة أيضاً ضروري، فإنه قد يكون نظرياً فقول الشارح: إذ لا يمكن تنبيه على ذلك .
.
قوله : (أن يحصل في حيز معين إن أراد في معين من المعينات فيجوز أن يكون المخصص له امتناع كونه لا في مكان أو في كل أمكنة، وما قيل : إن الحصول في المكان المعين أمر وجودي فلا يمكن استناده إلي الامتناع الذي هو عدمي فمدفوع لأنه يجوز أن يكون الاستناد إلى الجسمية بشرط هذا الامتناع . قوله : ( ممنوع إلخ) قد عرفت اندفاعه بما حررنا لك من أن الحيز أعم من المكان . :قوله حتى يخصصه الفاعل (المختار إنما قيد بالمختار لئلا يرد أن نسبة الفاعل إلى الأحياز على السوية، فلا يخصص إلا بحسب الاستعدادات . قوله : ( ولا نسلم إمكان خلوه في نفس الأمر لاخفاء أنه يكفي لنا إثبات إمكان فرض الخلو، وإن كان المفروض محالاً ولا شك في إمكانه، فإن الجسم يمكن فرضه موجوداً عارياً ما لا يدخل في تقوم ماهيته ووجوده ثم إذا ،فرضه فلا بد أن يحصل في حيز معين عرفت . ولا شك أن الحصول في ذلك الحيز من الأمور الممكنة، فلا بد له من علة، وليست الأشياء الغريبة لأنا إذا فرضنا الخلو عنها فهي إما ذاته أو مقوم ماهيته أو لازم ذاته، والفاعل من جميع جميع عن لما
The First Observatory - The Eighth Objective: Proving Prime Matter in Body. 'The body is left to itself and its nature' i.e., supposed after its existence devoid of all possible extraneous influences, it would necessarily have a place, for it is impossible for a body to be not in a place, nor can it be imagined to occur in all places simultaneously; rather it must occur in a specific place. And its occurrence in that place cannot be due to an external thing, since it is supposed to be free from it; nor to common corporeality because its relation to all places is equal; nor to prime matter because it follows corporeality in requiring some place absolutely. Rather [it must be due] to another thing internal to it and specific to it, which is what is meant by nature. (We said:) What you have mentioned is denied; rather if the body is left to itself and its nature, it would be like the limited (al-muhaddad) having no place, as is the doctrine of Aristotle and those who follow him. Or we say: If left to itself and its nature, its relation to all places is equal until the free agent specifies it with a specific place. And we do not admit the possibility of its being devoid in actual fact of the influence of the free agent and His specification. And we say: If... His words: 'i.e., supposed after its existence' indicates that place is among the concomitants of existence, not of quiddity; so the agent is considered insofar as He is the one who brings it into existence. His words: 'of all that it can be devoid of' which is other than the necessary concomitant of its quiddity and the agent insofar as He is the one who brings it into existence; so the objection does not apply that if by 'devoid of' the agent is also intended, then the body would not exist, let alone require place; and if by 'devoid of' what is other than the agent is intended, then it is possible that the specifier is the agent, because the supposed is devoid of it from this consideration as well. His words: 'the necessity' – the first necessity relates to the judgment itself, i.e., the implication; the second relates to the fact that the judgment is also necessarily made, for it may be theoretical. So the commentator's saying 'it is impossible' points to that. His words: 'to occur in a specific place' – if he means a specific one of the specific things, then it is possible that the specifier for it is the impossibility of its being not in a place or in all places. And what is said that occurrence in a specific place is an existent thing and cannot be based on impossibility which is non-existent is rebutted, because it is possible that the basis is corporeality conditioned by this impossibility. His words: 'denied, etc.' – you have known its rebuttal by what we have clarified for you that place is more general than location. His words: 'until the free agent specifies it' – He was only qualified by 'free' so that it does not arise that the agent's relation to places is equal, so He would not specify except according to dispositions. His words: 'and we do not admit the possibility of its being devoid in actual fact' – it is obvious that it suffices for us to prove the possibility of supposing it devoid, even if the supposed is impossible; and there is no doubt about its possibility, for a body can be supposed existent stripped of what does not enter into the constitution of its quiddity and existence. Then if he supposes it, it must occur in a specific place, as you know. And there is no doubt that occurrence in that place is among possible things, so it must have a cause. And it is not the extraneous things, because if we suppose it devoid of them, it is either its essence, or the constituent of its quiddity, or a necessary concomitant of its essence, and the agent from all...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from natural place for the existence of prime matter (hayula) and its refutation by the mutakallimun. The proof, attributed to Ibn Sina, argues: if a body is left to itself and its nature (i.e., isolated from all external influences), it must necessarily have a specific place. The reason is that a body cannot be without a place nor in all places simultaneously. The cause of its occurrence in that specific place cannot be external (since we supposed it free from externals), nor common corporeality (which relates equally to all places), nor prime matter (which only follows corporeality). Therefore it must be due to an internal specific thing, which is nature. This nature requires prime matter to subsist in. The refutation (the 'qulna') offers two replies. First, we deny the premise: if the body is left to its nature, it is like the limited (al-muhaddad) which has no place, following Aristotle. Second, we say its relation to all places is equal until the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) specifies it. Moreover, we do not admit that the body can be actually devoid of the agent's effect, because the agent is always involved. The commentary then explains the details of the proof, clarifying that 'devoid of' excludes necessary concomitants and the agent as producer. It also handles an objection about necessity: the necessity of the implication is itself necessary, though the judgment may be theoretical. Another point: the specification of a specific place could be due to the impossibility of being without place or in all places, which is a non-existent thing, but this is answered by saying that corporeality with that condition could be the cause. The passage ends with a discussion that while we may suppose the body free of externals, we cannot suppose it free of its own essence or necessary concomitants; so the cause of its specific place could be its essence or the agent.

Key Terms
خلا الجسم وطبعه
حيز معين
الطبيعة
الهيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof proves that nature is the direct cause of the specific place, but the proof only shows that it must be an internal cause, not necessarily nature as a separate entity.
Correction: The proof does not specify what this internal cause is; it only argues that there must be some internal principle. The identification with 'nature' is a metaphysical interpretation.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof exclude common corporeality as the cause of the specific place?
A) Because corporeality is external to the body.
B) Because corporeality relates equally to all places.
C) Because corporeality is the same as nature.
D) Because corporeality is not internal enough.

Key Takeaway
The proof from natural place argues that a body must have an internal nature that determines its specific location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own existence, can you honestly claim that your 'place' in life is determined solely by external circumstances? How does this proof challenge you to recognize an internal nature given by your Creator?
?
This proof shows that even a simple body has a fixed nature. How does that humble you to accept that your purpose and boundaries are not self-defined? What would change if you lived fully according to that divinely implanted nature?
?
Do you sometimes act as if you are 'free' from any inherent nature, as if your choices are purely independent? How does the logical necessity of a nature in this proof force you to confront your dependence on Allah’s design?

intermediate

Refutation of the Proof
Al-Jurjani presents the refutation of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main refutations against the natural place proof: the Aristotelian view of no place, and the Ash'ari view of equal relation until divine specification.

Core Idea
The proof fails because a body left to nature either has no place or is indeterminate until the Creator specifies.

The mutakallimun respond to the natural place argument with two powerful objections, showing that the inference to a necessary nature is not forced. Instead, they affirm the role of the free divine agent in specifying location. Let's examine these refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic text starting from 'qulna' (we said). Note that it offers two distinct replies separated by 'aw' (or).
The first reply is attributed to Aristotle; the second is the Ash'ari position.
2
Analyze the first reply
The first reply denies the conclusion: if the body is left to its nature, it would be like the limited (al-muhaddad) having no place at all. This rejects the premise that a body must have a place.
This is based on the idea that place is not intrinsic but accidental.
3
Analyze the second reply
The second reply admits that the body would have an equal relation to all places, so it needs a specifier. That specifier is the free agent (Allah), not an internal nature.
This preserves divine action and avoids necessitarianism.
4
Understand the deeper point
Both replies undermine the need to posit an internal nature. The mutakallimun show that the proof's conclusion does not follow; either a body can be placeless, or its place is determined by an external free agent.
This is part of the broader Ash'ari critique of Aristotelian naturalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The proof, attributed to Ibn Sina, argues: if a body is left to itself and its nature (i.e., isolated from all external influences), it must necessarily have a specific place. Moreover, we do not admit that the body can be actually devoid of the agent's effect, because the agent is always involved. It also handles an objection about necessity: the necessity of the implication is itself necessary, though the judgment may be theoretical. The passage ends with a discussion that while we may suppose the body free of externals, we cannot suppose it free of its own essence or necessary concomitants; so the cause of its specific place could be its essence or the agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the refutation only offers one objection, but it actually contains two distinct replies.
Correction: The text explicitly gives two replies separated by 'aw' (or). Both are valid refutations from different perspectives.

Checkpoint
What is the first reply in the refutation?
A) That the body would be in all places equally.
B) That the body would have no place at all, like the limited.
C) That the body's place is determined by prime matter.
D) That the body's nature is identical to its place.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that a body left to its nature does not force a specific place; alternatives exist without invoking an internal nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world around you, do you ever catch yourself assuming that things simply 'are the way they are' without considering the One who specifies every detail? What would it mean for your daily trust in Allah if you truly internalized that no object, event, or attribute is self-sufficient?
?
This refutation shows that even the concept of 'place' is not inherent to a body but requires a Creator's choice. Is there an area of your life—your career, your relationships, your struggles—where you are acting as if its outcome is determined purely by its own nature, rather than by Allah's active specification and will? What would change if you treated that area as equally needing His choice every moment?

Two Refutations of the Claim that Body Needs a Place by Nature
The author presents the claim and gives two refutations of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars refute the claim that a body left to its nature must occupy a specific place, and see two distinct arguments: one based on the analogy of a limit and one based on the role of a free agent.

Core Idea
A body left to itself has no inherent place; only a free agent can specify its location.

The passage presents an opponent's argument that a body, if stripped of all external influences, would still necessarily have a place due to its nature. This is a key claim in natural philosophy. The author responds with two incisive refutations that reveal the metaphysical commitments of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opponent's claim
First, locate the claim that a body left to its nature must have a place. Notice the reasoning behind it.
The opponent's argument is presented in the first part.
2
Examine the first refutation
The author gives an analogy: the body would be like a 'limit' (point) with no place. Understand how this refutes the necessity of place.
This refutation appeals to Aristotle's own doctrine.
3
Examine the second refutation
The second response says the body's relation to all places is equal, and only a free agent can specify it. See how this introduces divine agency.
This refutation rejects the assumption that the body can be devoid of the agent's influence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلي الجسم وطبعه أي فرض بعد وجوده خالياً عن جميع ما يمكن خلوه عنه من التأثيرات الغريبة لكان له مكان ضرورة إذ لا يمكن جسم لا في مكان ولا يتصور حصوله في جميع الأمكنة معاً، بل لا بد أن يحصل في حيز معين، ولا يكون حصوله في ذلك الحيز مستنداً إلى أمر خارج، إذ المفروض خلوه عنه ولا إلى الجسمية المشتركة لأن نسبتها إلى الأحياز كلها على السوية ولا إلى الهيولى لأنها تابعة للجسمية في اقتضاء حيز ما على الإطلاق، بل إلى أمر آخر داخل فيه مختص به، وهو المراد بالطبيعة قلنا ما ذكرتم ممنوع، بل لو خلي الجسم وطبعه لكان كالمحدد لا مكان له كما هو مذهب أرسطو ومن تابعه أو نقول إذا خلي وطبعه تكون نسبته إلى الأحياز كلها سواء حتى يخصصه الفاعل المختار بحيز معين ولا نسلم إمكان خلوه في نفس الأمر عن تأثير المختار وتخصيصه
If the body and its nature are left—i.e., supposed after its existence devoid of all extrinsic influences from which it can be stripped—it would necessarily have a place, since a body cannot be without a place, nor can it be conceived to occur in all places simultaneously; rather it must occur in a specific region. Its occurrence in that region is not attributable to an external thing, since it is supposed to be devoid of it, nor to the common corporeality because its relation to all regions is equal, nor to matter (hayula) because it follows corporeality in requiring some region absolutely, but rather to another internal thing particular to it, which is meant by 'nature'. We said: what you mentioned is rejected; rather, if the body and its nature are left, it would be like a limit [i.e., point] having no place, as is the doctrine of Aristotle and his followers. Or we say: if it and its nature are left, its relation to all regions is equal until the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) specifies it to a particular region, and we do not admit the possibility of its being devoid in reality of the agent's influence and specification.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an argument-and-response pair. It begins by presenting an opponent's claim: a body left to its nature must necessarily have a place. The reasoning is that a body cannot be without a place, nor in all places simultaneously, so it must occupy a specific region. That specific region cannot be due to external causes (since the body is assumed devoid of them), nor to common corporeality (which is indifferent to all regions), nor to prime matter (which follows corporeality). Therefore, it must be due to an internal principle called 'nature'. The author then gives two refutations. First, if the body is left to its nature, it would be like a mathematical point with no place at all, following Aristotle's own view. Second, even if we grant the thought experiment, the body's relation to all regions is equal, and only a free agent can specify it. Moreover, the assumption that the body can be completely devoid of the agent's influence in reality is not conceded.

Key Terms
طبيعة (nature)
حيز (region)
مكان (place)
هيولى (matter/hayula)
جسم (body)
فاعل مختار (free agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the opponent's argument proves a body's nature inherently tends to a specific place, and this cannot be avoided.
Correction: The refutations show that without external determination (or a free agent), a body has no specific place; either it is like a point or its location is indeterminate.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that a body left to its nature must have a place?
A) He accepts the claim and explains that nature determines place.
B) He gives two refutations: either the body becomes like a limit with no place, or its relation to places is equal until a chooser specifies.
C) He argues that the body's matter determines place.
D) He denies that a body can be left to its nature at all.

Key Takeaway
The claim that a body's nature necessitates a specific place is refuted by arguing that a body left to itself would have no place (like a point) or that its relation to all places is equal until determined by a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What specific area of your life have you been acting as if it must be a certain way 'by nature' — your personality, your circumstances, your habits — without acknowledging that only Allah's free choice determines its true place? Will you stop today and ask Him to reveal where He wants you to be?
?
Consider a belief or attachment you hold that feels 'natural' to you — like you couldn't possibly be without it. Does this lesson expose that feeling as an illusion? Are you willing to let that thing be stripped of its assumed necessity and submit to the Agent who alone gives it its place?
?
The opponent argued that a body cannot be without a place; the refutation says it would be like a point — having no place. What in your life have you assumed is inextricably tied to you (your status, your relationships, your identity) that you might need to see as neutral until Allah specifies it? Are you ready to live with that radical uncertainty?

Region as a Concomitant of Existence
The commentator clarifies that region is a concomitant of existence and explains the scope of 'stripping'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the thought experiment of 'khalw' requires that the body be considered after its existence, and how the agent's role is preserved.

Core Idea
Region depends on existence, not essence; the agent gives existence, not region.

This lesson unpacks a key clarification from the commentary on the conditions of 'khalw' (being left alone). The commentator explains that region (ḥayz) is a concomitant of existence, not of essence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for the thought experiment that follows in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms: ḥayz, wujūd, māhiyya, fāʿil, mukhaṣṣiṣ.
2
Identify the two key points
The passage makes two clarifications: (a) region is a concomitant of existence, not essence; (b) the phrase 'from all that it can be stripped of' excludes the concomitant of essence and the agent as giver of existence.
3
Understand the objection and its deflection
If stripping included the agent, the body would not exist, so the objection is invalid. If stripping excludes the agent, then the agent can still be the specifier.
This shows the thought experiment only removes extrinsic factors.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why these distinctions are necessary for the proof of God's existence. The commentary ensures the thought experiment is coherent.
This connects to the broader kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أي فرض بعد وجوده ) إشارة إلى أن الحيز من لوازم الوجود لا الماهية فالفاعل معتبر من حيث أنه موجد له . قوله : ( عن جميع ما يمكن خلوه منه وهو ما سوى لازم ماهيته والفاعل من حيث هو موجد له فلا يرد ما قيل : إن أريد التخلية من الفاعل أيضاً، فالجسم حينئذ لا يكون موجوداً فضلاً عن اقتضاء الحيز، وإن أريد التخلية مما سوى الفاعل فليجز أن يكون المخصص هو الفاعل لأن المفروض تخليته عنه من حيث هذا الاعتبار أيضاً
His saying: 'i.e., supposed after its existence' indicates that region (ḥayz) is a concomitant of existence, not of essence; thus the agent is considered insofar as he is the one who brings it into existence. His saying: 'from all that it can be stripped of, which is other than the concomitant of its essence and the agent insofar as he is the one who brings it into existence' — so the objection that if stripping from the agent is intended, then the body would not exist, let alone require a region, is not raised. And if stripping from other than the agent is intended, then it is permissible that the specifier be the agent, because the assumption is that it is stripped from him in this respect as well.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the thought experiment's conditions. The commentator first clarifies that 'after its existence' means that region (ḥayz) is a concomitant of existence, not of essence. Therefore, the agent (al-fāʿil) is considered insofar as he is the giver of existence. Next, the commentator explains that the phrase 'from all that it can be stripped of' excludes two things: the concomitant of its essence and the agent insofar as he brings it into existence. This prevents an objection: if one intended stripping from the agent as well, then the body would not exist at all, so it cannot require a region. But if stripping from other than the agent is intended, then it is permissible that the specifier (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ) is the agent, because the assumption is that it is stripped from him in this respect too. In other words, the thought experiment only removes extrinsic influences, not the agent's role in giving existence or the necessary concomitants of essence.

Key Terms
حيز (region)
وجود (existence)
ماهية (essence)
لازم (concomitant)
فاعل (agent)
مخصص (specifier)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The thought experiment strips the body of all factors, including the agent entirely.
Correction: The commentator explains that stripping excludes the agent as giver of existence and necessary concomitants; otherwise the body would not exist.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the phrase 'after its existence' indicate?
A) That the body must exist before it can have a region.
B) That region is a concomitant of existence, not of essence.
C) That the agent is not considered in the thought experiment.
D) That the body's essence determines its region.

Key Takeaway
Region is a concomitant of existence, and the agent's role as giver of existence is not stripped in the thought experiment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute your position, health, or circumstances to your own essence or effort, forgetting that even your existence and its necessary concomitants are wholly from Allah? How would your gratitude change if you truly internalised that region itself is a gift of existence?
?
In your daily life, do you act as if you control the 'region' you occupy—your status, your environment—or do you recognise that these are necessary consequences of the existence Allah granted you? What would it mean to let go of that illusion of control?
?
If the agent only gives existence and does not specify region, then what is your real responsibility? Could it be that your task is simply to exist in gratitude, and let the concomitant attributes follow naturally? Are you trying to force your region instead of accepting what existence brings?

Necessity and the Cause of Specific Place
The commentator explains the two necessary judgments and refutes objections about privation as cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of necessity in judgments and how impossibility can serve as a condition for corporeality in determining a specific place.

Core Idea
Even non-existence can be a cause when conditioned by corporeality.

This passage from the commentary clarifies two kinds of necessity and addresses a key objection: how can impossibility, a non-existent, be a cause? The commentator explains that it can be a condition attached to corporeality. Let's step through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two necessities
Read the first part where the commentator distinguishes between the necessity of the implication itself and the necessity of the judgment being necessary. Note that the second necessity may be theoretical.
The first necessity is the necessary relationship between premise and conclusion; the second is the modality of the judgment itself.
2
Learn the specifier for a specific place
Examine the explanation of 'specific region' (ḥayz muʿayyan). The specifier can be the impossibility of the body being in no place or in all places.
3
Address the objection about privation as cause
Consider the objection that impossibility (non-existence) cannot cause an existential matter. The commentator rebuts it by saying attribution can be to corporeality conditioned by the impossibility.
This is the key move: impossibility is a condition, not the direct cause.
4
Note the role of the free agent and the final argument
Understand why the text qualifies 'free agent' (mukhtār) – to avoid the objection that the agent's relation to all regions is equal. Finally, see the argument that assuming the body stripped of extrinsic factors leads to a cause within the body itself (essence, constituent, or concomitant).
This closes the discussion by showing that the cause is internal, not external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الضرورة) الضرورة الأولى بالنسبة إلى نفس الحكم أعني الملازمة والثانية بالنسبة إلى أن الحكم بالضرورة أيضاً ضروري، فإنه قد يكون نظرياً فقول الشارح: إذ لا يمكن تنبيه على ذلك . قوله : (أن يحصل في حيز معين إن أراد في معين من المعينات فيجوز أن يكون المخصص له امتناع كونه لا في مكان أو في كل أمكنة، وما قيل : إن الحصول في المكان المعين أمر وجودي فلا يمكن استناده إلي الامتناع الذي هو عدمي فمدفوع لأنه يجوز أن يكون الاستناد إلى الجسمية بشرط هذا الامتناع . قوله : ( ممنوع إلخ) قد عرفت اندفاعه بما حررنا لك من أن الحيز أعم من المكان . :قوله حتى يخصصه الفاعل (المختار إنما قيد بالمختار لئلا يرد أن نسبة الفاعل إلى الأحياز على السوية، فلا يخصص إلا بحسب الاستعدادات . قوله : ( ولا نسلم إمكان خلوه في نفس الأمر لاخفاء أنه يكفي لنا إثبات إمكان فرض الخلو، وإن كان المفروض محالاً ولا شك في إمكانه، فإن الجسم يمكن فرضه موجوداً عارياً ما لا يدخل في تقوم ماهيته ووجوده ثم إذا فرضه فلا بد أن يحصل في حيز معين عرفت . ولا شك أن الحصول في ذلك الحيز من الأمور الممكنة، فلا بد له من علة، وليست الأشياء الغريبة لأنا إذا فرضنا الخلو عنها فهي إما ذاته أو مقوم ماهيته أو لازم ذاته
His saying: 'necessity (al-ḍarūra)' — the first necessity pertains to the judgment itself, namely the implication; the second pertains to the judgment being necessary also, for it may be theoretical, so the commentator's statement: 'since it is not possible' alerts to that. His saying: 'that it occur in a specific region' — if he means specific among particulars, then it is possible that its specifier is the impossibility of its being in no place or in all places. And what is said: 'that occurrence in the specific place is an existential matter, so it cannot be attributed to impossibility which is non-existential' is rebutted because it is possible that the attribution is to corporeality (al-jismiyya) conditioned by this impossibility. His saying: 'rejected (mamnūʿ), etc.' — you already know its refutation from what we have set forth for you: that region is more general than place. His saying: 'until the free agent specifies it' — he only qualified it with 'free' (mukhtār) to prevent the objection that the agent's relation to regions is equal, so it would only specify according to dispositions (istiʿdādāt). His saying: 'and we do not admit the possibility of its being devoid in reality' — because it suffices for us to prove the possibility of assuming the void, even if the assumed thing is impossible, and there is no doubt about its possibility. For the body can be assumed existing stripped of what does not enter into the constitution of its essence and existence; then if one assumes it, it must occur in a specific region as you know. And there is no doubt that occurring in that region is among possible things, so it must have a cause. And the extrinsic things are not [the cause] because if we assume it stripped of them, then [the cause] is either its essence, or a constituent of its essence, or a concomitant of its essence.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that clarifies several points in the main argument. First, it distinguishes two types of necessity: the necessity of the implication itself, and the necessity of the judgment being necessary. Second, it explains that 'specific region' can be understood as specific among particulars, and then the specifier can be the impossibility of being nowhere or everywhere. It rebuts an objection that impossibility, being non-existent, cannot be the cause of an existent; the answer is that it can be a condition attached to corporeality. Third, it notes that the main text's 'rejected' is already refuted by the earlier point that region is more general than place. Fourth, the qualification 'free agent' is explained to avoid the objection that the agent's relation to regions is equal, so specification would require dispositions in the bodies. Finally, an argument that the possibility of assuming the body stripped of extrinsic factors is sufficient, and the cause of the specific place must be either the body's essence, a constituent, or a concomitant, not an external factor.

Key Terms
ضرورة
حيز
مكان
امتناع
جسمية
مختار
استعداد
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility (non-existence) cannot be the cause of an existential matter like occurrence in a place.
Correction: The commentator explains that the attribution is not directly to impossibility but to corporeality conditioned by impossibility, so impossibility can serve as a condition.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator rebut the objection that impossibility cannot be the cause of an existential matter?
A) He asserts that impossibility is itself existent.
B) He says that the cause is the free agent who is not limited by impossibility.
C) He replies that the attribution can be to corporeality conditioned by this impossibility.
D) He denies that the specific place is existential.

Key Takeaway
The cause of a body's specific place can involve impossibility as a condition attached to corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were stripped of all external factors—wealth, status, relationships, even your own body's familiar location—what would remain as the cause of your existence? Does your soul alone suffice, or does it point to a Creator you must rely on every moment? How will you let this truth change your attachment to worldly things today?
?
The passage shows that even the specific place of a body has a cause rooted in its nature. In your spiritual life, have you mistaken habit or comfort for necessity, attributing your faith's condition to circumstances rather than your own soul's orientation? What one step will you take today to align your inner state with what is truly necessary for your relationship with Allah?
?
The objector said impossibility cannot cause an existential matter, but the answer is it can as a condition. Have you ever dismissed a spiritual truth because it seemed contradictory—like mercy through hardship or growth through trials? Will you open your heart to the possibility that what seems like impossibility may be the condition through which Allah works in your life? What specific trial are you facing that you need to reconsider as a condition for your own transformation?

Two Corollaries on Natural Place
Two corollaries on natural place with proof and objection handling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two corollaries derived from the principle that every body has a natural place, and the reasoning behind them.

Core Idea
A body can have only one natural place; if it doesn't seek another, that other is not natural.

This lesson builds on the foundational principle that every body has a natural place. We now explore two specific implications (corollaries) that refine our understanding of what 'natural place' means. The first corollary addresses whether a body can have two natural places, and the second discusses the condition under which a body moves to its natural place after coercion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic text once without analyzing. Try to identify the two corollaries (فرعان) and their supporting arguments.
The passage includes commentary; focus first on the lines beginning with 'فرعان' and 'الأول'.
2
Understand the first corollary
The first corollary states a body cannot have two natural places. Follow the reasoning: if it is in one and seeks the other, the current place is not natural. If it does not seek the other, then the other is not natural.
Notice the key condition: seeking only occurs when the body is not already in its natural place.
3
Examine the second corollary
The second corollary discusses a body forced outside both natural places and then left to its nature. The text begins this reasoning but cuts off; the commentary clarifies that such a body will move to one of them.
Consider the commentary's explanation that being outside both does not prevent a natural direction.
4
Review the commentary on objections
The passage includes a discussion of an objection and answer. Understand why the lack of seeking does not disprove a place's naturalness, and the point that the requirement of a natural place is unconditional.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.
5
Apply the corollaries
Reflect on how these corollaries reinforce the uniqueness and determinacy of a body's natural place, which is essential for understanding motion in kalam physics.
Think of examples like water: its natural place is down; it cannot have two natural places.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرعان على أن لكل جسم مكاناً طبيعياً . الأول : لا يكون لجسم واحد حيزان طبيعيان فإنه إذا كان في أحدهما ، فإن طلب الآخر فهذا المكان الذي هو فيه الآن ليس طبيعياً له لأنه هارب عنه طالب لغيره وإلا أي وإن لم يطلب الآخر حال كونه في أحدهما فالآخر ليس طبيعياً له لأنه ليس طالباً له حين ما خلي وطبعه وأيضاً إذا كان الجسم خارجاً عنهما بالقسر ثم خلي وطبعه فإما أن ... قوله : ( وإن لم يطلب إلخ) في شرح التجريد عدم الطلب بمكان طبيعياً لا يقدح في كون هذا المكان طبيعياً، فإن طلب المكان إنما يكون إذا لم يكن موجداً لمكان هو مطلوبه وليس بشيء، لأن المكان الطبيعي على ما مر لو خلي الجسم وطبعه اقتضاه، والاقتضاء ليس مشروطاً بشيء، إنما المشروط بعدم وجدان الحركة إليه . قوله : (إذا كان الجسم إلخ ) والخروج عنهما غير اختصاص بجهة دون جهة ممكن، وإلا لكان أحدهما لازماً فلا يكون الثاني طبيعياً فيكون الخروج، لا على سمتها أيضاً ممكناً والتخلية ممكنة وليس بين الخروج والتخلية تناف حتى لا يمكن الاجتماع بعدم فرض وقوع الخروج، نقضاً . قوله : ( وقد يجاب إلخ ) هذا الجواب إنما يفيد إذا جعل قوله: لكل جزء من الأرض إجمالياً مذكوراً بعد إيراد المنع، وأما إذا جعل سنداً للمنع كما هو الظاهر فلا يفيد إذ هو أعني هذا الجواب يكون حينئذ كلاماً على السند مع أنه يمكن أن يقال : لا نسلم أن فرض خلو الجسم عن جميع الأمور الخارجية عنه يقتضي خلوه في نفس الأمر. قوله : ( فالآخر ليس طبيعياً وعليه منع ظاهر بأن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون عدم طلب الحيز الآخر لحصوله في أحد الحيزين الطبيعيين؟
Two branches based on the fact that every body has a natural place. First: a single body does not have two natural places. For if it is in one of them, and it seeks the other, then the place it is in now is not natural for it because it is fleeing from it and seeking another. Otherwise, i.e., if it does not seek the other while in one of them, then the other is not natural for it because it does not seek it when left to its nature. Also, if the body is outside both by coercion, then left to its nature, then either ... His statement: (And if it does not seek etc.) In Sharh al-Tajrid: the lack of seeking a place naturally does not detract from this place being natural, for seeking a place only occurs if it is not finding a place that is its sought object, and it is nothing. Because the natural place, as previously, if the body is left to its nature, it requires it, and the requirement is not conditional on anything; the condition is only the lack of finding motion towards it. His statement: (If the body is etc.) And being outside both is not impossible with no specification to one direction, otherwise one of them would be necessary and the second would not be natural, so being outside—not in their direction—is also possible, and abandonment (to nature) is possible, and there is no contradiction between being outside and abandonment such that they cannot coexist without assuming the occurrence of being outside, as a counterexample. His statement: (And it may be answered etc.) This answer only benefits if his statement 'for every part of the earth' is taken as a general statement mentioned after the objection. But if it is taken as a basis for the objection, as is apparent, then it does not benefit, because this answer then becomes speech about the basis, and it can be said: we do not grant that assuming the body free of all external matters necessitates its freedom in actual fact. His statement: (So the other is not natural) and upon this is an obvious objection: why is it not permissible that the lack of seeking the other place is due to its being in one of the two natural places?
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents two corollaries (فرعان) of the principle that every body has a natural place. The first corollary: a single body cannot have two natural places. The reasoning: if the body is in one place and seeks the other, then the current place is not natural because it is fleeing it. If it does not seek the other while in the first, then the other is not natural because the body does not seek it when left to its nature. The second corollary: if the body is outside both places by coercion and then left to its nature, it will move to one of them—the text cuts off. The commentary then explains: in Sharh al-Tajrid, the absence of seeking does not negate naturalness; seeking only occurs when the body does not already have its sought place. The requirement of a natural place is not conditional. On the second corollary, the commentator clarifies that being outside both does not preclude a direction; if it did, one of them would be necessary. Abandonment is possible and does not contradict being outside. The later commentary repeats an earlier point about the objection and answer, and finally notes an obvious objection: why can't the lack of seeking the other be because the body is already in one natural place (so it doesn't seek it)? This is a valid point.

Key Terms
فرعان
حيزان طبيعيان
مكان طبيعي
خلي وطبعه
قسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a body does not seek a particular place, that place cannot be its natural one.
Correction: The absence of seeking does not indicate a place is not natural; a body only seeks its natural place when it is not already there.

Checkpoint
According to the first corollary, if a body is in one place and does not seek another, what does that imply about the other place?
A) It is also natural.
B) It is not natural.
C) It is the body's original place.
D) It is indeterminate.

Key Takeaway
A body's natural place is unique; if it does not seek an alternative when left to its nature, that alternative is not its natural place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does the logic of this lesson—that a body seeks its natural place when left to itself—challenge you to examine your own inclinations without external distractions? What would it mean to 'leave yourself to your nature' (takhliya) and see if that goal truly moves you?

The Four Considerations of Simple Bodies
Commentary explains the third and fourth considerations and summarizes all four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four considerations of simple bodies and how they differ based on reality, perception, and naming conditions.

Core Idea
Simple bodies are classified by four considerations varying in generality and specificity.

In this passage, the commentator explains the third and fourth considerations of simple bodies, then summarizes all four, showing how each captures a different aspect of simplicity. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping how kalam classifies physical objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the technical terms like بسيط, اعتبار, and the examples.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the third and fourth considerations
The third consideration defines simple by reality (لا يتركب بحسب الحقيقة من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع). The fourth defines by perception (لا يتركب بحسب الحس من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع).
The first two considerations are on the previous page.
3
Follow the summary of all four
The commentator then summarizes the four considerations in terms of composition and naming conditions. Pay attention to the branching: first, second, etc.
The summary uses conditional distinctions.
4
Note the examples
Examples: elements (unconditional name), sphere (conditioned name), flesh (unconditional), artery/vein (conditioned). These illustrate how naming conditions affect membership.
Artery and vein are hollow, which conditions their name.
5
Understand the relationships
The first consideration is the most specific (only elements). The second and third have partial generality. The fourth is the most general.
This systematization helps classify any body.

المتشابهة، فإن كل جزء محسوس منها يساويها في الاسم والحد دون الفلك. الثالث : ما لا يتركب بحسب الحقيقة من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع فيشمل العناصر والفلك دون شيء من أعضاء الحيوان . الرابع: ما لا يتركب بحسب الحس من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع فيتناول الكل، فهو أعم الاعتبارات وأولها أخصها وبين الثاني والثالث عموم من وجه وتلخيصه أن ما لا يتركب من أجسام محسوسة مختلفة الطبائع إما أن لا يتركب من أجسام مختلفة ويتركب منها لكنها غير محسوسة. وعلى الأول : إما أن لا يكون اسمه موضوعاً له بشرط كونه موصوفاً بصفة مخصوصة كالماء والأرض والهواء والنار، فيشاركه أجزاؤه في اسمه وحده، وإما أن يكون مشروطاً به فلا يطلق اسمه على أجزائه، كالفلك إذ قد اعتبر في اسمه شكل معين. وعلى الثاني أيضاً، إما أن لا يعتبر في الاسم صفة كاللحم والعظم فيطلق اسمه على جزئه، أو يعتبر فلا يطلق كالشريان والوريد، إذ قد اعتبر فيهما التجويف والهيئة المخصوصة. فالاعتبار الرابع هذه الأربعة بأسرها. والأول يتناول واحداً منها ولا يخفى عليك حال الآخرين وإلى ما فصلناه لك أشار مجملا بقوله : ( فاعتبر ذلك ) أي الذي ذكرناه من اعتبار كل واحد من رسمي البسيط بحسب الحقيقة أو الحس ) في الأعضاء المتشابهة ) الحيوانية ( كاللحم والعظم) ونظائرهما ( وفي الفلك يظهر لك الفرق ) بين الاعتبارات الأربعة كما عرفت
قوله : ( وأولها أخصها لاختصاصه بالعناصر . قوله : ( وبين الثاني والثالث عموم إلخ ) لصدقهما على العناصر وصدق الثاني على الأعضاء المتشابهة بدون الثالث وصدق الثالث بدون الثاني في الفلك . قوله : (كالشريان) وهي العرق النابت من القلب المتحرك بحركته، والوريد العرق النابت من الكبد الغير المتحرك .
قوله : ( كالشريان والوريد إلخ الشريان هو عرق نابض مجوف ثابت من القلب، والوريد هو عرق مجوف مضاعف غليظ في العنق، وهو اثنان : ففي كل عنق وريدان كل منها اثنان . قوله : ( دون الفلك لا يكون جميع أجزائه المقدارية بحسب الحقيقة مساوياً في الاسم والحد ، فحينئذ لا يضر أن يكون بعض أجزائه كذلك مثل مجموع المتممين الحاوي والمحوي، فإنه جزء من فلكه وإنه مساو له في ذلك بحسب الحقيقة كما لا يخفى .
The similar (organs): for every perceptible part of them equals them in name and definition, except the sphere. Third: what is not composed in reality from bodies of different natures, so it includes the elements and the sphere, not any of the animal organs. Fourth: what is not composed according to perception from bodies of different natures, so it includes all. Thus it is the most general of the considerations, and the first is the most specific of them, and between the second and the third there is generality from one aspect. Its summary: that which is not composed from perceptible bodies of different natures either does not compose from different bodies, or composes from them but they are imperceptible. On the first: either its name is not posited for it with the condition of it being described by a specific attribute, like water, earth, air, and fire, so its parts share with it in its name and definition; or it is conditioned, so its name is not applied to its parts, like the sphere, for a specific shape has been considered in its name. On the second also: either no attribute is considered in the name, like flesh and bone, so its name is applied to its part; or it is considered, so it is not applied, like the artery and vein, for cavity and specific form have been considered in them. Thus the fourth consideration comprises all these four. The first takes only one of them, and the state of the others is not hidden from you. And to what we have detailed for you he alluded summarily in his saying: 'So consider that' — meaning what we have mentioned of considering each of the two definitions of the simple according to reality or perception — in the similar animal organs like flesh and bone and their likes, and in the sphere the difference appears to you between the four considerations, as you have known. His saying: 'And the first is the most specific of them' because of its specification to the elements. His saying: 'And between the second and the third there is generality, etc.' because they both apply to the elements, and the second applies to the similar organs without the third, and the third applies without the second in the sphere. His saying: 'like the artery' — it is the vein sprouting from the heart moving with its movement, and the vein is the vein sprouting from the liver not moving. His saying: 'like the artery and vein, etc.' — the artery is a pulsating hollow vein originating from the heart, and the vein is a hollow double thick vein in the neck, and there are two: in each neck two veins, each of them two. His saying: 'except the sphere' — not all of its quantitative parts are equal in name and definition according to reality; then it does not harm that some of its parts are like that, such as the totality of the complementaries, the container and the contained, for it is a part of its sphere and is equal to it in that according to reality, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the four considerations (i'tibārāt) of simple bodies (basīṭ). The commentator begins mid-enumeration, describing the third and fourth considerations, then summarizes all four. The third consideration defines a simple body by reality (ḥaqīqa): it is not composed from bodies of different natures, which applies to elements and the sphere but not animal organs. The fourth consideration defines by perception (ḥiss): it is not composed from perceptibly different bodies, so it includes everything. The commentator then explains the second and third have partial generality. He then provides a summary: things not composed of perceptibly different bodies either are not composed at all or are composed of imperceptible bodies. In the first case, the name either is unconditional (like elements, where parts share the name) or conditional (like sphere, where shape matters). In the second case, the name either has no condition (like flesh, parts share name) or has conditions (like artery/vein, where cavity matters). The fourth consideration encompasses all. The commentator also clarifies that the first consideration is the most specific (only elements) and the second and third have mutual generality. He then explains examples like artery and vein, and notes that the sphere's parts do not share its name in reality, though some composite parts may be equal.

Misconception: One might think the first consideration (most specific) applies to all simple bodies, but it applies only to elements.
Correction: The first consideration is specific to elements; other considerations cover different types.

The Four Considerations of Composite Bodies
Commentary enumerates four considerations of composite bodies and contrasts with simple bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four considerations used to define composite bodies and how they differ from the considerations of simple bodies.

Core Idea
Composite bodies are defined by four considerations, each with a different scope.

In this passage, the commentator builds on the earlier discussion of simple bodies to explain the analogous four considerations for composite bodies. He shows how the definitions change depending on whether we consider reality or perception, and he clarifies the scope of each consideration. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly classifying bodies in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is dense with technical terms.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage presents two main definitions of a composite body: one based on the quantitative part, and one based on composition from different natures. Find each.
Each definition has a reality-based and perception-based variant.
3
Observe the four considerations
The commentator says there are four considerations (i'tibārāt) for the composite. Identify each: (1) first def, reality; (2) first def, perception; (3) second def, reality; (4) second def, perception.
These correspond to the four possible combinations.
4
Understand the order of generality
The first consideration is the most general, the fourth is the most specific, and the middle two overlap in some cases. Note how this reverses the pattern for simple bodies.
This is a key difference from the simple body case.
5
Grasp the definitions of simple and composite
The passage ends with a clarification of what 'simple body' and 'composite body' mean in this context: simple is not composed of different natures, composite is opposite.
This provides a clear, concise definition for the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الجسم المركب بخلافه فهو على الرسم الأول ما لا يكون جزؤه المقداري بحسب الحقيقة مساوياً له في الاسم والحد فيخرج عنه من البسائط المذكورة العناصر دون الفلك والأعضاء المتشابهة . وإن اعتبر الجزء المقداري بحسب الحس خرجت تلك الأعضاء أيضاً، وعلى الرسم الثاني هو ما يتركب بحسب الحقيقة من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع فيخرج عنه العناصر والفلك دون الأعضاء المذكورة، وإن اعتبر التركيب بحسب الحس خرجت هذه الأعضاء أيضاً. هذه الأعضاء أيضا. ففي رسم المركب اعتبارات أربعة أيضاً إلا أن أولها أعمها ورابعها أخصها على عكس ما تقدم، وبين الباقيين عموم من وجه كما هناك . واعلم أن المراد بالجسم البسيط في هذا الموضع ما لا يتركب حقيقته في نفس الأمر من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع، وبالمركب ما يقابله .
And the composite body is contrary to it. So according to the first definition, it is that whose quantitative part is not equal to it in name and definition according to reality, so the elements among the mentioned simples are excluded from it, but not the sphere and the similar organs. And if the quantitative part is considered according to perception, those organs are also excluded. And according to the second definition, it is that which is composed in reality from bodies of different natures, so the elements and the sphere are excluded from it, but not the mentioned organs. And if composition is considered according to perception, these organs are also excluded. So in the definition of the composite there are also four considerations, except that the first of them is the most general and the fourth is the most specific, contrary to what preceded, and between the remaining two there is generality from one aspect, as there. And know that what is meant by the simple body in this place is that whose reality in itself is not composed from bodies of different natures, and by the composite what is opposite to it.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the four considerations (i'tibārāt) of composite bodies (murakkab). The commentator states that composite bodies are the opposite of simple bodies in classification. According to the first definition (based on reality of the quantitative part), a composite body is such that its quantitative part does not equal it in name and definition; this excludes elements but includes the sphere and similar organs. If the perception-based definition is used, similar organs are also excluded. According to the second definition (based on composition from different natures), a composite body is composed of different natures in reality, excluding elements and the sphere but including the mentioned organs; with perception-based composition, those organs are excluded. Thus, there are four considerations for composite bodies as well, but unlike simple bodies, the first consideration is the most general and the fourth is the most specific. The remaining two (second and third) have mutual generality, just as in the simple case. The commentator then clarifies that in this context, 'simple body' means a body whose reality is not composed of different natures, and 'composite' is its opposite.

Proof That Every Body Has a Natural Shape
Argument that every body has a natural shape due to finitude, with objections and replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that every body necessarily has a natural shape due to finitude, and the key objections that challenge whether finitude is intrinsic to the body's nature.

Core Idea
Finitude forces natural shape — but is finitude intrinsic to body?

This passage presents a foundational argument in Islamic cosmology: every body, whether simple or composite, must have a natural shape due to its necessary finitude. However, the argument invites critical examination, as objections challenge whether finitude is truly intrinsic to bodily nature. Let's follow the reasoning and its rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Read the main claim and proof
Start with the author's claim: every body has a natural shape because it is necessarily finite. Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
Note the phrase 'لوجوب تناهيه'.
2
Examine the marginal note
Notice that 'any body' includes both composite and simple. This sets the scope.
3
Understand the objection
The objector divides bodies into three cases and argues that simple bodies do not necessitate finitude.
The objection is from a gloss on Sharh al-Tajrid.
4
Follow the reply regarding place
The reply distinguishes between place as dimension (necessary) and place as surface (not necessary). See how this addresses the objection.
Reflect on why the reply is not fully conclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن المصنف ذكر هاهنا حكماً عاماً للأجسام البسيطة والمركبة، أن لها شكلاً وهو أن الشكل الطبيعي للبسيط ماذا فقال : ( ولكل جسم بسيطا كان أو مركباً (شكل طبيعي) وذلك ( لوجوب تناهيه ) لما سيرد عليك من استحالة لا تناهي الأبعاد (فلو خلي الجسم) أي جسم كان ( وطبعه ) بأن يفرض بعد وجوده خالياً عن ما يمكن خلوه عنه من التأثيرات الخارجية ( يحيط به حد ) أي طرف واحد
قوله : ( أي جسم كان مركباً أو بسيطاً.
قوله : ( لكل جسم شكل طبيعي وذلك لوجوب تناهيه كل جسم إما أن يقتضي تناهي أبعاده أو يقتضي لا تناهي أبعاده، أو يقتضي شيئاً منهما، والمسلم من هذه الأقسام هو الثالث، والأولان ممنوعان في الأجسام البسيطة، فلم يلزم أن يكون لها أشكال طبيعية كما زعموا، وأما المركبات فالظاهر أنها مقتضية للتناهي والتشكل، كما في أعضاء الحيوانات، وأغصان الأشجار بناء على القول بالإيجاب لا على القول بالاختيار. وقد اعترض الشارح في حاشيته لشرح التجريد فقال : ويرد عليه أن شكله يتوقف على تناهي الأبعاد ولا شك أن طبيعة الجسم لا يقتضي تناهي أبعاده وما يعرض للشيء بواسطة ليست مستندة إلى ذاته، لا يكون عارضاً له لذاته، فإن قلت: هذا بعينه وارد في المكان أيضاً، لأن حصوله فيه موقوف على وجود المكان الذي لا يستند إلى ذات الجسم . قلت : وجود الجسم لا يتصور في غير مكان عند القائل بأنه البعد فوجود المكان من لوازم وجوده من حيث هو بخلاف تناهي الأبعاد فإنه ليس من لوازم وجود الجسم من حيث هو ، لأن الواسطة إذا لم تستند إلى ذات الشيء، ولم تكن لازمة له كانت أمراً غريباً قطعاً بخلاف ما يستند إلى ذاته، أو ما يلزم من ذاته من حيث هو نعم لا شك في وروده على القول بأن المكان هو السطح، فإنه ليس لازماً لوجود الجسم، كما في المحدود، بل يتوقف على وجود جسم آخر، وهو أمر غريب إلى هاهنا كلامه، ويمكن أن يجاب عن بعضه، فتأمل.
Then the author mentioned here a general ruling for simple and composite bodies: that they have a shape, and that the natural shape of the simple is what? He said: 'And every body, whether simple or composite, has a natural shape, and that is due to its necessary finitude,' because the impossibility of infinite dimensions will come to you. 'So if the body, any body, is left with its nature' — by supposing it after its existence empty of what can be empty of external influences — 'a limit encompasses it,' i.e., one extremity. His saying: 'any body, composite or simple.' His saying: 'Every body has a natural shape and that due to its necessary finitude: every body either necessitates finitude of its dimensions, or necessitates infinity of its dimensions, or necessitates neither of them, and the accepted among these divisions is the third, and the first two are forbidden in simple bodies, so it does not follow that they have natural shapes as they claimed. As for composites, it is apparent that they necessitate finitude and shaping, as in animal organs and tree branches, based on the doctrine of necessitation, not on the doctrine of free choice. And the commentator objected in his gloss on Sharh al-Tajrid and said: It is objected to him that its shape depends on finite dimensions, and there is no doubt that the nature of a body does not necessitate finitude of its dimensions, and what occurs to a thing by means of a cause not originating from its essence is not an accident of it by itself. If you say: This same objection applies also to place, because its occurrence in place depends on the existence of place which does not originate from the essence of the body. I say: The existence of a body is inconceivable without place according to the one who says it is dimension, so the existence of place is a necessary concomitant of its existence as such, unlike finite dimensions, for it is not a necessary concomitant of the existence of a body as such, because the intermediate cause, if it does not originate from the essence of the thing and is not necessary for it, is certainly extraneous, unlike what originates from its essence or what is necessitated by its essence as such. Yes, there is no doubt that it applies to the doctrine that place is the surface, for it is not necessary for the existence of the body, as in the bounded, but depends on the existence of another body, which is extraneous. Up to here is his statement, and some of it can be answered, so reflect.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that every body has a natural shape, followed by objections and answers. The commentator first cites the author's claim: every body (simple or composite) has a natural shape due to its necessary finitude. The proof: if a body is left to its nature, free from external influences, it is necessarily finite (since infinite dimensions are impossible) and therefore has a limit, i.e., a shape. Then a marginal note clarifies that 'any body' includes composite and simple. A longer objection follows: the objector argues that the nature of a body does not necessitate finitude; the claimed necessity is not intrinsic. He divides bodies into three cases: necessitates finitude, necessitates infinity, or necessitates neither. He denies the first two for simple bodies, so their shape is not necessitated. For composites, shape may be necessitated but only on determinism (ijāb), not free choice. Then a specific objection from a gloss on Sharh al-Tajrid is quoted: shape depends on finite dimensions, but the nature of a body does not necessitate finite dimensions; what occurs via an extrinsic cause is not essential. The commentator replies that place (makān) is a necessary concomitant of body's existence (if place is dimension), unlike finitude. However, if place is a surface, then it is not necessary, so the objection stands. The passage ends by noting that some of it can be answered, inviting reflection.

Misconception: All bodies necessarily have a natural shape because they are finite.
Correction: The objection shows that finitude may not be intrinsic to the body's nature; for simple bodies, finitude is not necessitated by their essence, so shape is not a necessary property.
