Objection of Two Forms in the Heavens and the First Two Replies
Matn presenting objection of two forms and two replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that two specific forms cannot coexist in celestial bodies and how the first two replies refute it using the analogy of elemental composites and the precise definition of strong composition.

Core Idea
Two specific forms can coexist without strong composition.

This passage presents a key objection against the idea that celestial bodies can have multiple forms. The objector argues that two specific forms in one substance would lead to strong composition and contradict simplicity. The reply shows that in composites, elemental forms persist alongside a new form, so coexistence is possible. The second reply clarifies that strong composition requires different parts to have different powers, which is not the case here.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense theological style of the matn.
2
Identify the three sub-arguments of the objection
The objection consists of three distinct points: impossibility of two forms, resulting strong composition, and permissibility of multiple forms in all simples. Find each one.
3
Examine the first reply: analogy to elemental composites
The first reply denies impossibility by saying that in composites, elemental forms remain and a new specific form permeates. Understand how this shows coexistence is possible.
4
Examine the second reply: redefinition of strong composition
The second reply refines what strong composition means: different parts must have different powers. In the celestial sphere, the first form is universal, the second specific, so no multiplicity of powers.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these replies preserve the simplicity of the celestial sphere while allowing multiple forms. How does this affect the earlier claim about spherical shape?
This connects to the broader debate on celestial composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : مقدمة في تركيبة الجسم
أسباب تعود إلى الفواعل ، كما جاز استناده إلى أمور تعود إلى القوابل . لكن يبقى عليه أنه يلزم اجتماع صورتين نوعيتين في الكواكب والتدوير والخارج المركز، وهو محال وأنه إذا كان في الفلك صورتان كان فيه تركيب قوي وطبائع فلا يكون بسيطاً، وأنه إذا جاز أن يتصل بالفلك صور متعددة وهي مبادئ أفعال مختلفة جاز في سائر البسائط، فلا يلزم أن يكون شكلها مستديراً، وربما يدفع الأول بمنع استحالته، فإنه صور العناصر باقية في المركب وقد حل فيه صورة أخرى نوعية سارية في جميع أجزائه، وهي العناصر فيكون في كل عنصر هناك صورتان نوعيتان . والثاني بأن معنى التركيب القوي أن يكون لجزء من الجسم قوة ولجزء آخر منه قوة أخرى، حتى إذا كان له جزءان قويان كان له قوتان وليس الأمر في الفلك كذلك، إذ الصورة الأولى سارية في الكل والثانية مختصة ببعضه.
The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Introduction to the Composition of the Body. Causes that revert to the agents, just as its attribution to matters that revert to the recipients is permissible. But it remains against him that there necessarily follows the conjunction of two specific forms (ṣūratān nawʿiyyatān) in the planets, the epicycle (al-tadwīr), and the eccentric (al-khārij al-markaz), and that is impossible (muḥāl). And that if there are two forms in the celestial sphere (falak), then there would be a strong composition (tarkīb qawī) and natures, so it would not be simple (basīṭ). And that if it is permissible for multiple forms, which are principles of different actions, to attach to the celestial sphere, then it would be permissible in all other simple bodies, so it does not necessitate that their shape be spherical. And perhaps the first [objection] is refuted by denying its impossibility, for indeed the forms of the elements remain in the composite (murakkab), and another specific form has entered it, permeating all its parts, and they are the elements, so in each element there are two specific forms. And the second [objection is refuted] by [saying] that the meaning of strong composition is that a part of the body has a power and another part of it has another power, such that if it has two powerful parts, it has two powers, and the matter is not so in the celestial sphere, because the first form permeates the whole, and the second is specific to some of it.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is the matn (main text) presenting an objection against a previous claim about celestial bodies. The objector argues that positing multiple forms in planets, epicycles, and eccentrics leads to the impossibility of two specific forms coexisting in one substance, which would result in a strong composition and loss of simplicity. He further argues that if multiple forms were possible in celestial spheres, then all simple bodies could have varied forms, undermining the claim that spherical shape is necessary. The reply to the first objection denies its impossibility by analogizing with elemental composites: in a composite, the elemental forms persist alongside a new specific form that permeates the whole, so two specific forms coexist in each element without issue. The reply to the second objection refines the definition of strong composition: it requires that different parts have different powers, but in the celestial sphere, the first form is universal and the second is specific, so there is no multiplicity of powers across parts.

The Shaping Power in Simple vs Composite Matter
Presentation of third argument and its reply about shaping power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that a simple form necessitates a spherical shape, and how the shaping power's action in composite matter accounts for organic diversity.

Core Idea
A simple power in simple matter yields sphere; in composite matter, it yields diversity.

This passage examines the third argument that a simple form in a simple body can only produce a spherical shape. The counterexample of living beings with diverse limbs seems to challenge this, but the reply clarifies that the shaping power acts on composite matter with multiple recipients, allowing variation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to understand the flow.
Notice the structure: argument, counterexample, reply.
2
Identify the argument and counterexample
Find the claim that a single power in a simple matter produces only a spherical shape, and the counterexample of the shaping power producing diverse limb shapes.
The counterexample appears after 'الوجه (الثالث: الفاعل)'.
3
Understand the reply's logic
The reply states that the simple power acts on a composite matter with multiple recipients, so different effects are permissible. In a simple matter, the effect would be uniform.
The key phrase is 'في مركب من قوابل متعددة'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this shows that the nature of the matter (simple vs composite) influences the outcome, not just the power itself.

والثالث : بأن كل صورة تفرض في البسيط قوة واحدة تؤثر في مادة واحدة فلا تقتضي إلا شكلاً مستديراً. الوجه ( الثالث : الفاعل) عندهم ( لأشكال الأعضاء في الحيوان والنبات ومقاديرها في العظم والصغر وصفاتها من الملاسة ،والخشونة هي القوة المصورة وهي (قوة) واحدة بسيطة فعلها ألا ترى أنها لم تفد موادها شكل الكرة ، بل أشكالاً مختلفة. And the third [face]: because every form that is assumed in a simple body is a single power that acts on a single matter, so it necessitates only a spherical shape. The third face: the agent (al-fāʿil) according to them for the shapes of the limbs in animals and plants, their sizes in large and small, and their qualities of smoothness and roughness, is the shaping power (al-quwwa al-muṣawwira), and it is a single simple power. Its action – do you not see that it did not bestow upon its matters the shape of a sphere, but rather different shapes? And this is answered by them: that its action, i.e., the action of that simple power, in a composite (murakkab) from which the animal or plant is generated, and the variation of the effects of the simple power in a matter composed of multiple recipients is permissible, not in a simple matter.

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (ثلث) and its reply. The argument claims that any form in a simple body is a single power acting on a single matter, thus producing only a spherical shape. It then offers a counterexample: the shaping power in animals and plants produces diverse limb shapes despite being a single simple power. The reply answers that this power acts on a composite matter (murakkab) which has multiple recipients (qawābil), so different effects are permissible; but in a simple matter, the effect would be uniform. Thus, the original claim about simple bodies holds.

The Fourth Face: Variations in Epicycles and the Meaning of Uniform Action
Argument and reply on eccentric sphere thickness and uniform action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against celestial spheres being spherical and how the reply refines the concept of uniform action to mean uniformity in type rather than absolute identity.

Core Idea
Uniform action means same type, not exact sameness.

The discussion on the shape of celestial spheres continues with a fourth argument that points to eccentric spheres (al-aflāk al-khārija al-marākiz). These spheres have complementary parts that vary in thickness, yet a single nature is said to produce them. This seems to allow the nature to also produce different shapes. The reply clarifies what is meant by 'single action' and why variation in thickness does not undermine the uniformity of the type of action.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the first part of the passage which presents the fourth face: that eccentric spheres have varying thickness in their complementary parts, and a single nature produces different thicknesses, so it could also produce different shapes.
Notice how the argument uses analogy from thickness to shape.
2
Understand the reply
Read the reply: 'single action' means the action is similar in type, not absolutely identical. The examples given are surface, line, and point—they differ but belong to one type (geometrical entities).
The reply admits variation within a type.
3
Connect to the broader debate
Ask yourself: how does this reply defend the spherical shape? It argues that even if there is variation, the action remains one type (spherical shape), so minor variations do not break uniformity of type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الرابع: الأفلاك الخارجة المراكز كل من متمميها يختلف جانباه بالرقة والثخانة ) فقد فعلت الطبيعة الواحدة في كل من المتممين أفعالاً مختلفة في الثخن فيجوز أيضاً أن تختلف أفعالها في الشكل. وأجيب عن ذلك : بأن المراد بالفعل الواحد كما أومأنا إليه أن يكون متشابهاً غير مختلف بالنوع كالسطح والخط والنقطة، لا أنه لا يختلف أصلا . واختلاف الثخن والنقر أيضاً ، لا يوجب خروج فعل الطبيعة عن أن يكون نوعاً واحداً.
The fourth face: the eccentric celestial spheres (al-aflāk al-khārija al-marākiz), each of its two complementing parts (mutammimayn) – its two sides differ in thinness and thickness. For the single nature (al-ṭabīʿa al-wāḥida) has produced in each of the two complementing parts different actions in thickness, so it is also permissible for its actions to differ in shape. And it is answered about that: that what is meant by the single action, as we have indicated, is that it be similar, not differing in type (nawʿ), like the surface (saṭḥ), line (khaṭṭ), and point (nuqṭa); not that it does not differ at all. And the difference in thickness and indentation also does not necessitate the nature's action exiting from being one type.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (الوجه الرابع) and its reply. The argument points to eccentric spheres where the two complementary parts (e.g., the deferent and epicycle) have varying thickness (thin and thick) due to the nature's single action producing different thicknesses; by analogy, the nature could also produce different shapes. The reply clarifies that 'single action' means the action remains one in type (nawʿ), not that it is absolutely uniform without any variation. Examples are surface, line, and point which are different but belong to the same type (geometrical). Differences in thickness and indentation do not make the action leave its type; thus, the shape can still be fundamentally the same type (spherical) despite minor variations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single nature must produce exactly identical effects without any variation.
Correction: The reply shows that uniformity is at the level of type, not individual manifestations. Variation in thickness or shape can occur while still belonging to one type, like different geometrical figures.

Key Takeaway

The Spherical Shape of Simple Bodies and Water Surface
A corollary: water surface is a spherical segment centered on the world's center.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle that simple bodies have a natural spherical shape leads to the conclusion that water surfaces are spherical segments centered on the world's center, and how this explains varying water-holding capacity at different altitudes.

Core Idea
Water's surface is a spherical segment centered on the world's center.

This lesson explores a corollary from the principle that simple bodies naturally take a spherical shape. The author applies this to water, showing that its surface is not flat but a segment of a sphere centered on the world's center. This explains why vessels at lower altitudes hold more water than those at higher altitudes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial principle
The lesson builds on the idea that a simple body's natural shape is spherical. Review or accept this premise before examining its consequences.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the logical flow from the premise to the conclusion about water surface.
Notice the parenthetical explanations clarifying the center of the world.
3
Identify the key claim about water surface
The passage states that water surface, when left to its nature, is a segment of a spherical surface centered at the world's center.
4
Apply the claim to vessel capacity
Understand that the closer a vessel is to the world's center (lower altitude), the larger the segment of the sphere it can contain, leading to greater water-holding capacity.
Contrast with a vessel at a high altitude like a mountain top.
5
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider the causal chain: natural spherical shape of simple bodies → water surface spherical → dependence on distance from center.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فرع) على القول بأن الشكل الطبيعي للبسيط هو الكرة ) فالإناء كلما كان أقرب إلى المركز) أي مركز العالم الذي هو وسط الكل كما إذا كان في قعر بئر مثلاً ) كان أكثر احتمالاً للماء) مما إذا كان أبعد عنه كرأس جبل ) وذلك لأن ظاهر سطحه ) أي سطح الماء إذا خلي وطبعه في أي موضع فرض ( قطعة من دائرة ( بل من سطح كرة ( مركزها مركز العالم )
Branch: on the view that the natural shape of a simple body is the sphere, then a vessel, the closer it is to the center (i.e., the center of the world which is the middle of the whole, like when it is at the bottom of a well, for example), the more it can hold water than when it is farther away, like the top of a mountain. That is because the apparent surface of the water (i.e., the water's surface when left to its nature in any assumed place) is a segment of a circle, rather of a spherical surface, whose center is the center of the world.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a branch (farʿ) derived from the principle that the natural shape of a simple body is a sphere. It draws a consequence regarding water in vessels: the closer a vessel is to the world's center (the center of the universe), the more water it can hold, because the water surface naturally forms part of a sphere centered at the world's center. Thus, at lower altitudes (e.g., bottom of a well), the water surface is a larger segment of that sphere, allowing more water to be held compared to higher altitudes (e.g., mountain top) where the surface segment is smaller. The spherical curvature of the water surface is a direct manifestation of the simple body's natural spherical shape.

بسيط
كرة
مركز العالم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think water surface is flat.
Correction: According to this principle, water surface is a spherical section centered at the world's center, which explains varying water-holding capacity at different altitudes.

Key Takeaway

Definition and Existence of Direction (Jiha)
Jurjani defines jiha and proves its existence via sensory indication and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defines direction (jiha) and proves its existence using sensory indication and motion as key properties.

Core Idea
Direction exists because pure non-existence cannot be pointed to or moved towards.

In this passage, al-Jurjani establishes that direction is not a mere mental construct but has a real foundation. He defines it as the limit of sensory indication and the goal of motion, then argues that it must exist because pure non-being cannot serve as an object of pointing or as a destination. This lesson unpacks his reasoning, including the two interpretations that show how direction exists either in itself or in something else.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the full passage once to get the flow of Jurjani's argument. Notice how he moves from definition to proof to interpretation.
Pay attention to the two properties mentioned in the definition.
2
Identify the two properties of direction
From the definition, note that direction is (1) the limit of sensory indication and (2) the goal of motion. These are the basis for the proof.
3
Understand the proof and its implications
Reflect on why pure non-existence cannot have these properties. Then see how the two interpretations (outer sphere and lunar sphere) show that direction exists either in itself or in another, refuting the objection about 'down' direction.
This step connects the abstract proof to concrete examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجهة منتهى الإشارة الحسية ومقصد المتحرك الأيني بالحصول فيه، وذلك أن العقلاء يشيرون إشارة حسية إلى الجهات ويقولون: تحرك كذا في جهة كذا، فقد تعلق الإشارة الحسية بالجهة، وصارت أيضاً مقصداً للحركة المستقيمة، فهي موجودة لامتناع أن يكون العدم المحض كذلك، أي متعلق الإشارة الحسية ومقصد المتحرك بالوصول إليه. وقد فسروا بتفسيرين: مبنى الأول على العلو محدب الفلك الأعظم، ومبنى الثاني على مقعر فلك القمر. والنتيجة أن الجهة موجودة؛ إما في نفسها أو في غيرها، ومعنى وجودها كون الغير في الخارج بحيث تنتزع تلك الجهة منه، فلا يرد أن جهة السفل، أعني المركز ليست بموجودة في الخارج.
Direction is the limit of sensory indication and the goal of a moving body in terms of obtaining in it, because rational people point sensorily to directions and say: so-and-so moved in such a direction. So sensory indication is attached to direction, and it also becomes the goal of rectilinear motion. Therefore it is existent, because pure non-existence cannot be like that—i.e., the object of sensory indication and the goal of the mover by reaching it. They interpreted it in two ways: the first is based on the height of the convexity of the outermost sphere, and the second on the concavity of the sphere of the moon. The conclusion is that direction exists either in itself or in something else, and the meaning of its existence is that the other exists externally such that that direction can be abstracted from it, so it is not objected that the direction of below, i.e., the center, does not exist externally.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines direction (jiha) and proves its existence. The author first states that direction is the limit of sensory indication and the goal of a moving body's motion towards it. He then gives an example: people point to directions and say something moved in a certain direction, showing that sensory indication attaches to direction and that it is the goal of motion. From these two properties, he argues that direction must exist, because pure non-existence (al-ʿadam al-maḥḍ) cannot be the object of sensory indication or the goal of motion. The interpretation of the two properties leads to the conclusion that direction exists either in itself (like the outermost sphere) or in something else (like the center from which downward direction is abstracted). Thus the objection that 'down' (the center) is not externally existent is refuted.

Correction: The passage argues that direction must exist externally because it is the object of sensory indication and the goal of motion, which pure non-existence cannot be.

Second Objection: Sensory Indication as Imaginary and the Answer
Jurjani refutes the objection that sensory indication is imaginary, reaffirming direction's reality and materiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that sensory indication is imaginary and how Jurjani responds by affirming that direction is real and material.

Core Idea
Even an imaginary pointer points to a real endpoint.

Jurjani now tackles another challenge to the reality of direction: the claim that sensory indication is purely imaginary. If the act of pointing is imagined, how can its limit be real? In this lesson, you'll see the rebuttal that transforms the objection into a proof of direction's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objector argues, then Jurjani responds with 'لأنا نقول'.
Pay attention to the logical flow from objection to conclusion.
2
Identify the objector's argument
The objector claims that sensory indication is an imaginary extension, so its endpoint (direction) would also be imaginary and nonexistent. Understand this premise.
3
Analyze Jurjani's response
Jurjani grants that the extension is imagined, but insists we know necessarily that its endpoint exists externally. This is a key move: necessary knowledge overcomes the imaginary nature.
The response does not deny the premise but adds a stronger epistemological claim.
4
Understand the conclusion
Jurjani concludes that direction has position (dhū waḍʿ), meaning it is material (māddī), not abstract. He reasons that an abstract entity cannot be the object of sensory pointing or the goal of motion.
This links direction to physical reality, refuting the objection.
5
Reflect on the argument's significance
Consider why this objection and answer matter for the broader proof of direction. The objection attempts to undermine the empirical basis, but the answer reinforces it.
This lesson shows how kalam addresses objections by refining concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً الإشارة الحسية امتداد موهوم فلا يكون منتهاها موجوداً، لأنا نقول: إن الإشارة الحسية وإن كانت امتداداً موهوماً لكنا نعلم بالضرورة أن منتهى هذا الامتداد مشار إليه وموجود في الخارج. ولا شك أنها شيء ذو وضع أي مادي لا مجرد، لأن المفارق المجرد عن المادة تمتنع الإشارة الحسية إليه ويمتنع أيضاً أن يكون مقصداً للمتحرك بالحصول فيه.
And also sensory indication is an imaginary extension, so its limit would not be existent. For we say: Sensory indication, even if it is an imaginary extension, we know necessarily that the limit of this extension is pointed to and existent externally. And there is no doubt that it is something having position (dhū waḍʿ), i.e., material, not abstract, because the abstract separate from matter cannot be pointed to sensorily, nor can it be the goal of a moving body in terms of obtaining in it.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objector argues that sensory indication is an imaginary extension, so its limit (direction) would also be imaginary and non-existent. The answer replies that even if the extension is imaginary, we know by necessity that its endpoint is actually pointed to and exists externally. The author then concludes that direction must have position (dhū waḍʿ), meaning it is material, not abstract. This is because an abstract entity (al-mufāriq al-mujarrad) cannot be the object of sensory indication or the goal of attainment for motion. Thus direction is physical, not purely mental.

The Indivisibility of Direction and Its Nature as Boundaries
Argument that direction is indivisible and a boundary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that direction (al-jiha) is indivisible and must be a boundary (point, line, or surface) of a body, including the logical disjunction and the refutation of the objection that motion could be 'in' the direction.

Core Idea
Direction is not a divisible body but a boundary of the body.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles a fundamental question: Is direction itself divisible? He argues that it is not, through a careful logical disjunction. He then concludes that direction must be a boundary (point, line, or surface) and addresses a potential objection. This argument is crucial for understanding the nature of spatial relations in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim and disjunction
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi states that direction does not divide in the source of pointing and the extension of motion, and then presents a disjunction: if it did divide, then either the pointing/motion ends at the nearest part, making that part the whole direction, or it does not end there, making what is beyond the direction. Either way, the direction is not composed of both parts.
This is the core argument against divisibility.
2
Understand the objection
After the disjunction, an objector says that it is possible that the motion is 'in' the direction but not 'towards' it, so the nearest part could still be a part. This would break the disjunction. Grasp this nuance.
3
Examine the answer
The answer argues that if motion is 'in' the direction, then the direction would be a distance traversed, not a term towards which one is going. This violates the very definition of direction. Hence, the objection fails.
The answer preserves the indivisibility.
4
Analyze the conclusion
Once indivisibility is established, Sanusi concludes that direction must be boundaries (nihaayaat, hudoore) of bodies: points, lines, or surfaces. Reflect on why this follows – because if it were a body, it would be divisible.
This step connects the first part to the definition of direction as a limit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم (الحصول فيه أي حصول الجسم في المفارق والوصول إلى القرب منه. (و) لا شك أيضاً في ( أنها ) أي الجهة ( لا تنقسم في مأخذ الإشارة وامتداد الحركة ( وإلا ) أي وإن انقسمت في ذلك المأخذ والامتداد ) فالجهة أحد جزأيها ) لا هي بتمامها ) فإنا إذا فرضنا الإشارة أو الحركة اتفقت) أي وصلت إلى جزئها الأقرب فإن انتهت ) هناك الإشارة أو الحركة إلى تلك الجهة ( فهو ) أي ذلك الجزء الأقرب وحده هو ( الجهة دون ما وراءه أي لا مدخل له في تلك الجهة ) وإلا ) أي وإن لم تنته هناك الإشارة أو الحركة إلى تلك الجهة ) فالجهة ما وراءه دونه ( فإن قيل : ليس يلزم من عدم الانتهاء عند الجزء الأقرب أن لا يكون هو جزءاً من الجهة لجواز أن تكون تلك الإشارة أو الحركة الباقية في الجهة لا إليها . أجيب بأن هذا ينافي ماهية الجهة لأنها ما إليها الإشارة ،والحركة فلو كانتا في الجهة كانت الجهة مسافة لا جهة، وأنه محال وإذا ثبت أن الجهة موجودة في الخارج، وأنها ذات وضع وغير منقسمة في امتداد الإشارة واستقامة الحركة (فهي) أي الجهة (نهايات وحدود) أي أطراف، هي أعراض قائمة بالأجسام، لأنها إن لم تنقسم أصلاً كانت نقطاً، وإن انقسمت في امتداد واحد كانت خطوطاً ، أو في امتدادين كانت سطوحاً ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم تكن نهايات وأطرافاً، بل كانت أجساماً ( لكانت ( الجهة أمراً ( متحيزاً بالاستقلال فكان منقسماً ) في الامتدادات كلها لما مر من امتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وما في حكمه

قوله : ( فإن قيل إلخ ) يعني أن القسمة غير حاصرة لأنه يجوز أن يكون الحركة إلخ . قوله : ( أجيب إلخ ) إبطال للقسم الثالث لكن بعد إبطاله يثبت عدم انقسام الجهة من غير حاجة إلى الترديد السابق فكان جواباً بتغيير الدليل لا بإثبات المقدمة الممنوعة . قوله : (فهي نهايات وأطراف فهي بالنسبة إلى الإشارة والحركة جهة وبالنسبة إلى الامتداد طرف ونهاية . قوله: بل كانت أجساماً الصواب بل قائمة بذواتها لئلا يلزم استدراك قوله : لكانت متحيزاً بالاستقلال وقوله : لما مر من امتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ وما في حكمه فتدير.
First Observation - Second Chapter: First Section: Second Objective: On the Directions of the Body. (Its occurrence means the occurrence of the body in the separated [i.e., the direction] and the arrival to nearness of it. (And) there is no doubt also that (it) i.e., the direction (does not divide in the source of pointing and the extension of motion. (Otherwise) i.e., if it divided in that source and extension) then the direction is one of its two parts, not its entirety. For if we suppose the pointing or motion (agreed) i.e., arrived at its nearest part, then if it ended) there the pointing or motion at that direction (then it is) i.e., that nearest part alone is (the direction without what is beyond it, i.e., there is no entry for what is beyond in that direction. (Otherwise) i.e., if the pointing or motion did not end at that direction) then the direction is what is beyond it without it. (If it is said: It is not necessary from the non-ending at the nearest part that it should not be a part of the direction, because it is possible that that pointing or motion remains in the direction not towards it. The answer is that this contradicts the essence of direction because it is that towards which pointing and motion occur; so if they were in the direction, then the direction would be a distance not a direction, and that is impossible. And when it is established that direction exists externally, and that it has position and is not divided in the extension of pointing and straightness of motion, then (it is) i.e., the direction (boundaries and limits) i.e., extremities, which are accidents subsisting in bodies. Because if it does not divide at all, it would be points; and if it divides in one extension, it would be lines; or in two extensions, it would be surfaces. (Otherwise) i.e., if it were not boundaries and extremities, but were bodies) then (the direction would be something (occupying space independently, and would be divisible) in all extensions due to what has passed of the impossibility of the indivisible part and what is in its ruling.

He says: (As for the saying, etc.) means that the division is not exhaustive because it is possible that the motion etc. He says: (The answer, etc.) is an invalidation of the third division, but after its invalidation, the non-division of direction is established without need for the previous disjunction, so it was an answer by changing the proof not by establishing the precluded premise. He says: (So they are boundaries and extremities) so they are with respect to pointing and motion, direction, and with respect to extension, edge and limit. He says: (Rather they were bodies) the correct form is 'rather standing themselves' so that there is no need for the statement: 'they would be occupying space independently' and his statement: 'due to what has passed of the impossibility of the indivisible part and what is in its ruling' so reflect.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a proof that direction (al-jiha) is not divisible and must be a boundary of the body. The argument proceeds: If direction were divisible in the reference of pointing and the extension of motion, then it would have parts. A pointing or motion would either end at the nearest part (making that part the entire direction) or not end there (making what is beyond the direction). This disjunction shows that direction cannot be divided because either way the other part is excluded, contradicting the assumption that direction includes both parts. An objection is raised: it is possible that the motion is in the direction but not towards it, so the nearest part could still be a part. The answer: this violates the definition of direction as that towards which pointing and motion are directed; if motion is in it, it becomes a distance not a direction. So direction must be indivisible in the sense of pointing and motion, and thus it must be a boundary (nihaya) or limit (hadd) of the body, not a body itself. The commentary by the author elaborates on the objection and answer, clarifying the logic, and also corrects a textual issue: rather than saying 'bodies', the correct expression is 'subsisting themselves'.

Direction Cannot Be in Void or Homogeneous Matter
Proves that direction cannot be in void or homogeneous infinite matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why direction cannot exist in the void or in homogeneous infinite matter, and therefore must be a boundary of finite bodies.

Core Idea
Direction cannot be in the void or in infinite homogeneous matter—both are impossible.

In this passage, the author continues the argument about the nature of direction. He proposes two alternatives if direction is not a boundary of finite bodies: it must be either part of the void or part of homogeneous infinite matter. Both options are shown to be impossible, thereby confirming that direction is indeed a boundary of finite bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the structure of the argument and the key terms.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents that if direction is not boundaries, it must be either part of the void or part of homogeneous infinite matter. Identify these two options.
The void (khala') is abstract dimension; homogeneous matter (mala' mutashabih) is infinite body.
3
Understand why each is impossible
The void is impossible in both its senses (existing and imagined) to contain direction. Homogeneous matter cannot have distinct real directions because all parts are similar, so no natural seeking or fleeing.
4
Conclude the argument
Since both alternatives are impossible, direction must be boundaries of finite bodies. This is the logical conclusion.
This aligns with the author's earlier proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأيضاً فلو لم تكن الفاء زائدة والأظهر الأخصر أن يقرر هكذا لو لم تكن الجهة حدوداً وأطرافاً قائمة بالجسم لكانت أجزاء منه، إذ لا يجوز أن يكون جسماً لما مر من امتناع انقسامه في مأخذ الحركة، فأما أن يكون جزءاً من الخلاء أي البعد المجرد ومن الملأ المتشابه، أي الغير المتناهي، إذ جهات المتناهي أطراف ونهاية لأنها منتهى الإشارة ومقصد المتحرك، ولما كان جزء الخلاء خلاء أو جزء الملاء ملا، فإما الخلاء وإما الملا، ويؤيده اكتفاء المصنف بإبطال كون أحد جزئيه أولى من الآخر، ولم يتعرض لإبطال كونها الحدود وزاده الشارح لإتمام الاستدلال بناء على ما حرره . وقد بان بطلانه بما عرفت من استحالة انقسامها في مأخذ الإشارة وامتداد الحركة وأيضاً فلو لم تكن الجهة ( حدوداً مختلفة الحقائق قائمة بأجسام متناهية (فإما الخلاء أي فهي إما في الخلاء الذي هو البعد الموجود أو الموهوم ( وإنه ) أي الخلاء بكلا معنييه ( محال ) فكيف يتصور وجود الجهة فيه ) أو الملأ المتشابه ) أي أو هي في الملأ الذي لا يوجد فيه حدود مختلفة الحقائق، وهو الجسم الذي لا يتناهى ( فلا يكون ( هناك جهات متخالفة الماهية إذ لا يكون (أحد) جزأيه) أي جزأي الملاء المتشابه مطلوباً بالطبع، والآخر متروكاً بالطبع لأنهما متشابهان في الماهية، وكذلك الحدود المفروضة فيه لا تكون جهات موجودة متخالفة، فلا يتصور طلب بعض الأجسام بالطبع لبعضها، وهربه عن بعض آخر منها .
He says: (And also, if it were not that the letter fa' is redundant, and the clearer and shorter is to state thus: If direction were not boundaries and extremities subsisting in the body, it would be parts of it, for it is not permissible that it be a body due to what has passed of the impossibility of its division in the source of motion. So either it is part of the void (khala') i.e., abstract dimension, or part of the homogeneous (mala') i.e., the infinite, for the directions of the finite are extremities and boundaries because they are the terminus of pointing and the goal of the moving thing. And since a part of the void is void, or a part of the homogeneous is homogeneous, so it is either void or homogeneous. And it is supported by the author's sufficing with invalidating that one of its two parts is more deserving than the other, and he did not address invalidating that they are boundaries, and the commentator added it to complete the argument based on what he set down. And it has been shown to be false by what you know of the impossibility of its division in the source of pointing and the extension of motion. And also, if direction were not (boundaries different in reality, subsisting in finite bodies) then it is either the void i.e., it is in the void which is the existing or imagined dimension (and it i.e., the void in both its senses is impossible, so how can direction be imagined to exist in it?) or the homogeneous (mala') i.e., or it is in the homogeneous in which there are no boundaries different in reality, and it is the body that is infinite (so there can be no) directions different in essence, for (one of its two parts) i.e., the two parts of the homogeneous matter is not naturally sought, and the other naturally left, because they are similar in essence. Likewise, the boundaries supposed in it would not be existing directions different [in reality], so the natural seeking of some bodies for others and flight from others cannot be imagined.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage continues the argument about the nature of direction. It first presents a further argument: if direction were not a boundary subsisting in the body, then it would have to be either part of the void (khala') or part of homogeneous infinite matter (mala' mutashabih). Both are impossible: the void cannot contain direction because it is impossible in itself, and homogeneous infinite matter cannot have distinct real directions because all its parts are similar, so no natural seeking or fleeing could occur. The commentary explains that the author's proof implies that direction must be a boundary of finite bodies, and the commentator adds further clarification.

?
The passage argues that direction requires a finite boundary. In your relationship with Allah, do you acknowledge that your spiritual direction is bounded by His commands and prohibitions? Or do you imagine an infinite, undefined space where anything goes?

The Encompassing Sphere: True Determiner of Directions
Refutes lunar sphere as direction determiner and proves encompassing sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the lunar sphere cannot determine true directions and how the encompassing sphere establishes the concepts of above and below.

Core Idea
True directions require a single encompassing sphere, not partial or paired bodies.

In this passage, the author dismantles the idea that the lunar sphere can assign directions to bodies, then builds the case for an encompassing sphere that alone defines above and below. By following the logical refutation and the positive proof, you will see why a single outer sphere is necessary for the notion of true direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: first a refutation, then a positive proof.
The passage is from al-Mawaqif; it uses technical terms like فلك القمر and كرة محيطة.
2
Understand the refutation of the lunar sphere
The author rejects the claim that the lunar sphere (or a pair of bodies) determines directions. He gives two reasons: directions would have to be prior to the bodies, and only proximity (not distance) would be determined. Work through these points.
The key flaw: the lunar sphere and Earth cannot define 'where' distance lies.
3
Grasp the positive argument for the encompassing sphere
The author then asserts that what is truly established is the existence of a sphere encompassing all bodies. Its surface marks above, its center marks below. This solves the problem of direction determination.
The phrase 'فقد ثبت' signals the positive conclusion.
4
Review the key terms and the misconception
Review the key Arabic terms (جهات, فوق, تحت, etc.) and the common misunderstanding that the lunar sphere could determine directions. The passage guards against this error.
5
Try the checkpoint question
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your understanding of why the lunar sphere fails.
The question is answerable only from this passage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم فساد ما قيل من أن فلك القمر يحدد جهات الأجسام القابلة للحركة المستقيمة ) أو لا يحيط ) بعضها ببعض ( بل يكون كل منهما خارجاً واقعاً ( في جهة من الآخر فتكون الجهة متحدة قبلهما حتى يمكن وقوعهما فيها (لا) متحددة (بهما والمفروض خلافه وأيضاً فلا يتحدد بشيء منهما إلا جهة القرب دون البعد كما فإن البعد عن الجسم إذا كان خارجاً عنه، فالبعد عنه إلى أين ( فقد ثبت ) بما قررناه ( وجود كرة بها تتحدد الجهات الحقيقية ( محيطة بالكل) أي بجميع الأجسام ليكون سطحه الأعلى منتهى الإشارات وجهة الفوق ومركزه الذي يتساوى بعده عنه، وتنتهي به الإشارة النازلة عنه جهة التحت ) وهو المطلوب
The First Observation - Chapter Two: First Section: Second Goal: On the directions of the body. The corruption of what was said that the lunar sphere determines the directions of bodies capable of rectilinear motion, or that one surrounds the other, but each is external, occurring in a direction from the other, so the direction is unified before them so that their occurrence in it is possible, not determined by them, contrary to what is supposed. And also, nothing is determined by either of them except the direction of proximity, not distance, so where is the distance? It has been established, by what we have reasoned, the existence of a sphere by which the true directions are determined, encompassing the whole, i.e., all bodies, so that its upper surface is the limit of upward indications and the direction of above, and its center which is equidistant from it, and the downward indication ends at it, the direction of below. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific claim and establishes the correct determiner of true directions. The claim under attack is that the lunar sphere determines the directions of bodies capable of rectilinear motion. The refutation proceeds as follows: the lunar sphere and the Earth are described either as one surrounding the other or as both external to each other. If both are external, each lies in a direction from the other, and the direction would have to be unified before them to allow their occurrence in it—yet it is not determined by them, contrary to the assumption. Moreover, only the direction of proximity is determined, not the direction of distance, leaving the question 'where is the distance?' unanswered. The passage then asserts that what is truly established is the existence of an encompassing sphere that determines true directions. This sphere encloses all bodies; its upper surface marks the extreme of upward indications (above), and its center marks the endpoint of downward indications (below). This is the desired conclusion.

فوق (above)
تحت (below)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lunar sphere (or a pair of bodies like the Earth and the lunar sphere) can determine true directions for bodies.
Correction: The passage shows that direction must be prior to any two bodies external to each other, and only a single encompassing sphere can define both proximity and distance, i.e., true above and below.

The Famous Question on Direction Unity
Commentator explains and defends direction unity against a famous objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous objection to the direction unity argument and how the commentator refutes it.

Core Idea
Directions cannot be relative; they must be real and unified.

This passage delves into a classic theological debate about the nature of directions and whether they are determined by bodies or exist independently. The commentator explains the main text's claim that direction is unified before bodies and then addresses a pointed objection that tries to undermine this unity. Understanding this exchange is key to grasping the foundational assumptions about space and direction in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once along with the translation. Notice how the commentator first explains the text and then responds to an objection.
2
Identify the structure
The passage has two main sections: an explanation of direction unity, and a famous question with its response. Mark where each begins.
The question starts with 'وهاهنا سؤال مشهور'.
3
Analyze the famous question
The question concedes that only direction of proximity is determined, but asks why it cannot differ in kind between two bodies (one sought, one fled). Understand the challenge.
This would undermine the claim that direction is unified before bodies.
4
Examine the commentator's response
The commentator gives multiple arguments: the question about distance is irrelevant; the earlier analogy with sphere circumference is denied; and the objection assumes a principle of straight inclination that is not granted.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does this matter? The commentator defends the original position that direction is real and prior to bodies, not merely relative.
This is a key move in establishing the foundation for arguments about space and bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فتكون إلخ ) لأنه لا بد لكل منهما من حيز طبيعي يطلبه ويهرب عن حيز الآخر، فيكون الحيزان في جهتين حقيقتين لا يتبدلان بالاعتبار. قوله : ( فلا يتحدد بشيء منهما إلا جهة القرب ) وهاهنا سؤال مشهور وهو أنا سلمنا أنه لا يتحدد بشيء منهما إلا جهة القرب، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون جهة القرب من أحدهما مخالفاً بالنوع بجهة القرب في الآخر فيكون أحدهما مطلوباً بالطبع والآخر مهروباً بالطبع أو بالعكس؟ وأما قوله : فالبعد إلى أين قلنا : إنما يسأل عن البعد إذا كان جهة البعد مطلوباً للأجسام بالطبع عنه، كذلك وكلاهما ممنوعان هاهنا فلا عبرة بوجوده ولا بعدمه، وإنما ذكره آنفاً في قوله : فيكون الجهة متحددة قبلهما فهو أيضاً ممنوع، فإنكم لما جوزتم أن يتحدد جهة الفوق بمحيط كرة واحدة، ويتحدد جهة السفل بمركزها ولا يلزم هناك أن يكون الجهة قبلها، لزمكم جواز أن يتحدد جهة الفوق بمحيط أحد الجسمين وجهة التحت بمحيط الجسم الآخر ولا يلزم هناك أيضاً أن يكون الجهة قبلهما ولا يلزم ذلك أن لو كان في ذاتهما مبدأ ميل مستقيم وهو ممنوع.
His statement: (so the direction is unified etc.) because each of them necessarily has a natural place that it seeks and flees from the place of the other, so the two places are in two true directions that do not change by consideration. His statement: (nothing is determined by either of them except the direction of proximity) and here there is a famous question: we concede that nothing is determined by either of them except the direction of proximity, but why is it not permissible that the direction of proximity from one of them is different in kind from the direction of proximity from the other, so that one is sought by nature and the other fled from by nature, or the opposite? As for his statement: 'so where is the distance?' we say: he only asks about distance if the direction of distance is sought by bodies by nature from it, and both are denied here, so there is no regard for its existence or non-existence. And he only mentioned it earlier in his statement: 'so the direction is unified before them'—that too is denied. For when you allowed that the direction of above is determined by the circumference of a single sphere, and the direction of below is determined by its center, and it is not necessary there that the direction be before them, then you must allow that the direction of above is determined by the circumference of one of the two bodies and the direction of below by the circumference of the other body, and it is also not necessary there that the direction be before them, and that does not require that in themselves there be a principle of straight inclination—and that is denied.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining a portion of the main text and then addressing a famous objection. The first part explains the statement 'so the direction is unified before them' from the main argument. The commentator clarifies that each of the two bodies (the lunar sphere and the Earth) must have a natural place it seeks and flees from the other's place, so the two places lie in two true directions that are not subject to mere consideration. The second part discusses the statement 'nothing is determined by either of them except the direction of proximity.' A famous question is raised: even if we grant that only the direction of proximity is determined, why cannot the direction of proximity from one body be different in kind from that of the other, making one naturally sought and the other fled? The commentator answers that the question about 'where is the distance?' only applies if the direction of distance is naturally sought, but both directions (proximity and distance) are denied here, so there is no basis. Furthermore, the earlier claim that 'the direction is unified before them' is also denied: if one allows that above is determined by the circumference of one sphere and below by its center, then it should be possible for above to be determined by the circumference of one body and below by the circumference of the other, without requiring the direction to be before them. This does not necessitate a principle of straight inclination in the bodies themselves, and that is denied.

حيز طبيعي
جهة القرب
جهة البعد
سؤال مشهور
مبدأ ميل مستقيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for directions of proximity to differ in nature between bodies (one sought, one fled).
Correction: Directions are real and unified; they cannot be opposite in kind because the objection relies on an unproven principle of straight inclination.

Checkpoint

B) It might be physically different in kind from one body to another.
C) It is determined by the encompassing sphere.
D) It does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The commentator defends the unity of direction by showing that the famous objection fails to prove that directions can differ in nature.

?
The objection suggests that directions of proximity might differ in nature. Do you treat your closeness to Allah as something that can be defined by your own preferences, or do you submit to the single true direction He has set? What would it mean for you to be like the body that naturally seeks its place without hesitation—to seek Allah with the same inevitability?

The Determiner Must Be Simple – The Core Proof
Proves simplicity of the determiner via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the encompassing sphere (the determiner of directions) cannot be composite, because compositeness would permit disintegration, which requires rectilinear motion that presupposes predetermined directions – contradicting its role.

Core Idea
Composite implies possible disintegration, disintegration requires rectilinear motion, but rectilinear motion needs directions before the determiner – impossible.

The dialogue begins with the claim that the determiner of all directions (the outermost celestial sphere) must be simple, i.e., not made of multiple simple parts. This lesson presents the central argument: if it were composite, each of its parts would contact different things by different sides. A simple body, however, can contact the same thing by either end due to the equality of its ends. This equality would only allow such contact if the composite disintegrates. Disintegration requires rectilinear motion, which in turn requires that directions be established before the determiner itself – a contradiction. Therefore, the determiner cannot be composite and must be simple.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the logical flow: if composite then possible disintegration, which leads to rectilinear motion requiring predetermined directions.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the implication
Notice how the author establishes that compositeness leads to the possibility of disintegration, and that this possibility is enough to falsify the antecedent.
Kalam often uses such conditional arguments.
3
Understand the contradiction
Recognize that the consequent (disintegration via rectilinear motion) implies that directions are determined before the determiner, which is impossible because the determiner is supposed to define directions. This contradiction proves simplicity.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم له أحكام منها أنه بسيط لا مركب من بسائط متعددة ) وإلا جاز انحلاله واللازم باطل) فالملزوم مثله ( أما الملزومية فلان ( المحدد إذا كان مركباً من بسائط متعددة كان كل واحد من أجزائه ملاقياً بأحد جانبيه شيئاً غير ما يلاقيه بجانبه الآخر، ولا شك أن البسيط يمكنه أن يلاقي بأحد طرفيه ما يلاقيه بالآخر لتساويهما) أي تساوي الطرفين في الماهية فإذا لاقى أحدهما شيئاً جاز أن يلاقيه الآخر، وذلك إنما يتصور بالانحلال . ( وأما بطلان اللازم فلأن ذلك ) أي الانحلال ) لا يكون إلا بالحركة المستقيمة وتباعد بعض الأجزاء عن بعض . وقد يقال : جاز أن تكون الملاقاة بالحركة المستديرة، فلا يلزم الانحلال المستلزم للحركة المستقيمة ( وهي ) أعني الحركة المستقيمة ( لا تكون إلا من جهة إلى جهة ) أخرى ( فتكون الجهة متحددة قبله ) أي قبل المحدد حتى يمكن حركة أجزائه إليها ( لا ) متحددة به هذا خلف
قوله : ( وإلا جاز إلخ ) يمكن أن يعارض بأنه لو كان بسيطاً لجاز عليه الانحلال واللازم باطل بيان الملازمة لأنه لو كان بسيطا يساوي محدبه ومقعره في الماهية، ويجوز أن يكون ما يماس محدبه ما يماس مقعره وما ذلك إلا بالانحلال والحمل أن الجسم مطلقة مقتضى كل مكان، وبعد فرض الأجزاء الكل من الجسم والحيز يحصل لكل واحد من أجزاء الجسم والحيز خصوصية، فيجوز أن يقتضي خصوصية كل جزء من الجسم خصوصة كل جزء من الحيز. قوله : ( بأحد جانبيه إلخ ) أي بأحد مما جربه لأن التساوي في المائة للأجزاء لا للأطراف . قوله : ( أن البسيط يمكنه أن يلاقي بأحد طرفيه ما يلاقيه كلمة ما مع صلتها مفعول لقوله : أن يلاقي والضمير المستتر في قوله : يلاقي أو البارز في قوله طرفيه أو المستتر في قوله : يلاقيه راجع إلى البسيط المذكور والضمير المنصوب البارز في قوله : يلاقيه راجع إلى ما، وفي هذا المقام منع وهو أن يقال : الإمكان المذكور وإن كان مسلماً بالنسبة إلى البسائط لكنه ممنوع بالنسبة إلى المجموع المركب من تلك البسائط، فإنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون طبيعة الكل مانعة عن حركة البسائط بوجه ما أصلا. قوله : ( وقد يقال : جاز أن يكون إلخ ) فيه بحث فإنه لا شك أن بعض الأجزاء البسيطة حينئذ كان قريباً من السطح الأعلى لذلك الفلك المركب، وأن البعض الآخر من تلك الأجزاء كان قريباً من السطح الأسفل، لذلك الفلك أيضاً، وظاهر أن ما بين السطحين المذكورين بون بعيد بین هذا البسيط الأقرب من السطح الأسفل وبين ذلك البسيط الأقرب من السطح الأعلى إلا بالحركة المستقيمة كما لا يخفى . وأنه لا يتصور الملاقاة
Then it has rulings, among them that it is simple, not composed of multiple simples; otherwise, its disintegration would be possible, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. As for the implication (that if composite then disintegration is possible), it is because if the determiner were composed of multiple simples, each of its parts would come into contact with something different by one side than by its other side, and without doubt the simple can come into contact with one of its ends with what it contacts by the other, because of their equality—meaning the equality of the two ends in essence; so if it contacts one thing, it is possible that it contacts the other, and that is only conceivable by disintegration. As for the falsity of the consequent, it is because that disintegration does not occur except by rectilinear motion and the separation of some parts from others. And it may be said: it is possible that the contact be by circular motion, so the disintegration which entails rectilinear motion is not necessary. And it (rectilinear motion) does not occur except from one direction to another direction, so the direction is determined before it (the determiner) so that the motion of its parts towards it is possible, not determined by it—this is contradictory.
His statement: (otherwise it would be possible, etc.) can be countered by: if it were simple, disintegration would be possible for it, and the consequent is false. The explanation of the implication: because if it were simple, its convex and concave would be equal in essence, and it is possible that what touches its convex touches its concave, and that is only by disintegration. And the assumption is that the body absolutely requires every place, and after positing the parts of the whole body and the place, a particularity occurs for each part of the body and the place, so it is possible that the particularity of each part of the body demands the particularity of each part of the place. His statement: (by one of its sides, etc.) means by one of its two sides, because equality in the thing (essence) is for the parts, not for the extremities. His statement: (the simple can come into contact by one of its ends with what it contacts by the other) —the word 'what' with its clause is the object of 'to contact', and the hidden pronoun in 'contacts' or the visible pronoun in 'its ends' or the hidden pronoun in 'contacts it' refers back to the mentioned simple, and the visible accusative pronoun in 'contacts it' refers back to 'what'. And in this context there is a denial: it is said that the mentioned possibility, although conceded with respect to simples, is denied with respect to the whole composed of those simples, because why is it not possible that the nature of the whole prevents the motion of the simples in any way at all? His statement: (and it may be said: it is possible that the contact be, etc.)—in this there is an investigation: for there is no doubt that some of the simple parts then would be close to the upper surface of that composite sphere, and the other parts would be close to the lower surface of that sphere, and it is apparent that between the two mentioned surfaces there is a great distance between this simple part closest to the lower surface and that simple part closest to the upper surface except by rectilinear motion, as is not hidden. And contact is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage proves that the determiner of directions (the encompassing sphere) is simple, i.e., not composed of multiple simple parts. The argument proceeds as follows: If it were composite, then each part would contact something different by one side than by the other side. A simple body, by contrast, can contact the same thing by either end because its ends are equal in essence. That equality would allow contact with the same object by either end, but that can only happen through disintegration of the composite. Disintegration requires rectilinear motion, which in turn requires that directions be predetermined before the determiner itself, leading to a contradiction (since the determiner is supposed to define directions). Therefore, the composite is impossible, so the determiner must be simple.
The commentary then addresses several objections and clarifications:
- A counter-objection: if the determiner were simple, it could also disintegrate (by equality of sides), but the reply is that disintegration requires rectilinear motion, which presupposes directions.
- Another objection: contact could be by circular motion, avoiding rectilinear motion. But the reply holds that even circular motion of parts would require some parts to move rectilinearly to achieve contact.
- Further, it is argued that even if simples can contact in the way described, the composite whole might prevent such motion due to its nature. The commentator then refutes this by noting that the simple parts would need to move rectilinearly to reach each other across the distance between the upper and lower surfaces.
The overall conclusion is that the determiner must be simple and not composite.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
انحلال
حركة مستقيمة
ملاقاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the determiner is simple, it cannot cause directions, but the proof focuses on compositeness.
Correction: The argument does not address simplicity directly; it shows compositeness is impossible, so the determiner must be simple by elimination.

Checkpoint
What is the main contradiction reached in the proof?
A) Simple bodies cannot disintegrate.
B) Disintegration requires rectilinear motion, which needs directions before the determiner.
C) Circular motion allows disintegration without rectilinear motion.
D) The determiner cannot be simple.

Key Takeaway
The determiner must be simple because compositeness leads to an absurd consequence: predetermined directions before the determiner itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that any idea of parts in the determiner leads to an impossibility. Are there any areas of your belief about Allah where you unknowingly attribute composition or division? How can this logical rigor purify your conception of Him?
?
The argument relies on the necessity of pre-existing directions if motion were to occur. How does this deepen your understanding that everything—even motion and direction—is dependent on the Creator? Does your daily life reflect this dependence?
?
If the determiner were composite, it would need something else to determine its own parts. Do you ever act as though something other than Allah is self-sufficient or independent? What would it mean for you to truly accept that only Allah is simple and self-sufficient?

Refuting the Claim That Simplicity Implies Disintegration
Refutes counter-objection about simplicity implying disintegration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a counter-objection to the proof—that if the determiner were simple it could also disintegrate—is refuted by distinguishing between the possibility for simples and for the composite whole.

Core Idea
Even if a simple body could disintegrate in theory, the composite nature of the whole may prevent any actual motion of its parts.

The opponent challenges the proof by arguing that if the determiner is simple, its ends are equal, so it could also disintegrate (just as a composite would). This lesson presents the response: the possibility of disintegration is conceded for individual simples, but for the entire composite (the sphere), its nature as a whole may prevent such motion. The argument shows that not every possibility for parts holds for the whole.

Guided Path
1
Understand the counter-objection
Read the passage where the opponent argues that a simple body, due to equality of ends, could also cause disintegration. This challenges the need for compositeness.
The counter-objection is clever: it turns the argument on its head.
2
Follow the refutation
Notice that the commentator distinguishes between the possibility for a simple part and for the composite whole. The nature of the whole may be a barrier.
This is a key move in kalam: not every property of parts applies to the whole.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Understand that the objection fails because the composite whole can have a nature that prevents motion, even if its parts individually could move.
This preserves the original conclusion that the determiner is simple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم له أحكام منها أنه بسيط لا مركب من بسائط متعددة ) وإلا جاز انحلاله واللازم باطل) فالملزوم مثله ( أما الملزومية فلان ( المحدد إذا كان مركباً من بسائط متعددة كان كل واحد من أجزائه ملاقياً بأحد جانبيه شيئاً غير ما يلاقيه بجانبه الآخر، ولا شك أن البسيط يمكنه أن يلاقي بأحد طرفيه ما يلاقيه بالآخر لتساويهما) أي تساوي الطرفين في الماهية فإذا لاقى أحدهما شيئاً جاز أن يلاقيه الآخر، وذلك إنما يتصور بالانحلال . ( وأما بطلان اللازم فلأن ذلك ) أي الانحلال ) لا يكون إلا بالحركة المستقيمة وتباعد بعض الأجزاء عن بعض . وقد يقال : جاز أن تكون الملاقاة بالحركة المستديرة، فلا يلزم الانحلال المستلزم للحركة المستقيمة ( وهي ) أعني الحركة المستقيمة ( لا تكون إلا من جهة إلى جهة ) أخرى ( فتكون الجهة متحددة قبله ) أي قبل المحدد حتى يمكن حركة أجزائه إليها ( لا ) متحددة به هذا خلف
قوله : ( وإلا جاز إلخ ) يمكن أن يعارض بأنه لو كان بسيطاً لجاز عليه الانحلال واللازم باطل بيان الملازمة لأنه لو كان بسيطا يساوي محدبه ومقعره في الماهية، ويجوز أن يكون ما يماس محدبه ما يماس مقعره وما ذلك إلا بالانحلال والحمل أن الجسم مطلقة مقتضى كل مكان، وبعد فرض الأجزاء الكل من الجسم والحيز يحصل لكل واحد من أجزاء الجسم والحيز خصوصية، فيجوز أن يقتضي خصوصية كل جزء من الجسم خصوصة كل جزء من الحيز. قوله : ( بأحد جانبيه إلخ ) أي بأحد مما جربه لأن التساوي في المائة للأجزاء لا للأطراف . قوله : ( أن البسيط يمكنه أن يلاقي بأحد طرفيه ما يلاقيه كلمة ما مع صلتها مفعول لقوله : أن يلاقي والضمير المستتر في قوله : يلاقي أو البارز في قوله طرفيه أو المستتر في قوله : يلاقيه راجع إلى البسيط المذكور والضمير المنصوب البارز في قوله : يلاقيه راجع إلى ما، وفي هذا المقام منع وهو أن يقال : الإمكان المذكور وإن كان مسلماً بالنسبة إلى البسائط لكنه ممنوع بالنسبة إلى المجموع المركب من تلك البسائط، فإنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون طبيعة الكل مانعة عن حركة البسائط بوجه ما أصلا. قوله : ( وقد يقال : جاز أن يكون إلخ ) فيه بحث فإنه لا شك أن بعض الأجزاء البسيطة حينئذ كان قريباً من السطح الأعلى لذلك الفلك المركب، وأن البعض الآخر من تلك الأجزاء كان قريباً من السطح الأسفل، لذلك الفلك أيضاً، وظاهر أن ما بين السطحين المذكورين بون بعيد بین هذا البسيط الأقرب من السطح الأسفل وبين ذلك البسيط الأقرب من السطح الأعلى إلا بالحركة المستقيمة كما لا يخفى . وأنه لا يتصور الملاقاة
Then it has rulings, among them that it is simple, not composed of multiple simples; otherwise, its disintegration would be possible, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. As for the implication (that if composite then disintegration is possible), it is because if the determiner were composed of multiple simples, each of its parts would come into contact with something different by one side than by its other side, and without doubt the simple can come into contact with one of its ends with what it contacts by the other, because of their equality—meaning the equality of the two ends in essence; so if it contacts one thing, it is possible that it contacts the other, and that is only conceivable by disintegration. As for the falsity of the consequent, it is because that disintegration does not occur except by rectilinear motion and the separation of some parts from others. And it may be said: it is possible that the contact be by circular motion, so the disintegration which entails rectilinear motion is not necessary. And it (rectilinear motion) does not occur except from one direction to another direction, so the direction is determined before it (the determiner) so that the motion of its parts towards it is possible, not determined by it—this is contradictory.
His statement: (otherwise it would be possible, etc.) can be countered by: if it were simple, disintegration would be possible for it, and the consequent is false. The explanation of the implication: because if it were simple, its convex and concave would be equal in essence, and it is possible that what touches its convex touches its concave, and that is only by disintegration. And the assumption is that the body absolutely requires every place, and after positing the parts of the whole body and the place, a particularity occurs for each part of the body and the place, so it is possible that the particularity of each part of the body demands the particularity of each part of the place. His statement: (by one of its sides, etc.) means by one of its two sides, because equality in the thing (essence) is for the parts, not for the extremities. His statement: (the simple can come into contact by one of its ends with what it contacts by the other) —the word 'what' with its clause is the object of 'to contact', and the hidden pronoun in 'contacts' or the visible pronoun in 'its ends' or the hidden pronoun in 'contacts it' refers back to the mentioned simple, and the visible accusative pronoun in 'contacts it' refers back to 'what'. And in this context there is a denial: it is said that the mentioned possibility, although conceded with respect to simples, is denied with respect to the whole composed of those simples, because why is it not possible that the nature of the whole prevents the motion of the simples in any way at all? His statement: (and it may be said: it is possible that the contact be, etc.)—in this there is an investigation: for there is no doubt that some of the simple parts then would be close to the upper surface of that composite sphere, and the other parts would be close to the lower surface of that sphere, and it is apparent that between the two mentioned surfaces there is a great distance between this simple part closest to the lower surface and that simple part closest to the upper surface except by rectilinear motion, as is not hidden. And contact is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage proves that the determiner of directions (the encompassing sphere) is simple, i.e., not composed of multiple simple parts. The argument proceeds as follows: If it were composite, then each part would contact something different by one side than by the other side. A simple body, by contrast, can contact the same thing by either end because its ends are equal in essence. That equality would allow contact with the same object by either end, but that can only happen through disintegration of the composite. Disintegration requires rectilinear motion, which in turn requires that directions be predetermined before the determiner itself, leading to a contradiction (since the determiner is supposed to define directions). Therefore, the composite is impossible, so the determiner must be simple.
The commentary then addresses several objections and clarifications:
- A counter-objection: if the determiner were simple, it could also disintegrate (by equality of sides), but the reply is that disintegration requires rectilinear motion, which presupposes directions.
- Another objection: contact could be by circular motion, avoiding rectilinear motion. But the reply holds that even circular motion of parts would require some parts to move rectilinearly to achieve contact.
- Further, it is argued that even if simples can contact in the way described, the composite whole might prevent such motion due to its nature. The commentator then refutes this by noting that the simple parts would need to move rectilinearly to reach each other across the distance between the upper and lower surfaces.
The overall conclusion is that the determiner must be simple and not composite.

Key Terms
بسيط
انحلال
طبيعة الكل
مجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a thing is simple, its equality of ends implies it can disintegrate just as a composite can.
Correction: The refutation shows that the possibility for parts does not entail possibility for the whole; the whole may have a nature that prevents disintegration.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the counter-objection fail?
A) Simple bodies cannot possibly disintegrate.
B) The nature of the whole may prevent motion even if parts could.
C) The determiner is not actually simple.
D) Disintegration is always impossible for any body.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of disintegration for individual simples does not imply possibility for the composite whole, which may have a nature that forbids such motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that the nature of the whole can prevent what is possible for parts. Does your understanding of Allah's oneness and simplicity ever feel threatened by intellectual objections? How does this lesson equip you to trust the proofs despite apparent counter-examples?
?
The objector assumed that equality in essence implies interchangeability. In your own worship, have you ever assumed that if something is permissible in general, it must apply to all situations, ignoring the unique nature of the act? Will you now think twice before making such judgments?
?
This lesson shows that what is theoretically possible may be impossible due to the whole's nature. Are there areas of your life where you entertain possibilities that contradict the revealed truth about Allah's attributes? Will you commit to accepting the whole picture of Islamic teachings, even when parts seem difficult?

Logic and Technical Terms

Refuting the Objections of Circular Motion and the Composite Whole
Refutes two objections about contact and the composite whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objections that contact could be via circular motion and that the composite whole might prevent motion are refuted by demonstrating the necessity of rectilinear motion for any separation of parts across the sphere's surfaces.

Core Idea
Even if circular motion is possible, parts on opposite surfaces of the sphere would still need rectilinear motion to come into contact.

After the main proof, two objections arise: (1) contact between parts could be achieved by circular motion, avoiding rectilinear motion; (2) the composite whole might have a nature that prevents any motion of its parts. This lesson presents the refutation: the distance between the upper and lower surfaces of the sphere is vast, and for parts to move from one surface to the other, rectilinear motion is unavoidable. Therefore, both objections fail to save compositeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the passage where two objections are raised: circular motion and the nature of the whole. Understand each challenge.
These are sophisticated attempts to save the composite hypothesis.
2
Analyze the refutation
Notice how the refutation focuses on the physical distance between the surfaces. Even with circular motion, parts would need to move rectilinearly across the gap.
The argument is empirical as well as logical.
3
Conclude
Recognize that the objections are insufficient to disprove the original proof, so the determiner remains simple.
This reinforces the initial conclusion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that circular motion can avoid rectilinear motion entirely, so disintegration does not require predetermined directions.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation reject the circular motion objection?
A) Circular motion is impossible in the sphere.
B) The parts are too far apart to move circularly.
C) Any motion across the distance between surfaces must be rectilinear.
D) The sphere has no surfaces.

Key Takeaway
The physical distance between the surfaces of the sphere necessitates rectilinear motion, so the circular motion objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
